Simulated Contracts: The Three Binding Exceptions
States the rule and the hadith proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three contracts that are binding despite simulated intent and apply the hadith as proof in a fatwa context.

Core Idea
Three contracts are binding by form, not intention: marriage, divorce, emancipation.

In Hanafi jurisprudence, contracts are generally evaluated based on the real intentions of the parties. However, there is a well-known exception for three contracts that are given full legal effect regardless of the inner intent: marriage (nikah), divorce (talaq), and emancipation (itaq). This rule is derived from a Prophetic hadith and is a cornerstone for muftis when dealing with cases where parties claim they were joking. Understanding this rasm rule prevents incorrect fatwas based solely on subjective intent.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the general rule
Start by stating the general principle: contracts are judged by the real intention of the parties, not just the outward form. This is the default in Hanafi law.
2
Identify the exception
Recognize that the hadith creates an exception for three specific contracts: nikah, talaq, and itaq. These are binding even if done in jest or with a simulated intent.
3
Cite the hadith
Memorize the hadith text: 'ثلاث جدهن جد، وهزلهن جد: النكاح والطلاق والعتاق.' This is the primary evidence for the rule.
The hadith is narrated by Abu Dawud, Tirmidhi, and others, and is considered authentic (hasan).
4
Apply the rule
In a fatwa scenario, if someone claims they intended to joke, the mufti must rule that the contract is valid and its consequences follow. For example, a divorce pronounced in jest is effective.
5
Distinguish from other contracts
Understand that contracts like sale, lease, or gift are not covered by this exception. Their validity depends on actual intent, and simulated contracts are generally invalid.
This prevents overgeneralization of the exception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل (۳۳) صورية العقود
نتائجه كاملة دون نظر إلى الإرادة الحقيقية وهذه المستثنيات الثلاثة النكاح والطلاق والعتاق، لقول الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم: ثلاث جدهنَّ جِد، وهَزْلُهنَّ جِد النكاح والطلاق والعتاق. فعقد النكاح الجاري بالهزل أو بالمواضعة على صوريته يصح وتنتج نتائجه بين المتعاقدين فيحل المتعة بينهما ويلزمهما بوجوبه.
Chapter 33: Simulated Contracts. Its consequences are complete without regard to the real intention. And these three exceptions are: marriage, divorce, and emancipation, because of the statement of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him): 'Three things, their seriousness is serious, and their jest is serious: marriage, divorce, and emancipation.' So a marriage contract conducted in jest or with an agreement on its simulation is valid and produces its consequences between the contracting parties, so it permits intimacy between them and obligates them to its obligations.
Source pages p. 421

Commentary
This passage introduces the category of simulated contracts (صورية العقود) in Hanafi jurisprudence. The general rule is that contracts are evaluated based on the real intentions of the parties, not the outward form. However, there are three exceptions where the formal act is given full legal effect regardless of the inner intent: marriage (nikah), divorce (talaq), and emancipation (itaq). The proof is the hadith of the Prophet (SAW) that these three are serious even if done in jest. Thus, if someone marries or divorces jokingly, the contract is valid and all its legal consequences apply. This is a rasm rule: the mufti must be aware that despite the general principle of intention, these three contracts are binding based on their form.

Key Terms
صورية العقود (suriyyat al-ʿuqūd): Contracts that are formed outwardly but the parties do not intend their actual legal consequences.
النكاح (al-nikāḥ): Marriage contract.
الطلاق (al-ṭalāq): Divorce; repudiation by the husband.
العتاق (al-ʿitāq): Manumission of a slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi rule on simulated contracts, which of the following contracts is NOT binding if done in jest?
A) Marriage
B) Divorce
C) Sale
D) Emancipation

Key Takeaway
Marriage, divorce, and emancipation are binding by their form; a mufti must know this exception to avoid incorrect fatwas.

Coercion in Verbal Dispositions: The Hanafi Exception and School Disputes
Comparative exposition of Hanafi, Maliki, Shafi'i, and majority views on coercion in marriage, divorce, and manumission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi exception for coercion in marriage, divorce, and manumission, and compare it with the Maliki and Shafi'i positions as presented in the passage.

Core Idea
Hanafis alone hold that coercion does not invalidate marriage, divorce, or manumission, analogizing to jest.

The effect of coercion on verbal contracts is a contested issue across madhahib. This lesson examines the unique Hanafi position that three specific acts—marriage, divorce, and manumission—remain valid under coercion, by analogy to jest. We will also explore the Maliki view of non-binding validity and the Shafi'i position of nullity, ending with the majority's critique that distinguishes jest from coercion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi exception
Note that Hanafis exempt marriage, divorce, and manumission from the invalidating effect of coercion. They consider these acts effective even when coerced, analogizing to the rule for jest (hazl).
The analogy is based on the idea that just as jest in these acts makes them binding, so does coercion.
2
Analyze the financial consequence for coerced divorce
The passage notes that if coercion leads to divorce, the husband can recover the mahr from the coercer, and if the wife is the coercer, her mahr is forfeited. This shows that Hanafis do consider financial liability.
3
Examine the Maliki and Shafi'i positions
Malikis treat coerced contracts as valid but non-binding, with the coerced party having an option to annul. Shafi'is consider them void ab initio because consent is essential.
The Maliki view introduces the concept of 'pressure' (daghṭ) where coercion is indirect.
4
Understand the majority critique
The majority (jumhur) reject the Hanafi analogy because the jester acts voluntarily while the coerced does not. They rely on Sharia texts absolving the coerced.
The author (Zarqa) favors the majority view as more consistent with legislative wisdom.
5
Apply as a mufti: weighing positions
When issuing a fatwa, a Hanafi mufti would follow his own school (coerced marriage/divorce valid) but must note the financial liability and the existence of contrary views for cross-madhhab cases.
The mufta bihi in Hanafi school is the exception, but the majority view may be considered for public policy.

Source Text

الفصل (٣٤) عيوب الرضا - الإكراه
رأي الجمهور في الموضوع:
غير
أنَّ جمهور المجتهدين خالفوا الحنفية ولم يقبلوا هذا القياس، ففرّقوا بين الإكراه والهزل في هذه التصرفات الثلاثة التي لا يؤثر فيها الهزل، وقرروا أنَّ الإكراه يؤثر فيها ويمنع صحتها أو لزومها (على الاختلاف بين الاجتهادات الإسلامية في أثر الإكراه كما في سائر التصرفات. وذلك بخلاف الهزل، لأن الهازل مختار في مباشرة السبب لكنه لا يريد ثبوت حكمه، فيمكن إثبات الحكم رغماً عنه إذا اقتضت المصلحة التشريعية ذلك .
أما المتصرف بالإكراه فغير مختار فلا يسوغ إلزامه، ولا سيما أن نصوص الشريعة صريحة في عدم مؤاخذة الإنسان بما استكره عليه .
ورأي الجمهور هذا في نظرنا أوجه وأجرى مع حكمة التشريع
Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Fiqh. 34/5 - The Hanafis alone except from verbal dispositions those in which jest has no effect - i.e., those where simulation does not apply as previously explained in simulation of contracts, namely marriage, divorce, and manumission; coercion has no effect on their validity; they take effect under coercion as under consent according to them. This is their analogy of the state of coercion to the exceptional state of jest in these contracts and dispositions. However, in coercion to divorce which entails financial liability, the husband recovers the dower from the coercer. If the coercer on the husband to divorce is the wife herself, her dower is forfeited. d - Close to this is the Maliki school; the Maliki ijtihad holds that a contract under coercion is valid but not binding; the coerced person has the option to annul it, and they treat direct coercion and pressure equally. Pressure according to them is when coercion does not fall directly on the contract but on another cause that compels the pressured person to contract, like one whom the ruler unjustly forces to pay money so he is compelled to sell his property to pay it. This is called by them: sale of the pressured person... They disagreed on requiring the buyer's knowledge of the seller's pressure for him to have the right to annul the contract. - As for the Shafi'is, they adopted the theory of nullity and ruled: contracts and all verbal dispositions are void ab initio under coercion; because consent according to them is a condition of formation; choice and consent in their view are inseparable concepts; one cannot exist without the other, and coercion negates both; one who acts under coercion does not intend to conclude the contract but only to save himself. The words expressing will lose their signification in the state of coercion, with no difference between marriage, divorce, and others, contrary to the Hanafi view which we have elaborated on the possibility of separation of choice from consent. Chapter (34) Defects of Consent - Coercion. The Majority View on the Subject: However, the majority of mujtahids opposed the Hanafis and did not accept this analogy; they distinguished between coercion and jest in these three dispositions in which jest has no effect, and ruled that coercion does affect them and prevents their validity or binding effect (according to the difference among Islamic ijtihads on the effect of coercion as in other dispositions). This is unlike jest, because the jester is free in undertaking the cause but does not intend its legal consequence, so the consequence can be imposed against his will if legislative interest requires it. As for the one acting under coercion, he is not free, so it is not proper to bind him, especially since the texts of Sharia are explicit that a person is not held accountable for what he is coerced to do. This majority view in our opinion is more correct and more in line with the wisdom of legislation.
Source pages p. 429

Commentary
This passage presents the comparative fiqh discussion on the effect of coercion (ikrah) on verbal dispositions (al-tasarrufat al-qawliyyah). The Hanafi position is unique: they exempt marriage (nikah), divorce (talaq), and manumission (itq) from the general rule that coercion invalidates contracts, analogizing these to jest (hazl) where the acts are effective despite lack of serious intent. The author notes that for coerced divorce with financial consequences, the husband recovers dower from the coercer. He then presents the Maliki view (contract valid but non-binding, with option to rescind), the Shafi'i view (void ab initio, as consent is essential), and finally the majority (jumhur) view that opposes the Hanafi analogy, distinguishing jest (voluntary) from coercion (involuntary) and relying on scriptural texts that exempt the coerced from accountability. The author signals preference for the majority view.

Key Terms
إكراه (ikrāh): Compelling a person to perform an act against his will, negating consent but not necessarily choice in Hanafi view
هزل (hazl): Jest; speaking words without intending their legal consequences
عتاق (itāq): Manumission of a slave; one of the three exceptional verbal acts where jest and coercion do not invalidate according to Hanafis

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which madhhab holds that a coerced marriage is void ab initio?
A) Hanafi
B) Maliki
C) Shafi'i
D) Majority (Jumhur)

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi exception for coercion in marriage, divorce, and manumission is based on analogy to jest, but the majority rejects this analogy and considers coercion as negating choice.

Coercion in Actual Acts: Hanafi Rule on Liability
Footnote explaining Hanafi ruling on coercion in actual acts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule on coercion (ikrah) to determine liability of the doer vs. coercer, distinguishing between overwhelming (mulji') and non-overwhelming (ghayr mulji') coercion and between acts permissible at necessity and absolutely prohibited acts.

Core Idea
Overwhelming coercion transfers liability for permissible-at-necessity acts, but never permits killing.

This passage from Al-Zarqa's introduction clarifies the Hanafi position on coercion in actual acts involving aggression against others. The school differentiates between overwhelming (mulji') and non-overwhelming (ghayr mulji') coercion, and between acts that are temporarily permissible under duress and those that are never permissible. Understanding this nuance is essential for a mufti when evaluating responsibility in cases of duress.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act type
Determine whether the act is actual (fi'li) or verbal (qawli). This lesson applies to actual acts involving aggression against others (e.g., damaging property, killing).
Verbal dispositions have different rules.
2
Classify the act's permissibility under necessity
Determine if the act is among those that are permissible at necessity (e.g., usurping or destroying property when forced) or absolutely prohibited (e.g., killing an innocent person).
The Hanafi school only permits necessity-based acts under overwhelming coercion.
3
Classify the type of coercion
Distinguish between overwhelming coercion (ikrah mulji') that leaves no practical alternative, and non-overwhelming coercion (ghayr mulji') that still allows choice.
Overwhelming coercion vitiates choice; non-overwhelming does not.
4
Apply the rule for permissible-at-necessity acts
If the act is permissible at necessity and coercion is overwhelming, the act becomes permissible and liability transfers to the coercer. If coercion is non-overwhelming, the doer remains liable because his choice was intact.
Consent (rida) is not required; correct choice (ikhtiyar sahih) suffices for liability.
5
Apply the rule for absolutely prohibited acts
If the act is absolutely prohibited like killing, coercion never permits it. However, if the act is carried out, the penalty (qisas) falls on the coercer, not the doer.
This is a unique Hanafi position balancing the gravity of the act and the coercion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا حكم الإكراه على التصرفات القولية في الاجتهادات. أما التصرفات الفعلية التي فيها عدوان على الغير فرأي الحنفية فيها إنها إن كانت مما : يباح عند الضرورة كغصب مال الغير ،وإتلافه فإنها يؤثر فيها الإكراه القوي، أي الملجيء، فيبيحها ويكون المكره الحامل هو المسؤول دون الفاعل ولا يؤثر فيها غير الملجيء إذ يكفي للمسؤولية فيها الاختيار الصحيح ولا يشترط الرضا. فلو فعلها إنسان بإكراه غير ملجيء يكون هو المسؤول بالمال دون الحامل المكره، لنقصان الإكراه غير الملجيء بالنسبة إلى خطورة الفعل المكره عليه لأنه ليس مفسداً لاختيار الفاعل . وإن كانت مما لا يباح عند الضرورة، كقتل الغير، فإنه لا يبيحه الإكراه مطلقاً ولو أكره عليه بالقتل. لكن إذا أطاع الإكراه فقتل من يراد قتله فإن العقوبة الخاصة وهي القصاص تكون على الحامل المكره لا على الفاعل.
This is the ruling on coercion in verbal dispositions in the ijtihads. As for actual acts that involve aggression against others, the Hanafi view is that if they are from what is permissible at necessity, like usurping another's property or destroying it, then strong coercion, i.e., overwhelming (mulji'), affects them, making them permissible, and the coercer (the one who compels) is liable, not the doer; non-overwhelming coercion does not affect them because correct choice is sufficient for liability and consent is not required. If a person does an act under non-overwhelming coercion, he is financially liable, not the coercer, because non-overwhelming coercion is deficient relative to the gravity of the coerced act, as it does not vitiate the doer's choice. And if the acts are of the type not permissible even at necessity, like killing another, then coercion does not permit them at all, even if the coercer threatens death. But if he obeys the coercion and kills the intended person, the specific penalty, which is retaliation (qisas), is on the coercer, not the doer.
Source pages p. 429

Commentary
This passage, from a footnote, complements the main discussion by addressing actual acts (al-tasarrufat al-fi'liyyah) involving aggression. The Hanafi school distinguishes between overwhelming coercion (ikrah mulji') and non-overwhelming coercion (ghayr mulji'). For acts that are ordinarily prohibited but permissible under necessity (e.g., taking or destroying property in duress), overwhelming coercion makes the act permissible and transfers liability to the coercer; non-overwhelming coercion leaves liability on the doer because his choice remains intact. For acts never permissible even under necessity, like killing an innocent person, coercion does not justify the act; but if the act is carried out, the punishment of qisas falls on the coercer, not the coerced agent. This shows the nuanced Hanafi approach balancing coercion's effect with the gravity of the act.

Key Terms
إكراه ملجيء (ikrāh mulji'): Overwhelming coercion that vitiates choice and leaves no practical alternative; exempts the doer from liability for permissible-at-necessity acts
إكراه غير ملجيء (ikrāh ghayr mulji'): Non-overwhelming coercion that does not vitiate choice; the doer remains liable
تصرفات فعلية (tasarrufāt fi'liyyah): Actual acts or physical conduct, as opposed to verbal declarations

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view in the footnote, when is a person who commits an act under coercion financially liable?
A) When the act is permissible at necessity and coercion is overwhelming
B) When the coercion is non-overwhelming
C) When the act is killing someone
D) When the coercer is the ruler

Key Takeaway
Overwhelming coercion exempts the doer only for permissible-at-necessity acts; killing is never permitted, but its penalty shifts to the coercer.

Khiyānah in Trust Sales: Deception and Its Remedy
Definition and legal remedy for treachery in trust sales.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define khiyānah in the context of trust sales and state the Hanafi legal remedy, distinguishing between rescission and price reduction.

Core Idea
In a trust sale, a seller who lies about capital commits khiyānah, giving the buyer the option to rescind or reduce the price.

The first form of khlābah (deception) detailed in this passage is khiyānah, which occurs specifically in trust sales where the seller explicitly states the capital cost. If the seller misrepresents the capital to inflate profit, it is a betrayal that vitiates the buyer's consent. The Hanafi school provides the buyer with a legal remedy: either to rescind the contract entirely or to claim a proportional reduction in price. This lesson parses the definition, the remedy, and the underlying rationale.

Guided Path
1
Define khiyānah
Read the passage and identify what constitutes khiyānah in trust sales. Look for the key elements: seller lies about capital (ra's al-mal) to extract more profit.
Trust sales include murābaḥah, wadīʿah, etc.
2
Identify the legal remedy
There are two opinions within the Hanafi school: some give the buyer the right to rescind the contract (ibṭāl al-ʿaqd), others give the right to reduce the price (ḥaṭṭ al-thaman) proportionally to the fraud.
The later Hanafi position, as per Radd al-Muḥtār, is the reduction remedy.
3
Apply to a scenario
Suppose a seller says the capital is $100 but it was actually $80, and they agreed on a 10% profit. Determine the buyer's remedy: either rescind or pay $88 ($80 + $8 profit) instead of $110.
The ratio of fraud is 20% of capital; the profit reduction is also 20%.
4
Understand the rationale
Why does the law provide this remedy? Because the buyer's consent was based on a false premise. The remedy restores fairness.

Source Text

الفصل (٣٥) عيوب الرضا - الخلابة
على التعاقد تدخل في الخلابة. لكن المهم أن تكون هي التي حملته على التعاقد .
وتتجلى الخلابة سافرة في عدة صور ووسائل شاعت بين الناس وبعضها تناولته السنة النبوية بنهي خاص. فخصها فقهاء المذاهب بالذكر، فمن هذه الصور البارزة في الخلابة أربع صور مشهورة هي: الخيانة، والتناجش ،والتغرير وتدليس العيب.
/٢/٥ - (أولاً) : الخيانة :
فالخيانة يصورها الفقهاء في بيوع الأمانة (۱)، كما لو باع شيئاً بربح معين على رأس ماله المبين وكذب في بيان رأس المال فقال: إنه عشرة مثلاً، وهو في الواقع أقل. فهذا من البائع خيانة قد خلب بها المشتري ليستخلص منه ربحاً أكثر مما اتفقا عليه. فلذا يمنح المشتري في رأي بعض الفقهاء حق إبطال العقد، وفي رأي آخر يحط من الثمن مقدار الخيانة مع مقدار نسبتها من الربح فإذا كان مقدار الخيانة ربع رأس المال يحط معها من الثمن ربع الربح المشروط أيضاً :(ر: رد المحتار ١٥٥/٤).
/٣/٣٥ - (ثانياً) : التناجش :
وأما التناجش، أو النجش(٢)، فهو أن يتواطأ صاحب سلعة مع شخص ليتظاهر هذا بالرغبة في شرائها ويدفع فيها أكثر من قيمتها وهو لا يريد شراءها، بل ليوهم غيره مزاحمة تغريه بالشراء. وقد نهى النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم عن التناجش، لأنه من قبيل الخلابة.
والتناجش في نظر فقهاء الشريعة يعيب الرضا فيعطى المشتري بسببه حق إبطال العقد بشرط أن يؤدي إلى غبن المشتري في الثمن غَبْناً فاحشاً (۱). هذا ما عليه جمهور الفقهاء والمذاهب الثلاثة (المالكي والشافعي، والحنبلي. أما في المذهب الحنفي فالتناجش لا يعيب الرضا فلا يسوغ إبطال العقد، ترجيحاً لاستقرار التعامل، لكنه مكروه ديانة (٢).
(۱) الغين (بفتح فسكون هو أن تطغى مصلحة أحد المتعاقدين على مصلحة الآخر لا يكون توازن بين ما يأخذ وما يعطي. وأصله في اللغة من قولهم: غبن الثوب، إذا ثناه فخاطه لأجل تضييقه أو تقصيره. ويمكن أن يقع الغبن من الطرفين في عقود المعاوضة كالبيع والإجارة والصلح والقسمة، كما لو باع أحد شيئاً بأكثر من قيمة المثل أو بأقل، فإن البائع غابن في الأكثر ومغبون في الأقل.
والغبن بالنظر إلى مقداره يقسمه الفقهاء إلى قسمين: غبن يسير، وغبن فاحش. فالغبن اليسير هو ما لا يتجاوز حدود التفاوت المعتاد بين الناس في الأسعار، كما لو بيع شيء بعشرة دنانير مثلاً، ولو عرض على أهل الخبرة يقدره بعضهم بعشرة وبعضهم بتسعة. فالواحد المختلف فيه يعتبر غبناً يسيراً لأنه - كما يقول الفقهاء يدخل تحت تقويم المقومين، أي يتناوله تقدير بعضهم للقيمة . وأما الغين الفاحش فهو ما يتجاوز حدود التفاوت المعتاد في الأسعار، كما لو كان الشيء المبيع بعشرة يقدره بعض الخبراء بثمانية وبعضهم بتسعة أو سبعة بحيث لا تدخل العشرة في تقدير أحد من الخبراء. فالفرق بين العشرة وبين أعلى تقدير من خبير أمين هو غبن فاحش. على أن متأخري فقهاء المذهب الحنفي لجؤوا، ضبطاً للمقاييس القضائية، إلى تحديد الغبن الفاحش بما يعادل نصف عشر القيمة في العروض المنقولة، والعشر في الحيوان والخمس في العقارات فما بلغ من الغبن هذه الحدود فهو غبن فاحش وما دونه يسير. وبهذا أخذت المجلة في المادة / ١٦٥/ منها . ويلحظ أن الغبن اليسير لا تأثير له في نظر الفقهاء غالباً، لأنه لا يمكن التحرز منه فمتى ذكر الغبن في المواطن التي له فيها تأثير يراد به الغبن الفاحش.
(۲) انظر معنى حكم الديانة وحكم القضاء في أول الكتاب (ف ٣/٢ - ٤) .
Chapter (35): Defects of Consent - Deception (Khlābah)
... enters into the contract is part of deception. But the important thing is that it was what induced him to contract.
Deception appears clearly in several forms and means that have become common among people, some of which the Prophetic Sunnah has specifically prohibited. The jurists of the schools have specifically mentioned them. Among these prominent forms of deception are four well-known forms: treachery, bidding up, misrepresentation, and concealment of a defect.
/2/5 - (First): Treachery:
Treachery is depicted by the jurists in sales of trust (1), such as when someone sells an item at a specified profit on his stated capital and lies in his statement of capital, saying it is ten, for example, when in reality it is less. This from the seller is treachery with which he has deceived the buyer to extract more profit than they agreed upon. Therefore, the buyer is granted, in the view of some jurists, the right to rescind the contract. In another view, the price is reduced by the amount of treachery along with its proportion of the profit. So if the amount of treachery is a quarter of the capital, the price is reduced by a quarter of the stipulated profit as well. (See: Radd al-Muhtār 4/155).
/3/35 - (Second): Bidding up (Najašh):
As for bidding up (al-najash or al-Nijash) (2), it is that the owner of goods conspires with a person to pretend desire to purchase and offer more than its value while he does not intend to buy it, but to give the false impression of competition to entice another to buy. The Prophet (peace be upon him) prohibited bidding up, because it is a form of deception.
Bidding up, in the view of the jurists of the Shariah, vitiates consent and thus gives the buyer the right to rescind the contract, on condition that it results in a gross disadvantage (ghaban fahish) in the price (1). This is the view of the majority of jurists and the three schools (Maliki, Shafi'i, and Hanbali). As for the Hanafi school, bidding up does not vitiate consent and therefore does not justify rescinding the contract, prioritizing the stability of transactions, but it is disliked as a matter of religion (2).
(1) Ghaban (with a fatha on ghain and sukun on ba) is that the interest of one of the contracting parties prevails over the other's interest so that there is no balance between what he takes and what he gives. Its origin in language is from their saying: ghabana al-thawb, if he folds it and sews it to narrow or shorten it. Ghaban can occur from both parties in commutative contracts like sale, lease, settlement, and partition, as when someone sells something for more than the fair value or for less; the seller is a defrauder in the excess and defrauded in the shortfall.
Ghaban, with respect to its amount, is divided by the jurists into two types: minor ghaban and gross (fahish) ghaban. Minor ghaban is that which does not exceed the usual range of variation in prices among people, as if something is sold for ten dinars, for example, and when presented to experts, some value it at ten and some at nine. The one difference is considered minor ghaban because it falls within the assessment of appraisers, i.e., is covered by some of their estimates of value. As for gross ghaban, it is that which exceeds the usual range of variation in prices, as if the sold item is sold for ten and some experts estimate it at eight, and some at nine or seven, such that ten is not included in any expert's estimate. The difference between ten and the highest estimate of a trustworthy expert is gross ghaban. However, later Hanafi jurists, to regulate judicial standards, defined gross ghaban as equivalent to half a tenth (1/20) of the value for movable goods, one tenth (1/10) for animals, and one fifth (1/5) for real estate. Whatever ghaban reaches these thresholds is gross, and below it is minor. The Majalla adopted this in its Article 165. It is noted that minor ghaban generally has no effect in the view of the jurists because it cannot be avoided. So whenever ghaban is mentioned in contexts where it has effect, gross ghaban is intended.
(2) See the meaning of the ruling of religious obligation (diyānah) and the ruling of litigation (qaḍā') at the beginning of the book (Sections 3/2-4).
Source pages p. 433

Commentary
This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhī al-ʿĀmm by Muṣṭafá Aḥmad al-Zarqā discusses the defect of consent known as khlābah (deception) in contracts. The author defines khlābah as a misrepresentation that induces a party to contract. He highlights four types, but here he covers two: khiyānah (treachery) and al-najash (bidding up).

First, khiyānah occurs in sales of trust (buyūʿ al-amānah) where the seller lies about the capital cost to inflate profit. The remedy for the buyer is either rescission or reduction of price proportional to the fraud, as adopted in the Hanafi school (see Radd al-Muḥtār).

Second, al-najash is the collusion between the seller and a fake bidder to artificially raise the price. The Prophet ﷺ explicitly prohibited it. The majority of schools (Mālikī, Shāfiʿī, Ḥanbalī) give the deceived buyer the right to rescind if there is gross disadvantage (ghaban fāḥish). However, the Ḥanafī school holds that najash does not vitiate consent and thus does not give a right to rescind, prioritizing market stability over individual fairness. Nevertheless, it remains makrūh dīyānatan (religiously disliked).

The passage also includes an important footnote on ghaban (disadvantage), defining its types and the Hanafi judicial thresholds (1/20 for movables, 1/10 for animals, 1/5 for real estate) as adopted in the Majalla.

Key Terms
الخلابة (al-khlābah): الخداع الذي يحمل الشخص على التعاقد، ويشمل صوراً كالخيانة والتناجش والتغرير وتدليس العيب
الخيانة (al-khiyānah): الكذب في بيان رأس المال في بيوع الأمانة لاستخلاص ربح أكثر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the remedy for khiyānah in trust sales according to the passage?
A) The contract is automatically void (bāṭil).
B) The buyer can only claim damages but cannot cancel.
C) The buyer may either rescind the contract or demand a proportional price reduction.
D) The buyer must accept the contract but can sue for fraud.

Key Takeaway
In khiyānah, the deceived buyer may either rescind the contract or demand a price reduction proportional to the fraud.

Al-Najash (Bidding Up): The Hanafi Position vs. Majority
Definition, prohibition, and inter-madhhab dispute on bidding up.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on najash (bidding up) and compare it with the majority view, identifying the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
Najash is prohibited but does not vitiate consent in the Hanafi school; the buyer gets no right to rescind, unlike the majority who grant rescission for gross disadvantage.

The second form of khlābah is al-najash, where the seller and a conspirator artificially inflate the price. The Prophet ﷺ explicitly forbade it. However, the Hanafi school, prioritizing the stability of transactions, holds that najash does not give the buyer a right to rescind the contract, though it is religiously disliked (makrūh dīyānatan). The three other schools grant rescission if the buyer suffers gross disadvantage (ghaban fāḥish). This lesson dissects the disagreement and its practical impact.

Guided Path
1
Define najash
From the passage, najash is collusion between the seller and a fake bidder to drive up the price. The fake bidder does not intend to buy.
Also known as al-nijash.
2
Identify the prophetic prohibition
The Prophet ﷺ prohibited it as it deceives the real buyer. The hadith is a textual evidence (dalīl naqlī).
Hadith: 'Do not engage in najash'.
3
State the majority position
The Mālikī, Shāfiʿī, and Ḥanbalī schools consider najash as vitiating consent, giving the buyer the right to rescind, but only if there is gross disadvantage (ghaban fāḥish).
Gross disadvantage is defined later in the footnote.
4
State the Hanafi position and rationale
The Hanafī school holds that najash does not vitiate consent, so the contract is valid and cannot be rescinded. The rationale is to protect the stability of transactions (istiqrār al-muʿāmalāt). However, it is makrūh dīyānatan.
Dīyānatan means religiously disliked but not invalid in court.
5
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: under the majority, the buyer can rescind if gross disadvantage; under Hanafi, no such right. Thus, the Hanafi approach is more lenient in upholding contracts.

Source Text

الفصل (٣٥) عيوب الرضا - الخلابة
على التعاقد تدخل في الخلابة. لكن المهم أن تكون هي التي حملته على التعاقد .
وتتجلى الخلابة سافرة في عدة صور ووسائل شاعت بين الناس وبعضها تناولته السنة النبوية بنهي خاص. فخصها فقهاء المذاهب بالذكر، فمن هذه الصور البارزة في الخلابة أربع صور مشهورة هي: الخيانة، والتناجش ،والتغرير وتدليس العيب.
/٢/٥ - (أولاً) : الخيانة :
فالخيانة يصورها الفقهاء في بيوع الأمانة (۱)، كما لو باع شيئاً بربح معين على رأس ماله المبين وكذب في بيان رأس المال فقال: إنه عشرة مثلاً، وهو في الواقع أقل. فهذا من البائع خيانة قد خلب بها المشتري ليستخلص منه ربحاً أكثر مما اتفقا عليه. فلذا يمنح المشتري في رأي بعض الفقهاء حق إبطال العقد، وفي رأي آخر يحط من الثمن مقدار الخيانة مع مقدار نسبتها من الربح فإذا كان مقدار الخيانة ربع رأس المال يحط معها من الثمن ربع الربح المشروط أيضاً :(ر: رد المحتار ١٥٥/٤).

The Clear Error Maxim in Hanafi Contract Law
Definition and scope of a legal maxim on error

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi maxim on clear error and apply it to distinguish obstructive error (ghalat mani') from error giving khiyar.

Core Idea
Error invalidates consent only if it is clear and not surprising to the other party.

This passage introduces a key legal maxim regarding error (ghalat) as a defect of consent. While modern systems often treat hidden error as grounds for rescission, the Hanafi school prioritises transaction security: only a clear, manifest error—one that the other party could have been aware of—allows annulment. The discussion then applies this to error in genus, which prevents the contract from forming altogether.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal maxim
Locate the central rule: error is not a defect in consent unless it is clear (wadih). This is the qa'ida governing the entire discussion.
The maxim is derived from the jurists' scattered rulings.
2
Understand the rationale
If error is hidden, the true intention is concealed, so the manifest intention prevails to protect transaction security. If error is clear, the true intention is evident, allowing annulment without unfair surprise.
This balances the erring party's right with the other party's reliance.
3
Determine when error is clear
Error is clear either by explicit disclosure (e.g., specifying the genus or describing a quality) or by circumstantial evidence (qara'in).
Two cases are given: explicit disclosure and evident circumstances.
4
Apply the first sub-case: error in genus
If the error is in the genus (jins) of the subject matter (e.g., buying a 'ruby' that is glass), the contract does not conclude at all because the intended subject matter is non-existent. This is called obstructive error (ghalat mani').
This differs from error in attribute, which may give the option of inspection (khiyar al-ru'ya) but does not prevent formation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل (٣٦) عيوب الرضا - الغلط
العقد (۱) ما دامت الإرادة الباطنة الحقيقية خفية كما تقدم ف ٤/٣٢). وهكذا كانت مسائل الغلط في الفقه الإسلامي غير مجموعة في بحث خاص، بل متفرقة موزعة في موضوعات ومباحث شتى ذات صلة به، كظهور العيب في المبيع وكفوات الوصف أو اختلاف الجنس في المعقود عليه، وكخيار الرؤية، كما سنرى .
ولذا كان على من يريد صياغة نظرية للغلط التعاقدي في الفقه الإسلامي على غرار نظرية الغلط في الفقه الأجنبي أن يلتقط مسائلها وقواعدها وأحكامها من تلك الفصول والمباحث ذات الصلة بها. وهذا ما نحاوله هنا بقدر الإمكان، وبقدر ما يتّسع له هذا المدخل الفقهي. ٢/٣٦ - متى يعتبر غلط العاقد عيباً في رضاه يجيز له إبطال العقد؟ بعد هذا التمهيد نقول: إنَّ من يتتبع مسائل الغلط المتفرقة في فصول ومناسبات عديدة من فقه المذاهب الأربعة، يخلص إلى نتيجة هي أن فقهاء المذاهب لم يعتبروا الغلط العفوي من أحد العاقدين عيباً في رضاه يجيز له إبطال عقده إلا في الحالات التي يكون فيها الغلط واضحاً ليس فيه مفاجأة للعاقد الآخر.
ذلك لأن الغلط إذا لم يكن واضحاً فإنّ الإرادة الحقيقية للعاقد الغالط تكون في حيّز الخفاء، فمن الواجب عندئذ اعتماد إرادته الظاهرة. فإعطاؤه حق إبطال العقد عند ثبوت غلطه - وإن كان فيه استنقاذ له ورعاية لحقه ومصلحته - هو في الوقت نفسه جناية على حق العاقد الآخر ومصلحته دون أن يكون منه تسبب أو تقصير، وهذا ما يزعزع استقرار التعامل.
أما إذا كان الغلط واضحاً فإنَّ الإرادة الحقيقية عندئذ تكون هي أيضاً واضحة. فإعطاء الغالط في هذه الحال حق الإبطال هو الذي يفرضه مبدأ احترام الإرادة العقدية، ولا يخل باستقرار التعامل، لأن العاقد الآخر يكون غلط (۱) على بينة من رفيقه، فلا يكون في الإبطال مفاجأة له.
٣/٣٦ - كيف يكون غلط العاقد واضحاً:
والظاهر من تتبع نصوص الفقهاء أنَّ غلط العاقد في عقده يعتبر واضحاً إذا كشف العاقد بنفسه عن مراده كشفاً صريحاً خلال التعاقد أو كان مراده مكشوفاً ظاهراً من القرائن والدلائل. فهاتان حالتان :
٤/٣٦ - (الحالة الأولى) : كشف العاقد عن مراده بنفسه كشفاً صريحاً :
فأما حالة كشف العاقد الغالط عن مراده بنفسه صراحة فكما إذا عين في العقد جنس المعقود عليه، أو إذا وصفه بصفة، ثم ظهر على خلاف ذلك. وفي هذه الحال للفقهاء حكم تفصيلي حول درجة تأثير الغلط في العقد :
( أ ) فإذا كان الغلط واقعاً في جنس المعقود عليه، كما لو باع أو اشترى فص خاتم على أنه ياقوت فإذا هو زجاج، أو اشترى كيساً من الأرز فإذا هو قمح، فإنَّ العقد لا ينعقد أصلاً في الاجتهاد الحنفي، لأن اختلاف الجنس يجعل المحل المعقود عليه معدوماً، وهو أحد مقومات العقد المتقدمة الذكر ف (۱/۲۹) (۲) .
Chapter (36): Defects of Consent - Error.
(1) As long as the true hidden intention is concealed, as previously in 4/32. Thus, issues of error in Islamic jurisprudence have not been collected in a specific study, but are scattered across various related topics and discussions, such as the appearance of a defect in the sold item, loss of a description, difference in genus of the subject matter, and the option of inspection (khiyar al-ru'ya), as we shall see.
Therefore, whoever wishes to formulate a theory of contractual error in Islamic jurisprudence similar to the theory of error in foreign jurisprudence must extract its issues, rules, and rulings from those related chapters and discussions. This is what we attempt here as much as possible, and as much as this jurisprudential introduction allows. 2/36 - When is a contracting party's error considered a defect in his consent allowing him to annul the contract? After this preamble, we say: Whoever traces the scattered issues of error in various chapters and occasions of the jurisprudence of the four schools concludes that the jurists of the schools did not consider a spontaneous error by one of the contracting parties as a defect in his consent allowing him to annul his contract except in cases where the error is clear, without surprise to the other party.
This is because if the error is not clear, the true intention of the erring party remains hidden; then it is obligatory to rely on his manifest intention. Giving him the right to annul the contract upon proof of his error—although it rescues him and protects his right and interest—is at the same time an injury to the right and interest of the other party without any fault or negligence on his part, and this destabilizes transactions.
But if the error is clear, then the true intention is also clear. Granting the erring party the right to annul in this case is what the principle of respecting contractual intention requires, and it does not disrupt the stability of dealings because the other party is aware of his companion's error (1), so annulment is not a surprise to him.
3/36 - How is the contracting party's error clear?
From tracing the texts of the jurists, it appears that the error of a contracting party in his contract is considered clear if he himself explicitly reveals his intent during the contract, or if his intent is evidently manifest from indications and evidence. These are two cases:
4/36 - (First case): The contracting party's explicit disclosure of his intent himself.
As for the case of the erring party explicitly disclosing his intent himself, it is like when he specifies the genus of the subject matter in the contract, or describes it with a description, and then the contrary appears. In this case, the jurists have a detailed ruling on the degree of the error's effect on the contract:
(a) If the error is in the genus of the subject matter, such as selling or buying a gemstone as a ruby but it is glass, or buying a bag of rice but it is wheat, then the contract does not conclude at all according to Hanafi ijtihad, because the difference in genus renders the contractual object non-existent, which is one of the essential elements of the contract previously mentioned in 1/29 (2).
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage discusses the legal principle of error (ghalat) as a defect of consent in contracts. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, explains that in Islamic jurisprudence, unlike modern legal systems, the issue of error is not treated as a unified theory but is found scattered across various fiqh topics. He then derives a general rule: spontaneous error by one party does not invalidate the contract unless the error is clear and obvious to the other party. The rationale is that if the error is hidden, the manifest intention (al-irada al-zahira) prevails to protect the security of transactions. Only when the error is apparent does the principle of respecting true intention (al-irada al-batina) allow annulment without unfair surprise.

The author then clarifies when error is considered 'clear': either the erring party explicitly states his intent during the contract, or it is evident from surrounding circumstances. He elaborates the first sub-case: explicit disclosure. For example, if a buyer specifies the genus (jins) of the item, such as buying a 'ruby' that turns out to be glass, or buying 'rice' that is wheat. According to Hanafi fiqh, such an error in genus prevents the contract from concluding entirely (la yan'aqid aslan) because the subject matter (mahall) is not what was intended, which is a fundamental element of the contract. This corresponds to what modern jurists call 'obstructive error' (ghalat mani') that prevents formation, not merely a defect in consent.

The passage follows the author's own order: first outlining the general principle, then defining 'clear error', then giving the first sub-case with its Hanafi ruling. The commentary follows this sequence without importing outside rulings.

Key Terms
الغلط (al-ghalat): Spontaneous error by one party regarding a material aspect of the contract.
الإرادة الباطنة (al-irada al-batina): The true hidden intention of the contracting party.
الإرادة الظاهرة (al-irada al-zahira): The manifest intention expressed by the party.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view discussed, when does an error in the genus of the subject matter affect a sales contract?
A) The contract becomes voidable at the option of the erring party.
B) The contract is automatically annulled (fasid) and must be rescinded.
C) The contract does not conclude at all (la yan'aqid) because the subject matter is non-existent.
D) The contract remains valid but the buyer has the option of inspection (khiyar al-ru'ya).

Key Takeaway
Error invalidates consent only when clear; error in genus prevents contract formation in Hanafi fiqh.

Khiyar al-Ru'yah: The Buyer's Right to Annul Upon Sight
Stating the rule and rationale for Khiyar al-Ru'yah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule of Khiyar al-Ru'yah, identify its conditions and forfeiture, and explain why it applies only to the buyer.

Core Idea
The buyer of an unseen specific item may cancel the contract upon sight; the seller has no such right.

In Hanafi sales law, the general rule is that a contract is binding once concluded. However, as a special exception, the Option of Sight (Khiyar al-Ru'yah) allows the buyer who purchased an unseen specific item to rescind upon inspection. This right is derived from prophetic hadith and aims to prevent harm to the buyer, whose need is for the specific item itself. Let's examine the rule, its evidence, and its practical application for the mufti.

Guided Path
1
Identify the exceptional rule
Recognize that Khiyar al-Ru'yah is an exception to the principle of binding sale. It grants the buyer of an unseen specific item the right to annul upon sight if the item does not please him.
This is not a general option for all sales; it applies only when the item was unseen at contract.
2
Locate the dalil and its authority
The rule is based on the hadith: 'Whoever buys what he has not seen has the option when he sees it.' Note that despite weakness in one chain, Hanafi scholars accepted it as a valid proof because later jurists acted upon it.
This hadith is cited as the primary textual evidence.
3
Determine the scope: buyer only
Analyze the rationale: the buyer's need is to use the specific item; if it is unsuitable, he suffers harm. The seller's need is for the price, not the specific item, so harm is not expected. Therefore, the option is not granted to the seller.
This is a critical distinction; the rule is asymmetrical.
4
Identify conditions and forfeiture
The option exists if the item was unseen at the time of contract. It is forfeited by express or implied consent after seeing, or by any act that creates a third-party right (e.g., resale before sight).
Forfeiture preserves finality and protects third parties.
5
Apply the rule in fatwa
When a mufti encounters a case where a buyer bought an unseen specific item and wishes to cancel upon sight, he should confirm the item was unseen, check if the buyer has already consented or resold it, and then apply the option accordingly.
This step integrates the rule into mufti practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خيار الرؤية :
هناك حالة اعتبرها الشرع الإسلامي مظنة لغلط العاقد؛ فمنحه فيها حق الإبطال إن شاء لاحتمال غلطه وهي حالة شراء شيء لم يره
،
البيع لا يُلزم البائع والشراء لا يُلزم المشتري لأن الإرادة مكشوفة بالتسمية أو الاشتراط فالغلط واضح (ر) مصادر الحق للأستاذ السنهوري ص/ ١٢٣).
المشتري، فقد ورد في الحديث النبوي : من اشترى ما لم ير فله الخيار إذا رأى (١) .
وقد أخذ الاجتهاد الحنفي بهذا الحديث فقرر أن من يشتري شيئاً معيناً بالذات لم يره فله حق الإبطال إذا رآه فلم يوافقه. ويسمى هذا الحق: خيار الرؤية. ويسقط هذا الخيار بالرضا صراحة أو دلالة بعد الرؤية كما يسقط بكل تصرف يوجب حقاً للغير، كما لو باع المشتري ما اشتراه قبل أن يراه .
ويعلل الفقهاء مشروعية هذا الخيار بأنه لدفع الضرر عن المشتري إذ يجد أنَّ الشيء الذي اشتراه لاحتياجه إليه هو غير ملائم لحاجته لأنه لم يره فيتضرر بإلزامه، فإذا كان قد رآه حين التعاقد أو قبله بمدة لم يتغير خلالها فليس له خيار، لأنه اشترى على علم.
ولا يثبت هذا الخيار للبائع إذا باع ما لم ير، لأن الحديث النبوي لم يثبته إلا لمن اشترى ووجه ذلك أن غاية الشراء عادة هي استعمال عين الشيء في سد حاجة المشتري، فقد يتبين غير ملائم لهذه الحاجة، بخلاف البائع فإنَّ حاجته إلى الثمن لا إلى عين المبيع، فلا يتأتى في جانبه هذا الاعتبار.
والمراد من الرؤية هنا الاطلاع في كل شيء بحسبه، لا خصوص
(١) رواه البيهقي ٥: ٢٦٨ - ومن قبله ابن أبي شيبة (٦ ٧ (۱۸) - عن مكحول مرسلاً ، وفيه أبو بكر بن أبي مريم وهو ضعيف قال ابن الهمام في فتح القدير» ٥٣١:٥: عمل به - أي بالحديث - مالك وأحمد، وهو ممن نقل عنه تضعيف ابن أبي مريم فدل قبول العلماء على ثبوته .. وقد روي الحديث أيضاً مرفوعاً. رواه أبو حنيفة ... ويراجع لزاماً إعلاء السنن ٤٩:١٤ فما بعد، وفي ص ٥١ نقل عن الإمام محمد بن الحسن قوله في الحجة ص ٢٣٦: الحديث المعروف الذي لا يشك فيه عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم وعليه أمور المسلمين إلى يومهم هذا في الآفاق، أن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم قال: من اشترى شيئاً لم يره فهو بالخيار إذا رآه .
The Option of Sight: There is a case that Islamic law considered a source of mistake for the contracting party; so it granted him the right to annul if he wishes due to the possibility of his mistake. It is the case of buying something he has not seen. The sale does not bind the seller and the purchase does not bind the buyer because the intention is disclosed by naming or stipulating, so the mistake is clear (Source: Masadir al-Haqq, Prof. Sanhuri, p. 123). The buyer: it has been reported in the prophetic hadith: 'Whoever buys what he has not seen has the option when he sees it.' The Hanafi ijtihad adopted this hadith and ruled that whoever buys a specific thing that he has not seen has the right to annul when he sees it and it does not please him. This right is called the Option of Sight. This option is forfeited by express or implied consent after seeing, and also by any act that creates a right for others, such as if the buyer sells what he bought before seeing it. The jurists explain the legitimacy of this option as preventing harm from the buyer, as he finds the thing he bought for his need is unsuitable for his need because he had not seen it, so he would be harmed by compulsion. If he had seen it at the time of contract or before for a period during which it did not change, he has no option, because he bought knowingly. This option is not established for the seller if he sells what he has not seen, because the hadith only establishes it for the buyer. The reason is that the goal of buying is usually to use the specific thing to fulfill the buyer's need, so it may turn out unsuitable for that need, unlike the seller whose need is for the price, not the specific thing, so this consideration does not apply on his side. 'Seeing' here means inspection according to each thing, not necessarily literal sight. (1) Narrated by al-Bayhaqi 5:268, and before him Ibn Abi Shayba, from Makhul as a mursal hadith; it contains Abu Bakr ibn Abi Maryam who is weak. Ibn al-Humam in Fath al-Qadir 5:531 said: Malik and Ahmad acted upon it, and he was among those who reported the weakening of Ibn Abi Maryam, so the scholars' acceptance indicates its authenticity... It has also been narrated as marfu'. Abu Hanifa narrated it... See I'la' al-Sunan 14:49 ff., and on p. 51 it is reported from Imam Muhammad ibn al-Hasan in al-Hujja p. 236: the well-known hadith about which there is no doubt from the Prophet, upon which the affairs of the Muslims are based to this day in all regions, that the Messenger of Allah said: 'Whoever buys something he has not seen has the option when he sees it.'
Source pages p. 454

Commentary
This passage presents the Option of Sight (خيار الرؤية) as an exceptional case where Islamic law considers a hidden mistake as a valid excuse for the buyer to rescind the contract. The rule applies when someone buys an unseen specific item; upon seeing it, if it does not meet his approval, he may cancel. The Hanafi school adopts this based on the hadith 'Whoever buys what he has not seen has the option when he sees it.' The option is forfeited by explicit or implied consent after seeing, or by any act that creates a third-party right (e.g., resale). The rationale is to prevent harm to the buyer, whose need is for the specific item. The option is not granted to the seller because the seller's need is for the price, not the specific item. 'Seeing' is interpreted broadly as inspection appropriate to the item. The passage also provides a detailed discussion of the hadith's authenticity, noting that despite a weak narrator, it was accepted by leading scholars and acted upon in practice.

Key Terms
خيار الرؤية (khiyar al-ru'yah): The option granted to the buyer of an unseen specific item to cancel the contract upon sight.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when does the Option of Sight (خيار الرؤية) become void?
A) If the buyer sees the item and remains silent.
B) If the buyer sells the item before seeing it.
C) If the buyer pays the price immediately.
D) If the seller describes the item in detail.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school grants the buyer of an unseen specific item the right to cancel upon sight, but not the seller, based on hadith and the rationale of preventing harm to the buyer.

Hanbali Acceptance of Market Price Determination and the Hanafi Response
Discusses Hanbali acceptance of deferred price determination and bai' al-istijrar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanbali school allowed sale by market rate, identify the Hanafi original objection, and apply the later Hanafi concession of bai' al-istijrar to modern running accounts.

Core Idea
Price ignorance voids a contract — but reference to market rate can remove it, as Hanbalis and later Hanafis conceded.

This passage examines a key inter-madhhab dispute on the validity of sales where the price is not fixed at contract but left to the market rate. Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal allowed it, while the other three imams rejected it due to jahalah. Yet later Hanafis found a way to accommodate similar transactions through bai' al-istijrar, showing the dynamism of the Hanafi school in responding to commercial needs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanbali position
What did Imam Ahmad allow? Determine the specific sale method validated by the Hanbali school.
The sale is valid even without a specified price at contract; the price is determined by the market rate on the day of sale.
2
Identify the original Hanafi objection
Why did the other three imams reject this? Explain the concept of jahalah (ignorance) in price.
The price at contract is unknown, which leads to gharar and invalidates the sale according to the original Hanafi position.
3
Analyse the Hanbali justification
How does reference to market rate negate ignorance? Understand the reasoning of Ibn Qayyim.
The market rate provides an objective and verifiable standard that eliminates uncertainty and prevents disputes.
4
Locate the later Hanafi response
What is bai' al-istijrar and how does it differ from the Hanbali position? Identify the conditions and scope.
Bai' al-istijrar is a running account sale where prices are settled periodically based on market rates, allowed due to necessity.
5
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
What is the practical difference between the Hanbali and Hanafi positions in everyday transactions?
Hanbali: any sale by market rate is valid; Hanafi: only the specific form of istijrar is allowed, and only under necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الناحية الرابعة :
الفصل (٤٢) الاجتهادات في سلطان الإرادة العقدية
إنَّ الاجتهاد الحنبلي صحح طريقة البيع بما ينقطع عليه السعر، أي بما يستقر عليه سعر السوق في يوم البيع، دون تحديد للثمن عند العقد. وهذا لم يقبله أحد من الأئمة الثلاثة غير أحمد، لما فيه من جهالة الثمن عند العقد (۱)
أساساً
ولكن الاجتهاد الحنبلي اعتبر في هذا الاتفاق والشرط ما يضع صالحاً لتحديد الثمن ونفي الجهالة وحسم النزاع. وقد دافع عنه ابن القيم وأيد نظريته من الوجهة الشرعية والمصلحة الواقعية بما فيه الكفاية (ر ) إعلام الموقعين طبعة المطبعة المنيرية بمصر، (٣/٤()٢
وهذا غاية ما وصل إليه المنطق القانوني الحديث في شرائط التعاقد وما جاءتنا به المادة / ٩٦/ من القانون المدني الجديد لدينا من أنه : إذا اتفق العاقدان على النواحي الأساسية في العقد وأرجىء البحث في التفاصيل
(۱) إن متأخري الحنفية جوزوا من هذا القبيل النوع الذي أسموه: بيع الاستجرار، للحاجة إليه، واختلفوا في طريقة تخريجه (ر ) رد المحتار ١٢/٤). وبيع الاستجرار: هو أن يأخذ الإنسان حاجاته اليومية من عند اللحام أو البدال أو غيرهما دون اتفاق على ثمن كل منها عند الأخذ، ثم يحاسبه كل مدة. وهو المسمى اليوم بطريقة الحساب الجاري.
The fourth aspect: Chapter (42): Ijtihadat on the Authority of Contractual Will. Indeed, the Hanbali ijtihad validated the method of sale by what the price settles at, i.e., by what the market price stabilizes on on the day of sale, without specifying the price at the time of contract. None of the three imams other than Ahmad accepted this, due to the ignorance of the price at the time of contract fundamentally. But the Hanbali ijtihad considered in this agreement and condition what is valid for determining the price, negating ignorance, and settling dispute. Ibn Qayyim defended it and supported his theory from the legal and practical interest perspective sufficiently. (See I'lam al-Muwaqqi'in, Muniriyya Press, Cairo, 3/4(2). This is the utmost reached by modern legal logic in contracting conditions and what Article 96 of our new Civil Code brought us: If the two contracting parties agree on the essential aspects of the contract and postpone research on subsidiary details... (1) The late Hanafis permitted of this kind the type they called: bai' al-istijrar, due to need, and they differed on its method of derivation (see Radd al-Muhtar 12/4). Bai' al-istijrar is that a person takes his daily needs from the butcher or grocer or others without agreeing on the price of each at the time of taking, then settles accounts every period. It is what today is called the running account method.
Source pages p. 533

Commentary
This passage discusses the Hanbali school's innovative stance on contract pricing. While the other three schools require the price to be specified at the time of contract to avoid gharar (uncertainty), Ahmad ibn Hanbal validated a sale where the price is determined by the market rate on the day of sale. The Hanbali reasoning is that the reference to market rate provides a valid method of price determination that eliminates uncertainty and prevents disputes. Ibn Qayyim al-Jawziyya defended this view extensively. The author notes that later Hanafis allowed a similar practice called bai' al-istijrar (running account sale) due to necessity, though they differed on its juristic grounding. This shows the flexibility within the Hanafi school to accommodate changing commercial needs, even if the original Hanafi position requires a fixed price at contract.

Key Terms
بيع الاستجرار (bai' al-istijrar): A sale where the buyer takes goods regularly without fixed price each time, settling accounts periodically based on market rate
جهالة الثمن (jahalah al-thaman): Ignorance of the price, which invalidates a sale due to gharar
سلطان الإرادة العقدية (sultan al-irada al-aqdiyya): The authority of contractual will; the freedom of parties to stipulate terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary objection of the three imams (non-Hanbali) to a sale where the price is determined by market rate on the day of sale?
A) It involves riba
B) It involves gharar (uncertainty) in the price at contract
C) It is not explicitly mentioned in the Quran
D) It requires a written contract

Key Takeaway
The Hanbali school innovated by allowing price determination by market rate, and the later Hanafis conceded a similar practice (bai' al-istijrar) under necessity, demonstrating the flexibility of ijtihad.

Hanbali Contractual Flexibility and Its Relevance for Fatwa
Praise of Hanbali flexibility in contracts as a basis for modern legal comparison

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Hanbali approach to contractual freedom and apply its principle of expansion (tawsi'a) as a mufti considering contemporary contracts.

Core Idea
Following the Salaf led to contractual expansion, not restriction — a key principle for modern fatwa.

This lesson explores a passage from Mustafa al-Zarqa's work quoting Muhammad Abu Zahra, which highlights how Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal's adherence to the Salaf resulted in broad contractual permissions. For a mufti, understanding this flexibility is crucial when faced with modern contracts that may seem unprecedented. The Hanbali approach offers a paradigm of ease (rahma) that can inform fatwa in the Hanafi tradition, especially in situations of need.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central claim
Read the quote from Abu Zahra: it states that Imam Ahmad, following the Salaf, adopted 'expansion instead of restriction, and permissibility without prohibition' in contracts. This is the main point to absorb.
2
Trace the basis of the claim
The claim is based on the Salaf's method: the Companions dealt with emerging problems with the spirit of mercy, not burden. Imam Ahmad learned from their آثار (traditions). Note that this contradicts the stereotype that Salafism causes rigidity.
3
Contrast with a common criticism
The passage refutes the criticism that 'returning to the ways of the Salaf entails restriction'. The student should recognize this as a polemical context that shaped the quote.
4
Apply to the mufti's role
Consider how a Hanafi mufti might draw on this Hanbali flexibility. The passage implies that if the Salaf (including Hanbali fiqh) allowed certain contracts, it provides a basis for permissibility even in the Hanafi school, especially when Hanafi texts are silent or restrictive. This is a tool for talfiq or istihsan.
Remember that for fatwa, the mufti must still respect the authority of the Hanafi school; this is a comparative insight, not a direct source.

Source Text

This, and we have intended to elaborate on Hanbali ijtihad here because it is the one that, on the subject of the authority of contractual will, aligns with modern legal theories and is suitable to be a basis for comparison (1). (1) The venerable professor Sheikh Muhammad Abu Zahra says in his book 'Ibn Hanbal', after presenting Ahmad ibn Hanbal's madhhab on freedom of contracting and contractual conditions, the text: 'Thus you see that Imam who made the آثار (traditions) of the Salaf his teacher, graduated upon them and was guided by their guidance, ended up in contracts and many dealings of people with expansion instead of restriction, and permissibility without prohibition. And thereby the proof was established that those who claim that returning to the ways of the righteous Salaf entails restriction upon people do not know the reality of these آثار, and how the Companions trod the path, and how they treated the problems that arose with the spirit of the religion that came as a mercy to people, and did not come to burden them and restrict them. Commentary
This passage is a comparative note within a Hanafi work, quoting Muhammad Abu Zahra's book on Ibn Hanbal. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, explains that he chose to elaborate on Hanbali ijtihad because it resonates with modern legal theories on contractual freedom. The quote from Abu Zahra highlights that Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal, following the method of the Salaf, adopted a broad interpretive approach in contracts, permitting many transactions that later schools might restrict. This serves as evidence that returning to the Salaf does not cause hardship but rather brings ease, countering a common criticism. The examples of modern global contracts are mentioned as being accommodated within Hanbali fiqh. For students of Hanafi usul, this is an insight into comparative madhhab methodology, showing that different schools may reach different conclusions on contractual freedom, and that the Hanbali approach is particularly flexible.

Key Terms
سلطان الإرادة العقدية (sultan al-irada al-'aqdiyya): The authority or power of the contractual will — the principle that contracting parties have freedom to determine terms within the limits of Shariah.
التوسعة (al-tawsi'a): Expansion or leniency — the approach of permitting rather than prohibiting in contracts.
آثار السلف (athar al-salaf): The traditions and practices of the early generations (Salaf), including the Companions and their successors.

Key Terms from Source Passage

The Hanbali school, based on the Salaf's method, provides a flexible contractual framework that can serve as a resource for modern fatwa when the Hanafi school is silent or restrictive.

Sale as the Paradigm of Commutative Contracts: Terminology and Classification
Introduces sale as the chief commutative contract with subtypes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key terminological distinctions in the contract of sale (bay'/shira'/mabi'/thaman) and classify the derivative types of sale according to Hanafi jurisprudence, including the author's critique of bay' al-wafa'.

Core Idea
Sale is the 'head' of financial commutative contracts, setting the pattern for all other exchanges.

This passage from 'Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi' by Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa introduces the contract of sale (bay') as the foundational model for all financial commutative contracts in Islamic law. The author clarifies the technical Arabic terms used for the transaction, the parties, the object, and the price, and then lists the major derivative types of sale recognized by the Hanafi school. He also offers a critical note on the classification of bay' al-wafa', arguing it should be treated as an independent contract mixing sale and pledge. Understanding these basics is essential for any mufti who must correctly categorize contracts and apply the relevant rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central contract and its status
Notice the author's claim that sale (bay') is 'the head of financial commutative contracts' and that its rulings serve as the basis for analogy (qiyas) for many other contracts. This establishes sale as the prototype.
2
Parse the dual terminology (bay'/shira')
Understand that in Arabic the same word can refer to both buying and selling (addad). The author explains that customary usage has assigned 'sale' to the seller and 'purchase' to the buyer, but they are originally interchangeable.
Quranic example: 'And they sold him' (shara) means they sold.
3
Identify the subject matter and price
The object being sold is the mabi', and the financial consideration is the thaman (price). Thaman is usually money. Distinguish from qi'ma (value) in Hanafi fiqh.
4
List the derivative types of sale
The passage enumerates: salam (forward sale), muqayada (barter), sarf (currency exchange), istisna' (manufacturing contract), trust sales (murabahah, tawliyah, wadi'ah), and bay' al-wafa'. Note that these are considered branches of sale.
The Majalla also adds bay' al-marid (sale of a sick person), but the author argues it belongs in conditions of sale.
5
Evaluate the author's reclassification of bay' al-wafa'
The author disagrees with the Majalla's classification of bay' al-wafa' as a branch of sale. He argues it is a mixed contract combining sale and pledge, and should be treated independently after pledge. This is a methodological critique about contract taxonomy.
Consider how classification affects the application of rulings.

لكن عرف الناس والفقهاء على تخصيص لفظي «البيع، والبائع بجانب باذل السلعة التي تتعلق بها حاجة الانتفاع والاستعمال، وتخصيص لفظي «الشراء، والشاري بجانب باذل العوض المالي الآخر الذي يسمى أيضاً: «مشترياً ومبتاعاً».
والسلعة أو الشيء الذي هو محل البيع يسمى: «مبيعاً». والعوض المالي الآخر المتفق عليه ويبذله المشتري في مقابل امتلاك المبيع يسمى: ثَمَناً» (بفتحتين). وهذا يكون في الغالب من النقود.

(۱) بما أن كلا الطرفين في وضعين متماثلين من حيث المبادلة والمعاوضة، لذلك كان لفظ البيع معدوداً من أسماء الأضداد؛ وكذا لفظ الشراء، فيسمى كل من طرفي عقده في أصل اللغة بائعاً وشارياً، فيقال باع البضاعة أو شراها إذا عقد على تمليكها أو تملكها بعوض (ر: المصباح المنير). وفي القرآن العظيم حكاية عن يوسف عليه السلام: ﴿ وَشَرَوْهُ بِثَمَن بَخسٍ دَرَاهِمَ مَعْدُودَةٍ وَكَانُوا فِيهِ مِنَ الزَّاهِدِينَ ﴾ أي باعوه.
(۲) انظر ما تقدم عن بيوع الأمانة في بحث عيوب الرضا (ف ٢/٣٥ الحاشية).
الباب السادس : نظرية العقود وما إليها في الفقه
وقد ذكرت المجلة ثلاثة من هذه الأنواع الفرعية في آخر كتاب البيوع، وهي: بيع السَّلَم والاستصناع، وبيع الوفاء(۱).
غير أننا نرى أن بيع الوفاء يجب أن يعد عقداً مستقلاً ولا ينبغي أن يعتبر من فروع البيع، لأنه عقد مُستحدَث مزيج من بيع ورهن فسنفرده بالذكر بعد الرهن لأنه أقرب إليه في خصائصه كما سنرى .
(۱) ذكرت المجلة أيضاً بين هذه الأنواع الفرعية بيع المريض، وكذلك فعل قانوننا المدني. لكني أرى أن محله المناسب في مبحث شرائط البيع لأن النوعية في العقد إنما تختلف باختلاف في نوع موضوعه أو طريقته لا بنقص أهلية العاقد.
Chapter 46: Survey of the Most Important Named Contracts. It is the head of financial commutative contracts, and its rulings are the basis for analogy in many of their rulings. The Majalla began with it among named contracts and treated it in three hundred and some articles immediately after the general maxims from Article 101 to 403. This contract is originally called 'sale' and 'purchase' with respect to each of its two parties (1). However, the people and jurists have customarily specified the terms 'sale' and 'seller' for the party offering the commodity that is needed for use and utilization, and specified the terms 'purchase' and 'buyer' for the party offering the other financial consideration, who is also called 'purchaser' and 'buyer'. The commodity or thing that is the subject of sale is called 'the sold item'. The other agreed-upon financial consideration that the buyer offers in exchange for ownership of the sold item is called 'price' (with two fathas). This is usually money. Under the contract of sale there are many derivative types, such as salam or salaf (with two fathas in both), exchange, currency exchange, manufacturing contract, and trust sales (murabahah, tawliyah, wadiah)(2), and sale of wafa'. All these contracts are considered special branches of the contract of sale, and each is usually mentioned in a separate chapter within the book of sales where its rulings are treated. (1) Since both parties are in similar positions in terms of exchange and compensation, the word 'sale' is considered one of the antonyms; likewise the word 'purchase'. So in the original language each party to the contract is called seller and buyer; one says 'he sold the goods' or 'he purchased them' if he contracts to transfer ownership or acquire ownership for consideration (see al-Misbah al-Munir). In the Noble Quran, it is narrated about Yusuf (peace be upon him): 'And they sold him for a paltry price, a few dirhams, and they were among those who were indifferent to him' meaning they sold him. (2) See what preceded regarding trust sales in the discussion of defects of consent (section 2/35 footnote). Sixth Chapter: Theory of Contracts and What Pertains to Them in Fiqh. The Majalla mentioned three of these derivative types at the end of the book of sales: salam, manufacturing contract, and sale of wafa' (1). However, I believe that sale of wafa' should be considered an independent contract and should not be considered a branch of sale, because it is an innovative contract mixing sale and pledge, so we will treat it separately after pledge because it is closer to it in its characteristics, as we will see. (1) The Majalla also mentioned among these derivative types the sale of a sick person, and our civil code did likewise. But I think its appropriate place is in the discussion of conditions of sale because the specificity of a contract differs according to the type of its subject matter or its method, not due to a deficiency in the capacity of the contracting party.
Source pages p. 574

Commentary
This passage is an introductory survey of named contracts in Islamic jurisprudence, focusing on sale (bay') as the chief commutative contract. The author begins by establishing sale as the 'head' of financial commutative contracts, meaning it is the prototype from which analogies are drawn for other transactions. He notes that the Ottoman Majalla (civil code) devoted 303 articles to it. The passage then clarifies a linguistic point: in Arabic, 'bay' (sale) and 'shira' (purchase) originally apply to both parties, but custom and juristic usage have restricted 'sale/seller' to the party providing the commodity and 'purchase/buyer' to the party providing the price. The subject matter is called 'mabi', and the consideration is 'thaman'. Several derivative forms of sale are listed: salam (forward sale), muqayada (barter), sarf (currency exchange), istisna' (manufacturing contract), and trust sales (murabahah, tawliyah, wadi'ah), and bay' al-wafa' (sale with right of redemption). The author then critiques the classification of bay' al-wafa', arguing it should be treated as an independent contract combining sale and pledge, not as a branch of sale. This reflects a nuanced understanding of contract typology in Hanafi fiqh. The footnotes provide additional linguistic and historical context, including a Quranic verse where 'shara' means 'sold'.

Key Terms
بيع (bay'): The chief financial commutative contract; head of all such contracts.
شراء (shira'): The act of purchasing; originally interchangeable with bay' but customarily used for the buyer's side.
مبيع (mabi'): The commodity or object of sale.
ثمن (thaman): The price or financial consideration paid by the buyer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Sale is the paradigmatic commutative contract; its terminology and classification are foundational for understanding all financial transactions in Hanafi fiqh.
