Types of Kafalah in Hanafi Fiqh
Classification of kafalah by subject and legal effect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and differentiate the five types of kafalah (suretyship) in Hanafi fiqh and state the legal effect of each.

Core Idea
Kafalah adds liability; its types vary by subject and legal outcome.

This passage from Mustafa al-Zarqa's al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm classifies kafalah according to its subject matter. The classification is essential for a mufti to determine the rights and obligations of the parties in suretyship contracts. Understanding these types prevents common errors in fatwa, such as confusing personal guarantee with financial liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two primary categories
Read the first part of the passage and note that kafalah is divided based on its subject: financial (kafalah bil-mal) or personal (kafalah bin-nafs). Understand the key difference: one involves property, the other involves producing a person.
This is the first level of classification.
2
Examine the three sub-types of kafalah bil-mal
The passage lists three sub-types: kafalah bid-dayn (guarantee of a debt), kafalah bil-'ayn (guarantee of delivering a specific item), and kafalah bid-darak (guarantee against defects in title). For each, identify the specific obligation being guaranteed.
These are the second-level classifications under financial suretyship.
3
Understand the foundational principle of kafalah
The passage emphasizes that kafalah is based on participation (ishrak) and solidarity (tadhamun), not transfer of responsibility. This means the creditor can claim from the principal, the surety, or both. Contrast this with hawalah (transfer of debt).
This principle is critical for determining the rights of the parties.
4
Analyze the special case of kafalah bin-nafs
Kafalah bin-nafs alone has no financial result; it only obligates the surety to produce the person. However, if the surety conditions payment upon failure to produce, it becomes a financial guarantee. Recognize how intention and condition can change the nature of the contract.
This nuance is often misunderstood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل (٤٦) عرض لأهم العقود المسماة
وهذه الكفالة أنواع متميزة في بعض الأحكام بحسب اختلاف موضوعها :
( أ ) فقد يكون موضوعها التزاماً مالياً ؛ وهذه تسمى: كفالة بالمال. (ب) وقد يكون موضوعها التزام الكفيل بإحضار الشخص المسؤول الأصلي بالحق ؛ وهذه تسمى: كفالة بالنفس.
ثم إنَّ الكفالة بالمال ثلاثة أنواع:
۱ - فقد تكون التزاماً بأداء دين في ذمة الغير، وتسمى: كفالة بالدين . ۲ - وقد تكون بتسليم عين معينة موجودة في يد الغير، كرد المغصوب إلى الغاصب وتسليم المبيع إلى المشتري، وتسمى كفالة بالعين، أو كفالة بالتسليم.
٣ - وقد يكون موضوعها ضماناً لخلوص المال المبيع من كل حق لغير البائع، أي ضماناً لحقوق المشتري تجاه البائع إذا ظهر للمبيع مستحق، كما لو تبين أن المبيع مملوك لغير البائع أو مرهون.
وهذا النوع الأخير يسمى كفالة بالدَّرَك بفتحتين)، أي بما يدرك المال المبيع ويلحق به من خطر بسبب سابق على البيع.
ولما كانت الكفالة تقوم على أساس اشتراك الكفيل وتضامنه مع المكفول عنه في المسؤولية، لا على أساس نقل هذه المسؤولية من عهدة الأصيل إلى الكفيل كان للطالب المكفول له أن يطالب بحقه من يشاء من الأصيل أو الكفيل أو كليهما معاً.
على أنَّ الكفالة بالنفس فقط ليس لها من نتيجة مالية، وإنما نتيجتها إجبار الكفيل بقوة القضاء على إحضار المسؤول الأصيل.
ولكن تعليق المسؤولية بالمال على عدم تنفيذ الكفالة بالنفس يجعلها كفالة بالمال أيضاً كما لو قال: إن لم أحضر لك المدين في الوقت الفلاني فعلي ما عليه، فإنه يكون كفيلاً بالدين الذي عليه إن لم يحضره في الموعد المضروب . (المجلة ٦٤٢ و ٦٥١).
Chapter (46): Presentation of the Most Important Named Contracts. And this kafalah (suretyship) is of types distinguished in some rulings according to the difference of its subject: (a) Its subject may be a financial obligation; this is called: kafalah bil-mal (suretyship for property). (b) And its subject may be the surety's obligation to bring the original responsible person for the right; this is called: kafalah bin-nafs (suretyship for the person). Then, kafalah bil-mal is of three types: 1 - It may be an obligation to pay a debt in the liability of another, called: kafalah bid-dayn (suretyship for debt). 2 - And it may be for delivering a specific existing thing in the hand of another, like returning the usurped property to the usurper and delivering the sold item to the buyer, called: kafalah bil-'ayn (suretyship for the specific thing), or kafalah bit-tasleem (suretyship for delivery). 3 - And its subject may be a guarantee of the sold property being free from any right of other than the seller, i.e., a guarantee of the buyer's rights against the seller if a claimant appears for the sold property, as if it becomes clear that the sold property is owned by someone other than the seller or mortgaged. This last type is called: kafalah bid-darak (with two fathahs), i.e., for what catches up to the sold property and attaches to it of risk due to a prior cause to the sale. And since kafalah is based on the surety's participation and solidarity with the principal in responsibility, not on transferring this responsibility from the principal to the surety, the creditor may demand his right from whomever he wishes of the principal or the surety or both together. However, kafalah bin-nafs alone has no financial result; rather its result is to compel the surety by judicial power to bring the original responsible person. But attaching financial responsibility to the non-fulfillment of kafalah bin-nafs makes it also kafalah bil-mal, as if he says: 'If I do not bring you the debtor at such time, then I am liable for what is upon him,' then he becomes a surety for the debt that is upon him if he does not bring him at the appointed time. (Majallah 642 and 651).
Source pages p. 576

Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification of kafalah (suretyship) from the Hanafi perspective as found in the Majallah. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, first distinguishes between kafalah bil-mal (financial suretyship) and kafalah bin-nafs (suretyship for the person). Then he further divides kafalah bil-mal into three sub-types: kafalah bid-dayn (guarantee of a debt), kafalah bil-'ayn (guarantee of delivering a specific item), and kafalah bid-darak (guarantee against defects in title). He emphasizes that kafalah does not transfer the debt but adds a second liability, so the creditor can claim from either the principal or the surety. A key point is that kafalah bin-nafs alone only obligates the surety to produce the person, not to pay. However, if the surety conditionally promises to pay upon failure to produce, it becomes a financial guarantee. This aligns with Hanafi rules where intention and conditions can change the nature of the contract.

Key Terms
كفالة (kafalah): A contract where a surety (kafeel) joins his liability to that of the principal (makful 'anhu) for a debt or obligation.
كفالة بالمال (kafalah bil-mal): Suretyship where the subject is a financial obligation such as a debt, delivery of an item, or guarantee of title.
كفالة بالنفس (kafalah bin-nafs): Suretyship where the surety guarantees to produce the person of the principal, not to pay a financial amount.
كفالة بالدَّرَك (kafalah bid-darak): Suretyship guaranteeing the sold item is free from prior rights; the surety compensates if a prior claim emerges.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi fiqh, which type of kafalah involves a guarantee against defects in the seller's title to the sold property?
A) Kafalah bil-mal
B) Kafalah bid-dayn
C) Kafalah bid-darak
D) Kafalah bin-nafs

Key Takeaway
Kafalah in Hanafi fiqh is an additional guarantee, not a transfer; its types determine the scope of the surety's obligation.

Hawalah: Definition and Distinctive Features
Definition and distinguishing features of hawalah (assignment of debt)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define hawalah, identify its four parties, and distinguish it from kafalah by explaining the effect on liability.

Core Idea
Hawalah transfers liability entirely; the original debtor is released, unlike kafalah where both remain liable.

In this passage, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa presents the Hanafi definition of hawalah (transfer of debt) as a contract that shifts responsibility from one debtor to another. He carefully distinguishes it from kafalah (suretyship), highlighting that in hawalah the creditor's claim is confined to the new debtor. This distinction has significant practical implications for drafting fatwas on debt transfer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four parties of hawalah
Locate and list the Arabic terms for the original debtor, creditor, new debtor, and the debt itself as mentioned in the text.
The terms are: muheel, muhaal, muhaal 'alayh, muhaal bihi.
2
Define the legal effect of hawalah
Determine what happens to the original debtor's liability after the contract. Look for the phrase 'right is confined to the new debtor'.
The original debtor is released; the creditor can only claim from the new debtor.
3
Compare with kafalah
Identify the key difference between hawalah and kafalah as described in the text. Focus on the phrase 'by this transfer it differs from kafalah'.
In kafalah, the surety is added to the principal's liability; in hawalah, liability is transferred.
4
Note the Hanafi position
The Hanafi school holds that hawalah is a transfer, not a guarantee. The passage reflects this view.
This is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position in the Hanafi school.
5
Recognize cross-madhhab difference
The Shafi'i school treats hawalah as a form of guarantee, where the original debtor remains liable if the new debtor defaults. Compare this to the Hanafi view.
The difference affects practical fatwa outcomes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٤٦ / ٦ - (٤) الحوالة : (م/ ٦٨٣ - ٧٠٠) . وهي : عقد موضوعه : نقل المسؤولية بالدين عن المدين الأصلي إلى غيره . فالمدين محيل والدائن : مُحال . والشخص الثالث الذي التزم الدفع عن المدين : مُحال عليه. والدين الذي هو محل الحوالة : محال به . وفي الحوالة ينحصر حق الدائن بمطالبة المحال عليه الذي يصبح هو المكلف بأداء الدين المحال به. وبهذا يتجلى في الحوالة نقل المسؤولية بالدين. وبهذا النقل تفترق عن الكفالة التي تقوم على أساس ضم ذمة الكفيل إلى ذمّة الأصيل توثيقاً، فيطالب فيها الدائن كلاً من الكفيل والأصيل المكفول عنه كما تقدم آنفاً.
46/6 - (4) Al-Hawalah: (M/683-700). It is: a contract whose subject is the transfer of responsibility for the debt from the original debtor to another. So the debtor is the transferor (muheel) and the creditor is the transferee (muhaal). The third person who undertakes payment on behalf of the debtor is the transferee (muhaal 'alayh). The debt that is the subject of the hawalah is the transferred debt (muhaal bihi). In hawalah, the creditor's right is confined to claiming from the transferee, who becomes the one obligated to pay the transferred debt. By this, the transfer of responsibility for the debt is manifested in hawalah. And by this transfer, it differs from kafalah, which is based on joining the surety's liability to the principal's liability as a security, so the creditor claims from both the surety and the principal as mentioned earlier.
Source pages p. 577

Commentary
This passage defines hawalah (assignment of debt) as a contract that transfers the liability for a debt from the original debtor to a third party. The author clearly distinguishes hawalah from kafalah: in hawalah, the creditor's right is limited to the new debtor (muhaal 'alayh), whereas in kafalah the original debtor remains liable alongside the surety. This is a core Hanafi distinction; the Majallah adopts the view that hawalah is a transfer of liability, not merely a guarantee. The text also introduces the technical terms: muheel (transferor debtor), muhaal (creditor), muhaal 'alayh (transferee), and muhaal bihi (the debt). Understanding these terms is essential for applying hawalah correctly in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
حوالة (hawalah): A contract transferring a debt from one person to another, making the transferee the sole debtor.
محيل (muheel): The original debtor who transfers his debt to another.
محال عليه (muhaal 'alayh): The person who becomes liable for the debt after hawalah.
محال به (muhaal bihi): The debt that is the subject of the hawalah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi fiqh, what effect does a valid hawalah have on the original debtor's liability?
A) He remains jointly liable with the new debtor
B) He is released from liability
C) He becomes a guarantor for the new debtor
D) His liability is suspended until the new debtor defaults

Key Takeaway
Hawalah in Hanafi law is a transfer of debt liability that releases the original debtor, distinguishing it from kafalah where liability is cumulative.

Rahn: The Hanafi Pledge Contract and Its Priority Right
Definition, purpose, and distinction from kafalah of rahn (pawn/pledge)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi concept of rahn, distinguish it from kafalah, and apply the rule of priority in bankruptcy.

Core Idea
Rahn gives the creditor a priority right over other creditors in the pledged property, unlike kafalah which only provides personal liability.

In Hanafi law, a creditor can secure a debt by taking a pledge (rahn) of the debtor's property. This contract creates a stronger security than a personal guarantee (kafalah) because the creditor holds an asset. This lesson explores the definition, parties, and the crucial legal effect: the mortgagee's priority over other creditors in case of the debtor's bankruptcy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their roles
Who is the rahin (pledgor) and who is the murtahin (pledgee)? The rahin is the debtor, the murtahin is the creditor holding the property as security.
2
Understand the purpose of rahn
Rahn is a contract of tawthiq (security) – its purpose is to secure the debt by retaining property that can satisfy the claim.
Compare with kafalah (personal guarantee).
3
Analyze the bankruptcy scenario
If the debtor becomes bankrupt, the murtahin has priority (haqq al-taqaddum) over other creditors to recover his debt from the pledged property. Any surplus is distributed proportionally among other creditors.
This is the key practical difference from kafalah.
4
Compare with kafalah
In kafalah, the surety's liability is personal; if both principal and surety are insolvent, there is no asset to seize. Rahn provides a material guarantee.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(٧/٤٦ - (٥) الرهن (المجلة/ ۷۰۱ – ۷٦١) : وهو عقد موضوعه احتباس مال لقاء حق يمكن استيفاؤه منه . فصاحب الحق، أو الدائن آخذ الرهن هو المرتهن ومعطي الرهن، أي الشخص المدين، هو الراهن. وغاية الرهن توثيقية كالكفالة، لكنه أقوى توثيقاً منها، لأنه يوثق الاستيفاء باحتجاز مال يضعه المدين الراهن تحت يد المرتهن ؛ فيكون للراهن امتياز في استيفاء حقه منه. فلو أفلس المدين وليس له مال سوى المرهون فليس لبقية الغرماء الدائنين مزاحمة المرتهن في المرهون بل يكون للدائن المرتهن التقدم عليهم في استيفاء دينه منه. فإن زاد شيء من قيمته عن حق المرتهن يدفع إليهم فيقتسمونه بنسبة ديونهم بخلاف الكفالة، فإنها توثيق ذمة بذمة في المسؤولية ؛ فإذا أفلس كل من المكفول والكفيل لم يبق فيها ضمان مادي للتحصيل.
(46/7 - (5) Ar-Rahn (Majallah 701-761): It is a contract whose subject is the retention of property in exchange for a right that can be satisfied from it. So the owner of the right, or the creditor who takes the pledge, is the mortgagee (murtahin), and the giver of the pledge, i.e., the debtor, is the mortgagor (rahin). The purpose of rahn is security like kafalah, but it is stronger security than it, because it secures satisfaction by detaining property that the debtor-mortgagor places under the hand of the mortgagee; so the mortgagee has a priority in satisfying his right from it. If the debtor becomes bankrupt and has no property other than the pledged property, the other creditors have no right to compete with the mortgagee in the pledged property; rather the mortgagee-creditor has priority over them in satisfying his debt from it. If anything of its value exceeds the mortgagee's right, it is given to them and they divide it according to the proportion of their debts. This is unlike kafalah, for it is a security of liability by liability; so if both the principal and the surety become bankrupt, there remains no material guarantee for collection.
Source pages p. 577

Commentary
This passage explains rahn (pledge) as a contract where property is held as collateral for a debt. The author compares it with kafalah, noting that rahn provides stronger security because it involves actual property held by the creditor. A key Hanafi rule is that if the debtor goes bankrupt, the pledgee has priority over other creditors in recovering his debt from the pledged property. Any surplus goes to other creditors. This is the 'right of priority' (haqq al-taqaddum) specific to rahn. In contrast, kafalah only gives a personal right against the surety; if both principal and surety are insolvent, the creditor has no tangible asset to fall back on. The text also defines the parties: rahin (debtor/pledgor) and murtahin (creditor/pledgee). The Majallah articles (701-761) provide detailed Hanafi rules.

Key Terms
رهن (rahn): A contract where a debtor places property under the creditor's control as security for a debt, giving the creditor the right to sell the property if the debt is not paid.
راهن (rahin): The debtor who pledges his property as security.
مرتهن (murtahin): The creditor who holds the pledged property as security.
توثيق (tawthiq): The legal purpose of making a debt more secure; in this context, rahn is a stronger tawthiq than kafalah because it involves holding an asset.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi fiqh, what happens if a debtor who has given a pledge becomes bankrupt?
A) The pledged property is distributed equally among all creditors
B) The mortgagee has priority to recover his debt from the pledged property
C) The pledge is void and the property returns to the debtor
D) The mortgagee loses his right because of bankruptcy

Key Takeaway
Rahn provides a stronger security than kafalah because it gives the creditor a proprietary interest in the pledged property, granting priority in bankruptcy.

Critique of the Ottoman Land Code on Bai' al-Wafa
Critiques the codified law for misrepresenting a classical contract

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key error in the Ottoman Land Code regarding bai' al-wafa and explain the correct Hanafi default rule for benefit ownership.

Core Idea
Bai' al-wafa entitles the buyer to all property benefits by default—a misreading of the Majalla turned it into a mere mortgage.

The Ottoman Land Code of 3339 and the Majalla are supposed to codify Hanafi fiqh, but a close reading reveals a critical misunderstanding. This passage shows how misinterpreting Article 398 led to a law that stripped bai' al-wafa of its distinctive function. We will dissect this error and affirm the correct default rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify Article 95's rule
Find the specific provision in the Land Code that assigns benefits to the seller by default and makes the buyer responsible for fruits and profits.
This is the target of the author's critique.
2
Consequence of the rule
Understand that this rule eliminates any practical difference between bai' al-wafa and mortgage, making the contract futile.
The author calls it 'عبثاً وتكراراً محضاً لأحكام الرهن'.
3
Compare with correct Hanafi default
State the correct Hanafi position from Article 118 of the Majalla: the buyer owns all benefits unless otherwise stipulated.
Article 118 explicitly differentiates bai' al-wafa from mortgage.
4
Analyze the misinterpretation of Article 398
Explain why Article 398 does not change the default; it only applies when a stipulation is made for a portion of benefits.
The outward appearance of Article 398 may mislead, but it must be read together with Article 118.
5
Note the final prohibition
Recognize that the 1949 Civil Code banned bai' al-wafa entirely, but the author implies that with correct understanding, the contract is valid and useful.
This shows the impact of legal codification on classical fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كانت النصوص القانونية المعول عليها لدينا في أحكام بيع الوفاء هي المواد / ۹۱ - ١٠٠ من قانون الملكية العقارية ذي الرقم / ۳۳۳۹/ . وقد نصت المادة / ٩٥/ منه على أن منافع العقار المبيع بالوفاء تكون بمقتضى العقد للبائع لا للمشتري، ويكون المشتري مسؤولاً وضامناً لكل ما يستفيده من ثمرات ومنافع المبيع، فتطرح قيمتها من أصل الدين إلا إذا اشترط الطرفان خلاف ذلك. وبذلك لم يبق فرق يذكر بين رهن العقار وبيعه وفاء في أحكام هذا القانون فيصبح الوفاء فيه عبثاً وتكراراً محضاً لأحكام الرهن فيه باسم جديد. بيع وهذا من جهل واضعي القانون بمبنى الفرق بين الرهن وبيع الوفاء، بحسب منشأ هذا العقد. فإن الوفاء إنما استحدث إلى جانب الرهن على أساس أن يستحق المشتري بيع فيه منافع المبيع بمقتضى العقد على خلاف حكم الرهن، كما أوضحناه. ولعل خطأ واضعي هذا القانون من هذه الناحية ناشيء عما يوهمه ظاهر المادة/ ٣٩٨/ من المجلة، إذ تقول إنه : إذا اشترط في بيع الوفاء أن يكون قدر من منافع المبيع للمشتري صح ذلك». بيع فإن ظاهرها يوهم لكنها يجب أن تفهم بأنه إذا اشترط للمشتري بالوفاء جزء فقط من منافع المبيع ينحصر حقه في المشروط، أما إذا لم يشرط في العقد شيء فالمنافع كلها للمشتري بمقتضى العقد. وذلك بدلالة المادة // ۱۱۸ / من المجلة نفسها. فقد صرحت هذه المادة أن الوفاء إنما يشبه البيع الصحيح ويفترق عن الرهن من جهة أن المشتري وفاء يملك منافع المبيع، وهو الحكم الذي قرره الفقهاء في بيع الوفاء. وهذا ما حققه والدي الشيخ أحمد الزرقاء رحمه الله تعالى في شرحه على القواعد الكلية من المجلة. وتفصيل النظر الفقهي في بيع الوفاء وأحكامه، علاوة على ما ورد منه في المجلة، يرى في الدرر شرح الغرر»، وفي رد المحتار)، وفي الفتاوى البزازية أواخر كتاب البيوع، وفي الفصل // ۱۸ / من جامع الفصولين وهو أوفاها بحثاً. وأخيراً جاء القانون المدني سنة ١٩٤٩م، فمنع بيع الوفاء، واعتبره باطلا، استغناء عنه بالرهن (ر: ق م ٤٣٣).
The legal texts relied upon in our country for the rulings of bai' al-wafa were Articles 91-100 of the Land Law No. 3339. Article 95 of it stated that the benefits of the real estate sold by wafa belong by virtue of the contract to the seller, not the buyer, and the buyer is responsible and guarantees every benefit he gets from the fruits and profits of the sold item, so its value is deducted from the principal debt unless the parties stipulate otherwise. Thus, no significant difference remained between a mortgage of real estate and its sale by wafa in the provisions of this law, so the wafa became in it a futile and pure repetition of the rules of mortgage under a new name. This is due to the ignorance of the law makers of the basis of the difference between mortgage and bai' al-wafa according to the origin of this contract. For bai' al-wafa was devised alongside mortgage on the basis that the buyer in wafa is entitled to the benefits of the sold item by virtue of the contract, contrary to the rule of mortgage, as we have explained. Perhaps the error of the makers of this law arises from what is suggested by the outward wording of Article 398 of the Majalla, which says: 'If it is stipulated in bai' al-wafa that a portion of the benefits of the sold item is for the buyer, that is valid.' Its outward appearance is suggestive, but it must be understood that if a part only of the benefits of the sold item is stipulated for the buyer in wafa, his right is limited to what is stipulated; but if nothing is stipulated in the contract, then all benefits belong to the buyer by virtue of the contract. This is supported by Article 118 of the same Majalla. For that article explicitly states that wafa resembles a valid sale and differs from a mortgage in that the buyer in wafa owns the benefits of the sold item, which is the ruling established by the jurists in bai' al-wafa. This is what my father, Sheikh Ahmad al-Zarqa (may Allah have mercy on him) verified in his commentary on the general rules of the Majalla. Detailed fiqh investigation on bai' al-wafa and its rulings, besides what is mentioned in the Majalla, can be found in al-Durar Sharh al-Ghurar, Radd al-Muhtar, al-Fatawa al-Bazzaziyya at the end of the book of sales, and in Chapter 18 of Jami' al-Fusulayn, which is the most comprehensive. Finally, the Civil Code of 1949 came and prohibited bai' al-wafa, considering it void, dispensing with it due to mortgage (see Civil Code, Article 433).
Source pages p. 580

Commentary
This passage critiques the Ottoman Land Code and the Majalla for misunderstanding bai' al-wafa. The law incorrectly assigned benefits to the seller, making the contract identical to a mortgage. The author attributes this to a misreading of Article 398 of the Majalla, which seems to indicate that benefits go to the buyer only if stipulated. However, the author clarifies that the default in bai' al-wafa is that all benefits belong to the buyer, as supported by Article 118 of the same code. He refers to his father's work and other authoritative Hanafi sources for the correct ruling. Finally, he notes that the 1949 Civil Code banned bai' al-wafa altogether, deeming it unnecessary due to the existence of mortgage. This lesson demonstrates how legal codification can distort classical fiqh, and the importance of correctly understanding the original rulings.

Key Terms
المجلة (al-Majalla): The Ottoman Civil Code (Majallat al-Ahkam al-Adliyya), which codified Hanafi fiqh rules.
القانون المدني (al-qanun al-madani): The Civil Code of 1949 that prohibited bai' al-wafa.
بيع الوفاء (bai' al-wafa): A sale with the right of the seller to repurchase; used as a security for debt.
منافع (manafi'): Benefits/usufruct of the property, e.g., rent, fruits.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, what was the main error of the Ottoman Land Code regarding bai' al-wafa?
A) It allowed the buyer to take interest.
B) It assigned the benefits of the property to the seller by default, making it identical to a mortgage.
C) It prohibited bai' al-wafa entirely.
D) It required the buyer to pay a higher price.

Key Takeaway
The correct Hanafi position on bai' al-wafa is that all benefits belong to the buyer by default, and misinterpreting the Majalla led to a flawed codification.

6 min
Contract of Deposit (Wadi'ah) and Its Trust Nature
Defines the contract of deposit and its trust nature

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the contract of deposit (wadi'ah), identify its parties, and state the rule of liability for the depositary according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
A deposit is an amanah: the depositary is not liable unless he transgresses or neglects.

This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Aam introduces the contract of deposit (wadi'ah), one of the foundational trust contracts in Hanafi fiqh. Understanding its nature as an amanah (trust) is crucial because it determines the depositary's liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contract type
Recognize that deposit is a contract of safekeeping, not a transfer of ownership. Its subject is preservation of property.
2
Define the parties and subject matter
The depositor (mudi') entrusts property to the depositary (wadi'), and the property itself is called wadi'ah.
The term 'wadi'ah' can also refer to the contract itself.
3
Understand the amanah effect
The property is held in trust; the depositary is not liable for loss from a heavenly affliction (āfah samāwiyyah) or similar causes.
Amanah means no financial liability for fortuitous loss.
4
Note the exception for liability
Liability arises only if the depositary exceeds authority (ta'addi) or is negligent (taqsir) leading to destruction or loss.
These are the only two grounds for liability.
5
Distinguish from other trust contracts
Deposit's sole purpose is safekeeping, unlike lease (ijārah) or loan for use ('āriyah) which have other primary purposes and trust is incidental.
This makes deposit the 'head of trust contracts'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب السادس : نظرية العقود وما إليها في الفقه
٩/٤٦ - (٧) الإبداع: المجلة / ٧٦٢ - ٨٠٣).
وهو عقد موضوعه استعانة الإنسان بغيره في حفظ ماله . فصاحب المال مودع بكسر الدال». والعاقد الآخر المؤتمن على المال الذي تعهد بحفظه وديع والمال المدفوع للحفظ : وديعة .
وقد يطلقون «الوديعة» على معنى عقد الإيداع نفسه.
ومال الوديعة في يد الوديع يعتبر أمانة ، والوديع أمين.
ومعنى كونه أمانة في اصطلاح فقهاء الشريعة أنه لا يكون الأمين ضامناً له أي إنه غير مسؤول بماله عما يصيبه من التلف فما دونه بآفة سماوية. وإنما يكون الأمين ضامناً للأمانة إذا تعدى عليها أو قصر في حفظها حتى تلفت أو تغيبت ..
وعقد الإيداع يعتبر رأس عقود الأمانات، فإنه العقد الوحيد الذي موضوعه ومقصده المباشر إنما هو الائتمان على الحفظ. بخلاف العقود الأخرى التي يكون المال فيها بيد العاقد أمانة أيضاً، فإن لكل عقد منها هو مقصدها المباشر غير مجرد الائتمان كالإجارة والإغارة .
موضوعاً آخر
والشركة .
Book Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Fiqh. 9/46 - (7) Deposit: Majalla / 762-803. It is a contract whose subject is a person seeking help from another in preserving his property. So the owner of the property is the depositor (with kasra on the daal). The other party, the trustee of the property who undertakes its preservation, is the depositary, and the property handed over for preservation is the deposit. They may also use 'the deposit' to mean the contract of deposit itself. The property of the deposit in the depositary's hand is considered a trust, and the depositary is a trustee. The meaning of it being a trust in the terminology of the jurists of the Shari'ah is that the trustee is not liable for it, i.e., he is not financially responsible for what befalls it of destruction or the like due to a heavenly affliction. The trustee is only liable for the trust if he exceeds his authority or is negligent in its preservation so that it is destroyed or lost. The contract of deposit is considered the head of trust contracts, for it is the only contract whose subject and direct purpose is entrustment for preservation. Unlike other contracts in which property is also a trust in the hands of the contracting party, for each of them has a different direct purpose besides mere entrustment, such as lease and loan for use. Another subject: and partnership.
Source pages p. 581

Commentary
This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Aam defines the contract of deposit (wadi'ah). It explains the parties: the depositor (mudi') and the depositary (wadi'), and the property (wadi'ah). The key legal effect is that the property is an amanah (trust) in the hands of the depositary, meaning he is not liable for loss unless he exceeds his authority or neglects his duty. The contract of deposit is the head of trust contracts, as its sole purpose is safekeeping, unlike other contracts like lease or loan where trust is incidental.

Key Terms
الإيداع (al-Iida'): A contract where one person entrusts property to another for safekeeping.
أمانة (Amanah): A property held in trust, where the holder is not liable for loss due to no fault of his own.
الوديع (al-Wadi'): The person who accepts the deposit; the depositary.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When is the depositary (wadi') liable for loss of the deposit?
A) In all cases of loss
B) Only if the loss is due to transgression or negligence
C) Only if the loss is from a celestial affliction
D) Only if the deposit was not in a sealed container

Key Takeaway
The contract of deposit creates a trust: the depositary bears no liability for loss absent his own wrongdoing.

Contract of Loan for Use (I'arah) and Its Distinction from Ijarah
Defines the contract of loan for use and contrasts it with lease.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the Hanafi contract of i'arah (loan for use), identify its key terms, and distinguish it from ijarah (lease) based on the presence or absence of consideration.

Core Idea
I'arah grants free usufruct; ijarah sells it for a price.

In this passage, we learn the definition of the contract of loan for use (i'arah). It is a gratuitous contract where the lender grants the borrower permission to use an item without compensation. The passage also clarifies the key terms (mu'ir, musta'ir, 'ariyah) and contrasts i'arah with ijarah, emphasizing that the only difference is the presence of consideration.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core definition
Locate the sentence defining i'arah: 'a contract donating the benefits of a thing for its use and return.' This establishes the nature of the contract as gratuitous and temporary.
2
Recognize the key terms
Identify the parties (mu'ir = lender, musta'ir = borrower) and the object ('ariyah). Note that sometimes 'ariyah is used for the contract itself.
3
Understand the gratuitous nature
The passage states that in i'arah, ownership of the benefit is transferred without consideration, i.e., free of charge.
4
Contrast with ijarah
Compare i'arah with ijarah: both involve usufruct, but ijarah is a sale of benefits for compensation, while i'arah is a donation.

6/10 - (8) Loan for use: Majalla / 804-832. A contract; it pertains to donating the benefits of a thing for its use and return, so it involves ownership of the benefit gratuitously without consideration. So the owner of the thing is the lender, and the taker for use is the borrower, and the thing that is the subject of the contract is the loan article (with tashdid on the ya). They may also use 'the loan article' to mean the contract of loan for use itself. The contract of loan for use corresponds to lease, which is based on ownership of the benefit for compensation: in lease there is a sale of benefits, while in loan for use there is a donation of them. The only difference is the presence or absence of consideration.
Source pages p. 581

Commentary
This passage defines the contract of i'arah (loan for use). It is a gratuitous contract where the owner gives permission to use an item for free. The parties are mu'ir (lender) and musta'ir (borrower), and the item is 'ariyah. It contrasts with ijarah (lease) where the usufruct is sold for compensation. The difference is the presence or absence of consideration.

Key Terms
الإعارة (al-I'arah): A contract granting free use of a thing's usufruct for a period, after which the thing is returned.
العارية (al-'Ariyah): The thing given on loan for use; the subject of the contract.
الإجارة (al-Ijarah): A contract of lease where usufruct is sold for rent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
I'arah and ijarah differ only by the presence or absence of consideration; both involve usufruct.

Defining Mudaraba: Capital and Labor Partnership
Defines mudaraba as a type of partnership contract

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define mudaraba, identify its two parties, and explain the difference between the Hanafi term mudaraba and the Shafi'i/Maliki term qirad.

Core Idea
Mudaraba: capital from one, labor from another — profit shared, loss solely on capital provider.

This passage introduces mudaraba, a core partnership contract in Islamic finance. You'll learn its structure, key parties, and the synonymous term qirad used by other schools. Understanding this foundation is essential for analyzing more complex investment contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contract type
Recognize that mudaraba is classified as a type of contractual partnership (شركة العقد). This distinguishes it from ownership partnerships.
شركة العقد means a partnership formed by mutual agreement.
2
Distinguish the contributions
Note the separation: capital from one party (rabb al-mal) and labor/management from the other (mudarib). No mixing of roles.
If both provide capital, it becomes a different contract (sharika al-'inan).
3
Extract the profit-sharing rule
Profit is shared according to an agreed ratio. The passage does not detail loss, but loss is borne solely by the capital provider absent negligence.
This is a key feature of mudaraba.
4
Note the terminology difference
Observe that Hanafis use 'mudaraba' while Shafi'is and Malikis use 'qirad' for the same contract. This is purely terminological.
Knowing both terms helps in reading across madhhab texts.

Chapter 46: Presentation of the most important nominate contracts. 14/46 - (12) Mudaraba: (Majalla articles 1404-1430). It is a type of partnership contract in which it is agreed that capital is from one side, and work for its investment is from the other side, and profit is shared. So the owner of capital is called rabb al-mal, and the worker is called mudarib. The mudaraba contract is also called qirad (with kasra on qaf), it is said: qaradahu, and it is called mudaraba, when one gives money to another to work with it, and profit is shared according to a specific ratio they agree upon. However, the term mudaraba became widespread in the terminology of Hanafi jurists, and the term qirad in the terminology of Shafi'i and Maliki jurists.

This passage defines mudaraba, a key partnership contract in Islamic finance. It is a contract where one party provides the capital (rabb al-mal) and the other provides labor and expertise (mudarib). Profits are shared according to an agreed ratio; losses are borne solely by the capital provider unless due to negligence. The passage also notes the synonymous term qirad used by Shafi'i and Maliki schools. This is a foundational concept, derived from the Zahir al-Riwayah of Imam Muhammad.

Key Terms
المضاربة (al-mudaraba): A contract where capital is provided by one party and labor by the other, profits shared as agreed.
رب المال (rabb al-mal): The provider of capital in a mudaraba.
مضارب (mudarib): The worker/manager of the capital in a mudaraba.

Wakala: The Delegation Contract and the Fuduli Distinction
Defines wakala, distinguishes agent from fuduli, explains ratification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define wakala, distinguish it from fuduli, and explain the Hanafi ruling on retroactive ratification.

Core Idea
A valid agent acts with prior authority; a fuduli's act is void unless ratified retroactively.

Wakala is a foundational contract in Islamic law that allows one person to delegate authority to another. This passage from the Majalla defines wakala, emphasizes the original prohibition against acting in others' affairs without permission, and draws a sharp contrast between a properly appointed agent (wakil) and an unauthorized actor (fuduli). Understanding the retroactive effect of ratification (ijaza) in Hanafi law is crucial for any mufti dealing with contracts formed without prior authorization.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contract and its essence
Recognize that wakala is a delegation contract (عقد تفويض) where one person (muwakkil) appoints another (wakil) to act on his behalf. The essence is the release of authority (إطلاق الصلاحية).
The term 'تفويض' implies entrusting with decision-making.
2
Note the original restriction and its lifting
Every person is originally prevented (محجور) from acting in matters pertaining to others. Without authorization, such action is that of a fuduli (interloper) and is void unless ratified. Wakala lifts this restriction by granting prior authority.
The fuduli's action does not bind the right-holder initially.
3
Distinguish wakil from fuduli
The wakil acts with prior authority; his actions are binding on the muwakkil as if done by the muwakkil himself. The fuduli acts without authority; his actions become effective only if the right-holder subsequently ratifies them, and this ratification retroactively validates the act from its inception.
This is the key point of distinction: prior authorization vs. subsequent ratification.
4
Classify agency scope
Agency can be general (in all matters that admit delegation) or specific (in one or more particular matters). The agent's authority is limited to the scope defined by the principal; beyond that, the agent becomes a fuduli.
General agency is rare; specific agency is more common.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
١٧/٤٦ - (١٥) الوكالة : (المجلة ١٤٤٩ - ١٥٣٠). وهي عقد تفويض ينيب فيه شخص شخصاً آخر عن نفسه في التصرُّف. فالمستنيب : موكل (بصيغة الفاعل والمستناب وكيل ومحل الوكالة، وهو الأمر المستناب فيه : موكل به · وحقيقة التوكيل من قبيل إطلاق الصلاحية؛ فإنَّ كل إنسان في الأصل محجور عن أن يتصرف فيما يعود لغيره، ولو تصرّف كان فضولياً لا ينفذ تصرفه ولا يسري حكمه على صاحب الحق المختص إلا إذا أجازه هذا الأخير. فبالتوكيل يطلق الإنسان لغيره سلطة التصرف عن نفسه لأن الإنسان قد يعجز عن أن يقوم بجميع شؤونه وأعماله بنفسه . فبهذا الإطلاق والصلاحية المستمدة من الموكل يصبح تصرف الوكيل فيما وكل به نافذاً على الموكل، كما لو باشره الموكل بنفسه. وهذه نقطة الفرق بين الوكيل والفضولي. لكن بعد صدور التصرف من الفضولي لو رضيه وأجازه صاحب حق التصرف تكون هذه الإجازة اللاحقة كوكالة سابقة أي يثبت حكمها مستنداً بأثر رجعي) فينفذ عندئذ تصرف الفضولي من تاريخ صدوره كما لو كان حين التصرف (رف (۹/۳۸) وكيلاً . والوكالة قد تكون عامة في كل أمر يقبل الإنابة، فيقوم فيها الوكيل مقام الأصيل في كل تصرف وقد تكون خاصة في أمر أو أمور مخصوصة، من بيع أو شراء أو نكاح أو طلاق أو خصومة أو صلح أو صرف أو قبض إلخ. فتقتصر عندئذ صلاحية الوكيل وتتقيد بما حدده له الموكل ؛ فيكون فيما وراءه فضولياً لأنَّ سلطته مستمدة من موكله .
17/46 - (15) Wakala: (Majalla articles 1449-1530). It is a delegation contract in which a person appoints another person to act on his behalf. So the one who delegates: muwakkil (active participle), and the delegate: wakil, and the subject of the agency, which is the matter delegated: muwakkal bihi. And the reality of appointment is a release of authority; for every person is originally prevented from acting in matters pertaining to others, and if he acts, he is a fuduli (interloper) whose action is not effective and whose ruling does not extend to the right-holder unless the latter ratifies it. So by appointment, a person releases to another the power of acting on his behalf, because a person may be unable to manage all his affairs by himself. So by this release and authority derived from the muwakkil, the agent's actions in the delegated matter become binding on the muwakkil, as if the muwakkil himself had done them. This is the point of difference between the agent and the fuduli. However, after the action of the fuduli, if the right-holder approves and ratifies it, this subsequent ratification is like a prior agency, i.e., its ruling is established retroactively with retrospective effect, so the fuduli's action is then effective from the date it occurred, as if he were an agent at the time of the action. And the agency may be general in every matter that admits delegation, so the agent stands in place of the principal in every action; and it may be specific in one or more particular matters, such as sale, purchase, marriage, divorce, litigation, compromise, currency exchange, receipt, etc. So then the agent's authority is limited and restricted to what the muwakkil specified; so beyond that he is a fuduli because his authority derives from his principal.
Source pages p. 584

Commentary
This passage defines wakala (agency) as a contract of delegation. It distinguishes a valid agent (wakil) from an unauthorized actor (fuduli). The key difference is that an agent acts with prior authority, while a fuduli acts without. However, ratification (ijaza) retroactively validates the fuduli's action, making it equivalent to prior agency. The passage also explains that agency can be general or specific. This distinction is crucial in Hanafi contract law because unauthorized actions are void unless ratified. The authority weight of this ruling comes from the Majalla, which codifies Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
الوكالة (al-wakala): A delegation contract where one person appoints another to act on his behalf.
موكل (muwakkil): The principal who delegates authority.
وكيل (wakil): The agent who receives authority.
فضولي (fuduli): An interloper who acts without authority; his actions are void unless ratified.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The sale becomes valid from the time of ratification
B) The sale becomes valid from the time of the sale (retroactive)
C) The sale remains invalid
D) Only if the buyer rescinds

Key Takeaway
Wakala is delegation; a fuduli acts without authority; ratification retroactively validates.

Sulh (Settlement) Definition
Defines sulh as a contract to resolve disputes over rights

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the components of a sulh contract (musalih, musalah 'anhu, badal al-sulh) and classify a transaction as sulh based on the definition.

Core Idea
Sulh is a contract to end a dispute over a right by mutual agreement.

Sulh (settlement) is a recommended means of dispute resolution in Islam, avoiding costly litigation. In Hanafi fiqh, it is a binding contract with defined parties, subject matter, and consideration. Understanding this definition enables you to distinguish sulh from other transactions and apply it in legal analysis and fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its purpose
The definition states sulh is a contract (عقد) aimed at lifting a dispute (نزاع) over a right (حق). Recognize that the act is a bilateral agreement, not a unilateral action.
The purpose is resolution, not profit.
2
Name the parties
Each party is called a musalih (مصالح). Both are equal in consent and obligation.
There is no 'plaintiff' or 'defendant' in the definition; both are sulh parties.
3
Identify the subject matter
The disputed right is called musalah 'anhu (مصالح عنه). It can be a property or a personal right, but not matters involving hudud or certain prohibited areas.
The right must be in dispute; sulh on an undisputed right may be a gift or sale, not sulh.
4
Identify the consideration
What is given to end the dispute is musalah 'alayhi (مصالح عليه) or badal al-sulh (بدل الصلح). This can be payment, forgiveness of a debt, or any lawful exchange.
Consideration is required for validity in the Hanafi school.
5
Note the scope and exceptions
Sulh is permissible for most civil disputes. It is invalid for hudud and certain rights of Allah (e.g., qisas after execution).
This nuance prevents sulh from being used to circumvent criminal penalties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٤٦ / ١٨ - (١٦) الصلح: (المجلة/ ١٥٣١ - (١٥٦٠). وهو عقد يتفق فيه المتنازعان في حق على ما يرتفع به النزاع بينهما . فكل واحد من عاقدي الصلح مصالح. والحق المتنازع فيه : مُصَالَح عنه. وما يؤديه أحدهما أو يلتزم به لخصمه قطعاً للنزاع : مُصَالَح عليه ؛ ويسمى أيضاً : بدل الصلح.
46/18 - (16) Sulh: (Majalla articles 1531-1560). It is a contract in which two disputants over a right agree on something that lifts the dispute between them. So each of the two parties to the sulh is a musalih. The right in dispute: musalah 'anhu (the subject of settlement). And what one of them pays or commits to the opponent to definitely end the dispute: musalah 'alayhi; it is also called: badal al-sulh (consideration for settlement).
Source pages p. 585

Commentary
This passage defines sulh (settlement) as a contract to end a dispute over a right (haqq). The parties are called musalihin. The disputed right is musalah 'anhu, and the consideration paid to settle is musalah 'alayhi or badal al-sulh. Sulh is a recommended means of dispute resolution in Islam, as it avoids litigation and promotes peace. Hanafi law permits sulh even for rights that are subject to litigation, subject to specific conditions to avoid prohibited compromises (e.g., on hudud punishments).

Key Terms
الصلح (al-sulh): A contract to resolve a dispute over a right through mutual agreement.
مصالح (musalih): A party to a sulh contract.
مصالح عنه (musalah 'anhu): The disputed right or matter in a sulh.
بدل الصلح (badal al-sulh): The consideration given in a sulh to end the dispute.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Musalah 'anhu
B) Badal al-sulh
C) Al-maw'idhah
D) Al-ijarah

Key Takeaway
Sulh is a contract that resolves a dispute through mutual agreement, with distinct roles for the parties (musalih), the disputed right (musalah 'anhu), and the consideration (badal al-sulh).

Tahkim (Arbitration): Definition and Legal Nature
Defines the contract of tahkim and its scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the Hanafi contract of tahkim, identify its parties, and explain the legal effect of the arbitrator's decision.

Core Idea
Tahkim is a consent-based contract; the arbitrator's authority derives from mutual agreement.

In Hanafi fiqh, dispute resolution can take two forms: qada (judicial proceedings) and tahkim (arbitration). While qada is a public function, tahkim is a private contract. Understanding the definition and conditions of tahkim is essential for muftis who may be asked about its validity and binding nature. This lesson unpacks the concise definition from the Majalla.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal category
Recognize that tahkim is a contract ('aqd), not a unilateral appointment or a judicial decree. It is a bilateral agreement between disputing parties.
Contracts in Hanafi fiqh require offer and acceptance, capacity, and lawful subject matter.
2
Identify the parties involved
The parties are: (1) the disputing parties (at least two), and (2) the arbitrator (hakam) who is appointed by them. The definition explicitly allows more than two disputants.
The arbitrator must be a person acceptable to both sides, capable of adjudicating.
3
Note the essential condition: mutual consent
The contract is based on the parties' consent (bi ridahima). Without mutual agreement, the appointment is not valid. This distinguishes tahkim from compulsory court jurisdiction.
Consent must be free and clear; coercion invalidates the contract.
4
State the legal effect
Once appointed, the arbitrator's decision is binding on the parties (laysa li ahadin faskhuhu illa bi ittifaqihim). The arbitrator acts as a judge for that specific dispute.
The binding nature is conditional on the arbitrator not exceeding the scope of appointment and not violating Islamic law.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
19/46 - (۱۷) التحكيم: (المجلة/ ١٨٤١ - ١٨٥١).
وهو عقد بين طرفين متنازعين يجعلان فيه برضاهما شخصاً آخر حاكماً بينهما لفصل خصومتهما. وقد يكون بين أكثر من طرفين.
19/46 - (17) Arbitration: (Majalla/ 1841-1851). It is a contract between two disputing parties in which they, with their consent, make another person a judge between them to settle their dispute. And it may be between more than two parties.
Source pages p. 586

Commentary
This passage defines the contract of tahkim (arbitration). It is a contract between conflicting parties who mutually agree to appoint a third party as an arbitrator to resolve their dispute. The definition notes that it can involve more than two parties, meaning multiple disputants can agree to arbitration. The reference to the Majalla indicates the codified Hanafi position. Arbitration is a recognized method of dispute resolution in Islamic law, distinct from formal qada (judicial litigation) because it is based on the parties' consent and the arbitrator's authority derives from that agreement.

Key Terms
التحكيم (al-taḥkīm): A contract between disputing parties to appoint a third party as an arbitrator to resolve their dispute.
حكم (ḥakam): The arbitrator appointed by the parties.
رضا (riḍā): Consent; the essential condition for the validity of the tahkim contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the essential condition for a valid tahkim contract?
A) The arbitrator must be a government judge
B) The dispute must be about property
C) Mutual consent of the disputing parties
D) The presence of witnesses

Key Takeaway
Tahkim is a consensual contract that gives the arbitrator binding authority over the dispute.

The Adaptive Nature of the Contract of Conciliation (Sulh) in Hanafi Law
Explains the nature of sulh and its legal treatment via a qawida.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi qawida that sulh is considered according to the nearest contract to its form, and identify the conditions under which guardians may validly settle disputes over minors' or waqf property.

Core Idea
Sulh takes the color of whatever contract it most resembles—sale, lease, or release.

The contract of conciliation (sulh) is a unique instrument in Islamic law: it has no fixed subject but adapts to the dispute it resolves. This flexibility, however, comes with strict limits when guardians manage others' rights. In this lesson we unpack the core Hanafi principle that determines the legal nature of any sulh, and explore its practical consequences through concrete examples from the Majalla.

Guided Path
1
Identify the flexibility of sulh
Note that sulh lacks a specific subject matter—it can be used in any dispute. Its essence is mutual concession, usually the claimant giving up part of a claim.
This is what distinguishes sulh from fixed contracts like sale or lease.
2
Apply the guardian restriction
Understand that a wali, wasi, or mutawalli cannot settle for less than full value unless the opponent denies and takes an oath, and there is no evidence. In that case sulh is considered 'profit' (avoiding total loss).
This protects the rights of minors and waqf beneficiaries.
3
State the qawida
The jurists ruled: 'Sulh is considered according to the nearest contract to it by the form in which it occurs.' This is a dabt (control maxim) that governs how we classify a given sulh.
The metaphor: like a clear crystal vessel that takes the color of its contents.
4
Work through the furuat
Apply the qawida to the three examples given: property-for-property → sale; property-for-benefit → lease; part-of-debt-release → combination of receipt and release (ibra).
Each classification triggers the full set of rulings of that contract (e.g., sale conditions, lease duration).
5
Extract the legal consequence (thamarah)
The consequence is that all subsidiary rules of the nearest contract apply to the sulh. Therefore, a sulh classified as a sale requires offer and acceptance, delivery, etc.
This ensures legal coherence and predictability.

وعقد الصلح ليس له موضوع معين يميزه بطبيعة وقابلية خاصة، لأنه يجري في كل نزاع غير أنه يتضمن عادةً تنازل المدعي عن بعض مطلوبه. فلذا لا يقبل من الولي أو الوصي أو المتولي صلح عن حق عائد لصغير أو لقاصر أو لوقف على بدل أقل من قيمة هذه الحقوق، لأنه لا يجوز للقائمين على إدارة أموال القاصرين والأوقاف أن يتبرعوا بشيء من أموال هؤلاء أو يتنازلوا عن شيء من حقوقهم، إلا إذا كان الخصم جاحداً ومستعداً لحلف اليمين وليس لدى الوصي أو الولي أو المتولي بينة يثبت بها الحق المتنازع فيه. فتسوغ لهم إذ ذاك مصالحة الخصم، لأن الصلح ربح. أو إجارة أو غيرهما (ر : المجلة/ ١٥٤٨ - ١٥٦٠).
(۱) انظر ما تقدم في كتاب عمر بن الخطاب إلى أبي موسى الأشعري رضي الله عنهما في القضاء من قوله : الصلح جائز إلا صلحاً أحلّ حراماً أو حرم حلالاً (ر: ف ٦/٣) .
Chapter (46): Presentation of the Most Important Named Contracts. The contract of conciliation does not have a specific subject that distinguishes it by a particular nature and capacity, because it applies to every dispute, but it usually involves the claimant relinquishing part of his claim. Therefore, it is not accepted from a guardian, executor, or trustee to settle a right belonging to a minor, an interdicted person, or a waqf for a consideration less than the value of these rights, because those managing the assets of minors and awqaf are not permitted to donate anything from their assets or relinquish any of their rights, unless the opponent is denying and ready to take an oath, and the executor, guardian, or trustee has no evidence to prove the disputed right. Then it is permissible for them to settle with the opponent, because conciliation is profit (see Majalla articles 1539-1540) at that time. Since the contract of conciliation has no specific subject, the jurists have decided that conciliation is considered according to the nearest contract to it by the form in which it occurs. Thus, conciliation of property for property is considered as a sale; conciliation of property for a benefit is considered as a lease, such as if the subject of dispute is specific property, and they reconcile that one leaves it for the other in return for living in his house for a month for example; and conciliation in a claim of debt where the claimant takes only part of it and abandons his claim is considered as taking part of the right and a release of the remaining right. So conciliation is like a clear white crystal vessel that takes the color of what is in it. The consequence of this is that the rulings of the contract by which it is considered, whether sale, lease, or other, apply to conciliation (see Majalla articles 1548-1560). (1) See what has preceded in the letter of Umar ibn al-Khattab to Abu Musa al-Ash'ari, may God be pleased with them, regarding judiciary, from his saying: Conciliation is permissible except a conciliation that makes lawful what is unlawful or unlawful what is lawful (ref: F 6/3).
Source pages p. 586

Commentary
This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm introduces the contract of conciliation (sulh) and its unique nature. The author begins by noting that sulh has no fixed subject matter, making it flexible across all disputes. However, it typically involves the claimant giving up part of their claim. A key restriction is that guardians, executors, or trustees of minors, interdicted persons, or endowments cannot settle for less than the full value of the right unless the opponent denies and is ready to take an oath, and there is no evidence. This is because they cannot donate others' property; but if they settle in such a situation, it is considered a profit (the settlement avoids a total loss). The core legal principle is that sulh takes the ruling of the nearest contract to its form: if it involves property for property, it is like a sale; property for benefit, like a lease; part of a debt released, like a combination of taking and release. The metaphor of a clear glass vessel taking the color of its contents illustrates this adaptability. The consequence is that all subsidiary rulings of the respective contract apply to the sulh. The footnote references a famous hadith on sulh from the letter of Umar to Abu Musa.

Key Terms
الصلح (al-sulh): The contract of conciliation that ends a dispute by agreement, often involving waiver of part of the claim.
الولي (al-wali): Guardian of a minor or incapacitated person, responsible for managing their property.
الوصي (al-wasi): Executor appointed by the deceased to manage the estate and carry out the will.
المتولي (al-mutawalli): Trustee or manager of a waqf (endowment) property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the legal characterization of a sulh in which the claimant takes part of a debt and abandons the rest?
A) It is treated as a sale of debt.
B) It is treated as a lease of the debt.
C) It is treated as taking part of the right and a release of the remaining right.
D) It is treated as a gift.

Key Takeaway
Sulh in Hanafi fiqh is a flexible contract that takes the ruling of the nearest contract to its form—sale, lease, or release—and guardians may only settle for less under the specific exigency of a denying opponent with no evidence.

The Legal Nature of Arbitration (Tahkim) in Hanafi Fiqh
Defines arbitration as a contract and contrasts it with formal judgeship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define arbitration as a contract, identify its key features (consent, limited authority, revocability before verdict), and distinguish it from formal judgeship in Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
Arbitration is a revocable consent-based contract; the arbitrator's power is limited to those who submitted.

This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Am defines arbitration (tahkim) as a special contract between disputing parties. It clarifies that the arbitrator is not a state judge; his authority derives solely from the parties' agreement. Understanding this distinction is crucial for fatwa practice when parties seek to resolve disputes privately. The key ruling is that the arbitration contract is not binding until a verdict is issued, meaning each party can withdraw at any time beforehand.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal act and its classification
Determine that tahkim is a contract ('aqd) between parties. Note its voluntary nature: it requires mutual consent and appointment of a third person as arbitrator.
The act is a type of 'aqd, not a governmental appointment.
2
Distinguish the arbitrator from the state judge
Compare the two: the judge has general jurisdiction over all people in his district; the arbitrator's authority is limited to those who submitted to him. The judge is appointed by the state; the arbitrator is chosen by the parties.
This distinction determines the scope of enforcement.
3
Determine the binding moment
Identify when the arbitration becomes binding: only after the verdict is issued. Before that, each party may revoke the arbitrator's mandate. The contract is ghayr lazim (non-binding) until then.
This is the key legal consequence discussed in the passage.
4
Identify the scope and limits of the arbitrator's power
The arbitrator's judgment is enforceable only against the parties who consented, and only within the limits they set. Cases involving hudud (fixed punishments) require a state judge in Hanafi fiqh.
The passage does not detail hudud, but it is a known exception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

19/46 - (17) Arbitration: (Majalla/1841-1851). It is a contract between two disputing parties in which they, by mutual consent, make a third person an arbitrator between them to settle their dispute. It may involve more than two parties. So each of the two disputing parties is a 'muhakkam' (one who submits to arbitration), and the person they choose to arbitrate is called 'hakam' (with two fatha) or 'muhakkam' (with shadda on the open kaf). The arbitrator differs from the official judge in important matters, including that the authority and jurisdiction of the judge are general over all people in his district. The authority of the arbitrator is restricted to those who submit to him because originally he is not appointed for judgment by an authority that has general binding power; rather, he is made an arbitrator by the will of those who chose him, so his judgment is only enforced against them and within the limits they set for him (Majalla 1842). And we have stated that arbitration is a non-binding contract before the verdict is issued, so each of the ones submitting to arbitration may revoke the arbitration and dismiss the arbitrator as long as he has not issued his judgment (ref: F 6/40).
Source pages p. 587

Commentary
This passage from al-Madkhal defines arbitration (tahkim) as a voluntary contract between disputing parties who appoint a third party to settle their dispute. It distinguishes the arbitrator from a state-appointed judge: a judge has general jurisdiction over all people in his territory, while an arbitrator's authority is limited to those who consent to his appointment. The key legal point is that the arbitration contract is not binding (ghayr lazim) until the arbitrator issues a verdict. Before that, any party can revoke the arbitrator's mandate. This reflects the Hanafi view that arbitration is based on mutual consent and remains revocable until final judgment. The footnote references further discussion in Fatawa.

Key Terms
التحكيم (al-tahkim): Arbitration: a contract where parties appoint a third person to judge their dispute.
حكم (hakam): The arbitrator appointed by the parties.
محتكم (muhakkam): A party who submits to arbitration.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Only after the verdict is issued
B) At any time before the verdict is issued
C) Only if the arbitrator is biased
D) Never, once the agreement is made

Key Takeaway
Arbitration in Hanafi fiqh is a revocable contract; the arbitrator's authority is limited to consenting parties and only becomes binding upon issuance of verdict.

Mukharaja: The Inheritance Buyout Contract
Defines mukharaja and its two payment scenarios

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define mukharaja, identify its parties (mukhrij and mukharaj), and apply the correct distribution rule when compensation is paid from the estate versus from personal wealth.

Core Idea
Mukharaja lets an heir exit by selling his share; distribution of that share depends on the payment source.

Mukharaja is a practical contract that allows heirs to avoid prolonged joint ownership of inherited property. Understanding its legal structure is essential for a mufti when advising on inheritance buyouts. This lesson parses the definition, identifies the parties, and explains the two main payment scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contract and parties
Read the definition: an heir sells his share and exits; the buyer (mukhrij) pays consideration and steps into the seller's place. The departing heir is the mukharaj.
Mukharaja derives from kharaja (to exit).
2
Determine the payment source
The compensation may come from the estate itself (using estate assets) or from the personal wealth of the co-heirs. This is the key variable.
3
Apply the distribution rule
If from the estate: the departing share is distributed among the paying heirs in proportion to their original shares. If from personal wealth: distributed in proportion to the amount each paid.
Memorize this distinction: estate → ratio of shares; personal → ratio of payments.
4
Recognize the outsider case
If the buyer is a stranger (non-heir), the contract is treated as a sale of the inheritance share, and the stranger steps into the heir's place.
No special distribution; the stranger simply acquires the share.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٢٠/٤٦ - (۱۸) المخارجة أو التخارج :
عقد وهي
يبيع فيه أحد الورثة حِصَّته من تركة مورثه المتوفى فيخرج
منها ويحل محله مشتريها .
يقال: تخارج الورثة إذا خارج بعضهم بعضاً. فدافِعُ المال الذي سيحل في المخارجة محل الوارث في حقه الإرثي هو مُخارج : (بصيغة الفاعل). والوارث الذي أخذ البدل وخرج من التركة : مُخارَج (بصيغة المفعول، أو خارج فإذا كان بدل المخارجة مدفوعاً من التركة نفسها استحق الورثة
المخارجون نصيب المخارج بنسبة سهامهم في التركة . وإذا كان البدل مدفوعاً من أموالهم الأخرى الخاصة يكون نصيب الخارج الإرثي بينهم بنسبة ما دفع كل منهم.
وقد تجري المخارجة بين وارث وأجنبي عن التركة، فتكون بمنزلة بيع الوارث حصته الإرثية لذلك الأجنبي، فيحل هذا الأخير محله في نصيبه الإرثي بين بقية الورثة.
20/46 - (18) Al-Mukharaja or al-Takharuj: A contract by which one of the heirs sells his share from the estate of his deceased predecessor, thus leaving it, and the buyer takes his place. It is said: the heirs have taken part in mukharaja when they mutually buy each other out. The payer of the consideration who will take the place of the heir in his inheritance right is called 'mukhrij' (in active form). And the heir who takes the compensation and leaves the estate is called 'mukharaj' (in passive form) or 'kharj'. If the compensation for mukharaja is paid from the estate itself, the heirs who pay (al-mukharajun) are entitled to the departing heir's share proportionate to their own shares in the estate. If the compensation is paid from their own private wealth, then the departing heir's share is divided among them in proportion to what each paid. And mukharaja may also occur between an heir and a stranger to the estate; in that case it is considered like a sale of the heir's share to that stranger, so the latter takes his place in the inheritance share among the remaining heirs.
Source pages p. 587

Commentary
This passage defines the contract of mukharaja (or takharuj), a mechanism for buying out an inheritance share. The term 'mukharaja' literally means to cause one to exit. The contract involves one heir selling his share to others (co-heirs or an outsider) and thereby leaving the estate. The buyer (mukhrij) pays consideration and steps into the seller's place with respect to the inheritance rights. The text distinguishes two scenarios: if the compensation is paid from the estate itself (i.e., the co-heirs use estate assets to buy out the share), then the share is distributed among the remaining heirs according to their original shares. If the compensation is paid from their personal wealth, then the outgoing heir's share is divided among the buyers in proportion to what each paid. The passage also notes that mukharaja can occur with a non-heir (ajnabi), which is treated as a sale of the inheritance share, and the outsider steps into the heir's place among the remaining heirs.

Key Terms
المخارجة (al-mukharaja): An inheritance buyout contract where an heir sells his share to others.
مُخارِج (mukhrij): The party who pays the consideration and buys out the heir's share.
مُخارَج (mukharaj): The heir who receives compensation and exits the estate.
الأجنبي (al-ajnabi): A stranger to the estate, i.e., a non-heir.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Equally, regardless of how much each paid
B) In proportion to what each paid
C) According to their original inheritance shares
D) The elder heir takes it all

Key Takeaway
Mukharaja is a permissible contract for buying out an inheritance share; the distribution of the departing share depends on whether the compensation is paid from the estate (proportional to original shares) or from personal wealth (proportional to amounts paid).

Types of Guardianship: Chosen and Court-Appointed Executors
Describing two types of guardianship and their legal distinctions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the chosen executor (al-wasi al-mukhtar) and the judge-appointed executor (wasi al-qadi) in Hanafi law, and state the legal consequences regarding resignation, dismissal, and scope of authority.

Core Idea
A chosen executor cannot be dismissed without cause; a court-appointed executor has narrower powers.

In Hanafi law, guardianship over a bequest or minors' property can arise either by the deceased's own appointment or by the judge's nomination. This passage clarifies the distinction between the two types, emphasizing the contractual nature of the chosen executor's appointment versus the administrative nature of the judge's appointee. Understanding this difference is crucial for anyone involved in drafting wills or managing estates in an Islamic legal context.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of executors
From the passage, identify the chosen executor (al-wasi al-mukhtar) – appointed by the testator during his lifetime by contract – and the judge-appointed executor (wasi al-qadi) – appointed by the judge when no executor is named.
The chosen executor's appointment is a contract; the judge's appointment is an administrative act.
2
Compare appointment and dismissal rules
The chosen executor cannot resign after acceptance, nor can the judge dismiss him unless he is incapable, dishonest, or negligent. The judge-appointed executor serves at the judge's discretion and can be removed more easily.
The chosen executor's authority derives from the deceased, not the judge.
3
Analyze the scope of authority
The passage states that the chosen executor has broader authority than the judge-appointed executor. Think about why: the chosen executor is trusted by the deceased, while the judge's appointment is a default measure with limited powers.
Court-appointed executors often require judicial approval for major decisions.
4
Note the historical and modern context
The passage references the Ottoman-era Orphans Council and modern personal status laws. Recognize that these historical structures aimed to protect minors' property, and modern codifications (e.g., Egyptian and Syrian laws) build on Hanafi principles.
These laws restrict executor powers and impose legal procedures.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل (٤٦) عرض لأهم العقود المسماة
وليس له أن يعزل نفسه عن الوصاية متى قبلها بعد وفاة الموصي، ولا للقاضي أن يعزله إذا كان كفءاً أميناً، لأنه منصوب بإرادة الميت لا بإرادة القاضي (ر: الدر المختار أول باب الوصي). ولكنه إذا كان عاجزاً، أو خان في عمله المؤتمن عليه أو قصر، يعزله القاضي عزلاً، ويضمنه ما خان فيه أو قصر من حقوق القاصرين أو الوصية.
وهذا الوصي الذي اختاره المتوفى في حياته يسمى: الوصي المختار، وهو الذي يكون العهد إليه من الموصي عقداً كما بيناه.
فإذا لم يعهد الإنسان في حياته بوصاية إلى أحد، وترك وصية مالية أو أولاداً ينصب القاضي بمقتضى صلاحيته العامة وصياً لتنفيذ الوصية المالية، وإدارة أموال الأولاد فيسمى عندئذ وصي القاضي، ويكون نصبه أعمال القضاء الإدارية لا عقداً بالمعنى الاصطلاحي في هذا المقام.
ويفترق الوصي المختار عن وصي القاضي بأحكام تتسع فيها صلاحية الأول عن الثاني.
ولإدارة أموال الأيتام نظام خاص في بلادنا منذ العهد العثماني قيدت به صلاحيات الأوصياء، وألزموهم فيه بطرائق قانونية تحت إشراف مجلس يسمى : مجلس الأيتام مرتبط بالمحكمة الشرعية وذلك حفظاً لحقوق الأيتام التلاعب. ثم صدر لدينا قانون الأحوال الشخصية سنة / ١٩٥٣ م/ من فنظم هذه الأحكام من جديد.
وترى في كتاب الأحكام الشرعية في الأحوال الشخصية للمرحوم قدري باشا المصري أحكام الوصاية وصلاحيات الأوصياء ومسؤولياتهم مفصَّلة في المواد / ٤٣٥ - ٤٨١ منه ؛ وأحكام الوصية في المواد / ٥٣٠ - ٥٥٨/ ؛ وأحكام الزواج وما يتفرّع عنه من طلاق ونسب ونفقة وولاية وغيرها في المواد / ١ - ٤٣٤/ .
الباب السادس : نظرية العقود وما إليها في الفقه
وكذا في المواد / ١٧٦ - ١٩٩ من قانون الأحوال الشخصية السوري، كما ترى في أبوابها الخاصة من سائر الكتب الفقهية الأخرى. هذه صورة خاطفة تصوّر للطالب حقيقة كل عقد وموضوعه ومميزاته . وهذه الخلاصات، إذا كانت بالنسبة إلى العلماء الحقوقيين تعد من البديهيات، فإنها بالنسبة إلى طالب الحقوق في بدء دراسته تعد ضرورية نظراً للغاية التعليمية التي يهدف إليها هذا الكتاب. فهذه العقود ستمر أسماؤها أمام الطالب في شتى المناسبات والأمثلة قبل أن يصل إلى أبوابها الخاصة، فيجب تزويده بفكرة إجمالية ومعلومات أساسية عن كل منها.
Chapter 46: Presentation of the Most Important Nominate Contracts. He (the executor) may not resign from guardianship once he accepts it after the death of the testator, nor may the judge dismiss him if he is competent and trustworthy, because he is appointed by the will of the deceased, not by the will of the judge (see: al-Durr al-Mukhtar, beginning of the chapter on the executor). But if he is incapable, or betrays the trust, or is negligent, the judge dismisses him and holds him liable for what he betrayed or neglected regarding the rights of minors or the estate. This executor chosen by the deceased during his lifetime is called: the chosen executor, and the appointment by the testator is a contract as we explained. If a person does not appoint an executor during his lifetime, and leaves a financial bequest or children, the judge, by virtue of his general jurisdiction, appoints an executor to execute the financial bequest and manage the children's property, who is then called the judge’s executor, and his appointment is an administrative act of the judiciary, not a contract in the technical sense in this context. The chosen executor and the judge’s executor differ in rulings, with the former having broader authority than the latter. The management of orphan funds has a special system in our countries since the Ottoman era, which restricted the powers of executors and obligated them to follow legal procedures under the supervision of a council called the Orphans Council, affiliated with the Sharia court, to protect the orphans' rights from manipulation. Then our Personal Status Law of 1953 AD was issued, organizing these provisions anew. You will find in the book 'al-Ahkam al-Shar'iyya fi al-Ahwal al-Shakhsiyya' by the late Qadri Pasha al-Misri, the provisions of guardianship, powers, and responsibilities of executors detailed in articles 435-481; provisions of bequests in articles 530-558; provisions of marriage and its branches (divorce, lineage, maintenance, custody, etc.) in articles 1-434. Likewise in articles 176-199 of the Syrian Personal Status Law, as you see in their specific sections from other fiqh books. This is a quick overview that gives the student a true picture of each contract, its subject, and its characteristics. These summaries, although considered self-evident by legal scholars, are necessary for a law student at the beginning of his studies due to the educational purpose of this book. The names of these contracts will appear to the student in various contexts and examples before he reaches their specific sections, so he must be provided with a general idea and basic information about each.
Source pages p. 596

Commentary
This is an introductory passage from a textbook on fiqh that explains the two types of guardianship (wasaya) recognized in Hanafi law. The first is the 'chosen executor' (al-wasi al-mukhtar), appointed by the testator during his lifetime through a contract. Once appointed and after the testator's death, this executor cannot unilaterally resign, nor can the judge dismiss him as long as he remains competent and trustworthy, because his authority derives from the deceased's will. However, if the executor becomes incapable, dishonest, or negligent, the judge may dismiss him and hold him financially liable. The second type is the 'judge's executor' (wasi al-qadi), appointed by the judge when someone dies without naming an executor but leaves a bequest or minor children. This appointment is an administrative act, not a contract. The key difference is that the chosen executor has broader powers than the court-appointed one. The passage also mentions the historical Ottoman-era system with an Orphans Council to oversee executors, and references modern codifications in Egyptian and Syrian personal status laws. This material is foundational for understanding the classification of contracts and fiduciary relationships in Islamic law.

Key Terms
الوصي المختار (al-wasi al-mukhtar): The executor appointed by the testator during his lifetime by a contract, who cannot be dismissed without cause.
وصي القاضي (wasi al-qadi): The executor appointed by the judge when no executor was named, to manage a bequest or minors' property; appointment is an administrative act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The chosen executor can resign at any time.
B) The chosen executor has broader authority than the judge-appointed executor.
C) The judge-appointed executor is appointed by a contract.
D) The chosen executor is appointed after the testator's death.

Key Takeaway
The chosen executor derives authority from the deceased and is protected from arbitrary dismissal, while the court-appointed executor serves at the judge's discretion.
