Classification of Contracts by Enforceability: Nafidh vs Mawquf
Classifies contracts by enforceability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between enforceable (nafidh) and suspended (mawquf) contracts, identify the elements that cause suspension, and apply this classification to fatwa scenarios.

Core Idea
A contract is enforceable immediately or suspended by a third-party right or impediment — validity depends on ratification.

In Hanafi fiqh, enforceability is distinct from validity. A valid contract may be unenforceable due to a third-party interest or a defect like duress. This lesson parses the two categories — nafidh and mawquf — and explains how suspension is lifted through ratification (ijazah).

Guided Path
1
Distinguish formation from enforceability
Recognize that a contract can be valid (sahih) in its formation yet suspended in its effects. Enforceability is a separate condition.
Formation is about consent and elements; enforceability is about freedom from external hooks.
2
Define enforceable contract (عقد نافذ)
A nafidh contract is free from any right of a third party that would suspend it and from any impediment preventing its immediate effect. It is immediately binding.
Think of a standard sale between two capable parties.
3
Define suspended contract (عقد موقوف)
A mawquf contract involves a transgression on another's right (e.g., fuduli sale) or an impediment like duress. It is valid but its enforceability depends on ratification by the affected party or removal of the impediment.
It is not void; it is pending.
4
Understand the mechanism of suspension lifting
Ratification (ijazah) by the rightful party or elimination of the impediment (e.g., coerced party's post-agreement consent) transforms the mawquf contract into nafidh.
Ratification can be explicit or implicit (e.g., taking possession).
5
Apply to a fatwa scenario
Given a query about a fuduli contract, the mufti must first classify it as mawquf, then check if ratification occurred, and rule accordingly.
The mufti should never declare such contracts invalid outright.

Source Text

Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Fiqh. Ownership, mortgage, or easement, etc. ... because the legal rulings require registration in the land registry: either for their formation, or for their effects to take place and to be binding against third parties, according to the direction of codification from these measures as previously explained (see: F 5/31). 2 - Consensual non-formal contracts, which are not subject in their formation except to mere mutual consent, like most contracts (1). Commentary
This passage classifies contracts from the perspective of enforceability (nafadh). It first discusses formality, noting that Islamic law generally favors consensual (non-formal) contracts, unlike positive laws which often require registration. Then it presents two categories: enforceable contracts (عقود نافذة) that are free from third-party rights or impediments, and suspended contracts (عقود موقوفة) that require the consent of a third party (e.g., fuduli contracts) or removal of an impediment (e.g., coercion). The passage concludes by reiterating that the suspended contract depends on ratification.

Key Terms
عقد نافذ (‘aqd nāfidh): A contract free from any third-party right that suspends it and from any impediment preventing its enforceability.
عقد موقوف (‘aqd mawqūf): A contract that involves a transgression upon the right of another, thus suspended on his permission, or has an impediment like duress.
عقد رضائي (‘aqd riḍā’ī): A contract that is concluded by mere mutual consent without any formalities.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a contract may be valid but unenforceable (mawquf) until the right of a third party or impediment is removed through ratification or elimination of the defect.

The Five Fixed Punishments (Hudud): Overview and Penalties
Introduces the five fixed corporal punishments (hudud) with their prescribed penalties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state each of the five hudud punishments, identify their textual source (Quran or Sunnah), and explain the conditions for their application (e.g., ihsan for zina).

Core Idea
Hudud are divinely prescribed deterrent punishments that cannot be reduced; they include lashing, amputation, and death.

This passage introduces the five fixed punishments (hudud) in Islamic law, detailing their penalties and foundational texts. As a mufti, understanding the precise rulings and conditions of each hadd is essential for correct fatwa issuance. This lesson provides the key points for memorization and application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five hudud crimes
List the five crimes mentioned by the author: zina (adultery/fornication), qadhf (false accusation of zina), theft, hirabah (highway robbery), and drinking wine.
The first four are Quranic; the fifth is from Sunnah.
2
Memorize each prescribed penalty
For each crime, note the specific corporal punishment as detailed: qadhf and drinking wine: 80 lashes; zina: 100 lashes (non-muhsan) or stoning (muhsan); theft: cutting off hand; hirabah: death, crucifixion, cross-limb amputation, or exile depending on circumstances.
The penalty for zina varies based on ihsan (marital status).
3
Identify the textual source for each hadd
Confirm that four hudud are from the Quran (e.g., hirabah verse from Surah al-Ma'idah 5:33-34) and the punishment for drinking wine is from the Sunnah.
The passage explicitly states this distinction.
4
Understand conditions for application
For zina, note the conditions for ihsan (free, sane, adult, married with consummation, both spouses free). For qadhf, note that the accused must be chaste. For theft, nisab is implied. For hirabah, the punishment may vary based on whether killing occurred.
These conditions are crucial for enforcement and prevent arbitrary punishment.
5
Recognize the flexibility in hirabah punishment
The passage provides two interpretations of the hirabah verse: imam's discretion or distribution by case. The prevailing view assigns a specific penalty to each scenario (e.g., killing alone → execution; killing + theft → crucifixion; theft alone → cross-limb amputation).
This flexibility serves the purpose of deterrence and justice.

البحث الأول : الحدود (۱)
٢/٤ - فالحدود خمس عقوبات؛ وهي حد الزني، وحد القذف (۲)، وحد السرقة، وحد الحرابة (۳)، وحد شرب الخمر.
والحدود الأربعة الأولى جاء بها القرآن أما حد شرب الخمر فلم يذكر في القرآن وإنما جاءت به السنة.

الباب السابع: نظرية المؤيدات الشرعية في الفقه
ب - وحد الزنى مائة جلدة، وهو ما ورد في القرآن. وقد ورد في السنة أيضاً رجم الزاني بالحجارة إذا كان محصناً حتى الموت. والمقصود الأساسي بالإحصان هنا أن يكون الشخص متزوجاً مع الدخول.

فقيل في تفسيرها يتخير الإمام بين هذه العقوبات الأربع في حق قاطع الطريق، كما يفيده ظاهر الآية، مع ملاحظة أن الإمام يجب عليه أن يختار ما تقتضيه مصلحة القمع والزجر، فلا يتساهل باختيار أدنى العقوبة لأشدّ حالات الجريمة. وهذا تفسير يفسح مجالاً لمراعاة ظروف الحال في تطبيق العقوبة. ولكن الرأي الفقهي السائد أنَّ هذا التفصيل موزّع على حالات:
- فالقتل هو لقاطع الطريق إذا كان قاتلاً.
- والتصليب مع القتل هو لمن قتل وسلب المال.
- وقطع اليد والرجل من خلاف (أي اليد اليمنى والرجل اليسرى) هو لمن أخذ مالاً يعادل نِصاب السرقة دون أن يرتكب قتلاً.
Chapter Forty-Nine
Disciplinary Sanctions: Fixed Punishments
1/49 - Indeed, the fixed textual punishments in Islamic law are two types: hudud and qisas.
First Research: Hudud (1)
2/49 - So hudud are five punishments: the punishment for zina, the punishment for qadhf (2), the punishment for theft, the punishment for hirabah (3), and the punishment for drinking wine.
The first four hudud are mentioned in the Quran, but the punishment for drinking wine is not mentioned in the Quran; rather it is established by the Sunnah.
And all these hudud are bodily punishments, the least of which is flogging with a whip, and the greatest of which is death:
A - The punishment for qadhf and the punishment for drinking wine are eighty lashes distributed over parts of the body except the vital areas, with a medium whip.
(1) Hudud: plural of 'hadd' (with fathah on the ha and shaddah on the dal). Linguistically, it is a verbal noun meaning prevention. It also comes as a noun meaning a barrier that prevents. These punishments are legally called hudud because they prevent people from the crimes that necessitate them.
(2) Qadhf linguistically means throwing. In legal terminology: it is that a person accuses another of zina: such as saying 'O fornicator' or 'O fornicatress' or 'You are not your father's son', for this last statement is qadhf against the mother of the addressee.
(3) Hirabah (with kasrah on the ha) is cutting the road for plunder, looting, and killing.
[PAGE_635]
[PAGE_636]
Chapter Seven: The Theory of Legal Sanctions in Jurisprudence
B - The punishment for zina is one hundred lashes, as stated in the Quran. It has also been stated in the Sunnah that the fornicator is stoned with stones if he is muhsan until death. The basic meaning of ihsan here is that the person is married with consummation.
And the conditions for a person to be muhsan through marriage are that he be free, sane, adult, and that the other spouse also be free.
C - The punishment for theft is cutting off the hand at the wrist.
D - The punishment for hirabah is what the Great Quran detailed in Surat al-Ma'idah (5:33-34) with His saying: 'Indeed, the penalty for those who wage war against Allah and His Messenger and strive upon earth [to cause] corruption is only that they be killed or crucified or that their hands and feet be cut off from opposite sides or that they be exiled from the land. That is for them a disgrace in this world; and for them in the Hereafter is a great punishment, except for those who repent before you apprehend them. And know that Allah is Forgiving and Merciful.' (At-Tawbah? Actually 5:33-34).
So it is said in its interpretation that the imam may choose among these four punishments for the highway robber, as the apparent meaning of the verse indicates, with the note that the imam must choose what the interest of suppression and deterrence requires, and he should not be lenient in choosing the lowest punishment for the most severe cases of crime. This interpretation opens room for considering circumstances in applying the punishment. But the prevailing juristic opinion is that this detail is distributed according to cases:
- Killing is for the highway robber if he kills.
- Crucifixion with killing is for whoever kills and takes wealth.
- Cutting off the hand and foot from opposite sides (i.e., right hand and left foot) is for whoever takes wealth equivalent to the nisab of theft without committing murder.
Source pages p. 635

Commentary
This passage is an exposition on the fixed punishments (hudud) in Islamic law, which are one of the two categories of fixed textual punishments alongside qisas. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, first defines hudud linguistically and technically. Linguistically, hadd means prevention or barrier; technically, these punishments are called hudud because they prevent people from committing the prescribed crimes. The hudud are five: punishment for adultery (zina), false accusation of adultery (qadhf), theft (sariqa), highway robbery (hirabah), and drinking wine (shurb al-khamr). The first four are Qur'anic; the last is from the Sunnah.

The author then details each punishment:
- Qadhf (false accusation) and drinking wine: 80 lashes, distributed over the body avoiding vital spots, with a medium whip.
- Zina: 100 lashes for non-muhsan; stoning to death for muhsan (married and consummated). Conditions for ihsan include being free, sane, adult, and having a free spouse.
- Theft: cutting off the hand at the wrist (subject to nisab conditions, though not detailed here).
- Hirabah: the verse from Surah al-Ma'idah gives four options: execution, crucifixion, cross-limb amputation, or exile. The author mentions two interpretations: either the imam chooses freely based on deterrence, or the punishments are assigned to specific crime scenarios (killing alone, killing plus theft, theft without killing). The latter is the prevailing juristic view.

Note: This passage is a general overview and does not delve into Hanafi-specific details such as the differentiation between fard and wajib in hudud or the strict conditions for proof. As a madkhal text, it presents the mainstream Sunni view without extensive ikhtilaf.

Key Terms
الحدود (al-hudud): Fixed corporal punishments prescribed by the Quran and Sunnah for specific major sins; linguistically 'prevention' and legally 'deterrent penalties'.
القذف (al-qadhf): The act of accusing a chaste person of zina; legally it is a specific verbal accusation, e.g., saying 'You are an adulterer' or implying it through lineage denial.
الحرابة (al-hirabah): Highway robbery involving violence or threat of violence; also known as qat' al-tariq.
الإحصان (al-ihsan): Legal condition for stoning: being married with consummation, free, sane, adult, and having a free spouse.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the hadd punishment for qadhf (false accusation of zina) according to this passage?
A) 80 lashes with a medium whip, distributed over the body avoiding vital spots.
B) 100 lashes followed by stoning.
C) Cutting off the hand at the wrist.
D) Execution or crucifixion.

Key Takeaway
The five hudud are fixed corporal punishments from Quran and Sunnah, with specific penalties and conditions; their application serves as a deterrent (hadd = prevention).

The Hanafi Distributive Interpretation of Hiraba Punishments
Describes the fixed hadd punishments and their conditions with Hanafi interpretation of hiraba

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi distributive interpretation of the four punishments for hiraba in Quran 5:33 and apply it to a fatwa case by matching the crime to the prescribed punishment.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school assigns specific hiraba punishments based on the crime, balancing deterrence with justice.

In this lesson, we examine a foundational rule for the mufti: the classification of the four Quranic punishments for hiraba (highway robbery) according to the Hanafi school. The passage from al-Zarqa clarifies that while the apparent reading of the verse (5:33) suggests the imam's discretion, the prevailing Hanafi view distributes the punishments proportionally to the crime. Understanding this distributive rule is essential for issuing correct fatwas in criminal cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three hudud crimes
The passage mentions three fixed punishments: zina (100 lashes or stoning for muhsan), theft (amputation), and hiraba (four options). Focus on hiraba, as it is the main subject of the dispute.
Note the conditions for ihsan (marriage with consummation).
2
Extract the four punishments from the verse
From Quran 5:33, list the four punishments: execution, crucifixion, cross-limb amputation (right hand and left foot), and exile (interpreted as imprisonment by Hanafis).
The verse uses 'aw' (or), which can imply either choice or distribution.
3
Contrast the two interpretations
One interpretation (apparent meaning) gives the imam full discretion; the other (Hanafi) distributes punishments based on the crime's severity. The Hanafi view aligns with proportionality.
The passage labels the Hanafi view as 'al-ra'y al-fiqhi al-sa'id' (prevailing juristic opinion).
4
Memorize the Hanafi distribution scheme
Execution for murder only; crucifixion for murder + theft; cross-limb amputation for theft only (if nisab reached); exile/imprisonment for capture before any killing or theft.
Cross-limb amputation requires other limbs to be sound to avoid total disability.
5
Apply the rule in a fatwa scenario
Given a specific case (e.g., robber killed and stole), determine which punishment applies according to Hanafi school and justify using the distributive rule.
Repentance before capture drops the hadd but not qisas or property rights.

ب - وحد الزنى مائة جلدة، وهو ما ورد في القرآن. وقد ورد في السنة أيضاً رجم الزاني بالحجارة إذا كان محصناً حتى الموت. والمقصود الأساسي بالإحصان هنا أن يكون الشخص متزوجاً مع الدخول. ومما يشترط ليكون الشخص محصناً بالزواج أن يكون حراً عاقلاً بالغاً، وأن يكون الزوج الآخر أيضاً حراً. ج - وحد السرقة قطع اليد من الرسغ. د - وحد الحرابة هو ما فصله القرآن العظيم في سورة المائدة (٣٣/٥ - ٣٤) بقوله : إنما جزاوا الَّذِينَ يُحَارِبُونَ اللهَ وَرَسُولَهُ وَيَسْعَوْنَ فِي الْأَرْضِ فَسَادًا أَن يُقَتَّلُوا أَوْ يُصَلَبُوا أَوْ تُقَطَّعَ أَيْدِيهِمْ وَأَرْجُلُهُم مِّنْ خِلَافٍ أَوْ يُنفَوْا مِن الْأَرْضِ ذَالِكَ لَهُمْ خِزْيٌ فِي الدُّنْيَا وَلَهُمْ فِي الْآخِرَةِ عَذَابٌ عَظِيمُ إِلَّا الَّذِينَ تَابُوا مِن قَبْلِ أَن تَقْدِرُوا عَلَيْهِمْ فَاعْلَمُوا أَنَّ اللَّهَ غَفُورٌ رَّحِيمٌ الله التوبة : ٣٣ - ٣٤) . فقيل في تفسيرها يتخير الإمام بين هذه العقوبات الأربع في حق قاطع الطريق، كما يفيده ظاهر الآية، مع ملاحظة أن الإمام يجب عليه أن يختار ما تقتضيه مصلحة القمع والزجر، فلا يتساهل باختيار أدنى العقوبة لأشدّ حالات الجريمة. وهذا تفسير يفسح مجالاً لمراعاة ظروف الحال في تطبيق العقوبة. ولكن الرأي الفقهي السائد أنَّ هذا التفصيل موزّع على حالات: - فالقتل هو لقاطع الطريق إذا كان قاتلاً. - والتصليب مع القتل هو لمن قتل وسلب المال. وقطع اليد والرجل من خلاف أي) اليد اليمنى والرجل اليسرى) هو لمن أخذ مالاً يعادل نِصاب السرقة دون أن يرتكب قتلاً . ويُشترط للقطع أن تكون أطرافه الأخرى سليمة كي لا يبقى عديم المنفعة والنفي لمن قُبض عليه قبل أن يباشر قتلاً أو سلباً. وهذا هو الاجتهاد الحنفي أيضاً، وقد فسروا هنا النفي من الأرض بالحبس، باعتبار أنه إبعاد عن الحياة في البيئة الاجتماعية. (ر) أحكام القرآن لأبي بكر الرازي الجصاص وتفسير الفخر الرازي). وتوبة قاطع الطريق قبل القبض عليه تُسقط عنه الحد. ولكن إذا كان يجري عليه حكم القصاص الآتي بيانه وإن كان سالباً يجب عليه رد المال المسلوب أو ضمانه . قاتلاً وهناك بعض شرائط تفصيلية تُعرف بالرجوع إلى ما قرره الفقهاء في باب قطع الطريق ( ر ) رد المحتار ٢١٢/٣ - ٢١٥).
Chapter Seven: Theory of Legal Sanctions in Fiqh. B - The hadd for zina is one hundred lashes, as mentioned in the Quran. Also mentioned in the Sunnah is stoning the adulterer with stones until death if he is muhsan. The basic meaning of ihsan here is that the person is married with consummation. Conditions for a person to be muhsan by marriage: he must be free, sane, adult, and the other spouse must also be free. C - The hadd for theft is amputation of the hand from the wrist. D - The hadd for hiraba (highway robbery) is detailed by the Great Quran in Surah al-Ma'idah (5:33-34): 'Indeed, the penalty for those who wage war against Allah and His Messenger and strive upon earth corruption is that they be killed or crucified or that their hands and feet be cut off from opposite sides or that they be exiled from the land...' It was said in its interpretation that the imam chooses among these four punishments for the highway robber, as indicated by the apparent meaning of the verse, noting that the imam must choose what the interest of repression and deterrence requires, so he should not be lenient by choosing the lowest punishment for the most severe cases of crime. This interpretation provides leeway to consider circumstances in applying punishment. But the prevailing juristic opinion is that this detail is distributed according to cases: - Execution is for the highway robber if he killed. - Crucifixion with execution is for one who killed and took property. - Cutting off the hand and foot from opposite sides (i.e., right hand and left foot) is for one who took property equivalent to the nisab of theft without committing murder. - It is required for amputation that his other limbs be sound so that he does not become disabled. - Exile is for one who was captured before he committed murder or theft. This is also the Hanafi ruling, and they interpreted 'exile from the land' here as imprisonment, considering it removal from life in the social environment. (Ref: Ahkam al-Quran by Abu Bakr al-Razi al-Jassas and Tafsir al-Fakhr al-Razi). The repentance of the highway robber before capture drops the hadd. But if he is subject to the ruling of qisas explained later, and if he was a robber, he must return the stolen property or guarantee it. There are some detailed conditions known by referring to what the jurists established in the chapter on highway robbery (Ref: Radd al-Muhtar 3/212-215).
Source pages p. 636

Commentary
This passage explains the fixed punishments (hudud) for three major crimes: zina, theft, and hiraba. For zina, the hadd is one hundred lashes for unmarried offenders, and stoning for married (muhsan) offenders, with ihsan requiring a free, sane, adult person in a consummated marriage. For theft, the hadd is amputation of the right hand at the wrist. For hiraba (highway robbery), the Quran (5:33-34) mentions four punishments: execution, crucifixion, cross-limb amputation (right hand and left foot), and exile. The Hanafi school adopts a distributive interpretation: execution for murder alone; crucifixion for murder with theft; cross-limb amputation for theft only (if nisab reached); and exile (interpreted as imprisonment) for those captured before killing or stealing. Repentance before capture does not drop the hadd for hiraba if other rights (qisas, property) remain. The passage emphasizes the importance of deterrence and the imam's duty to choose the appropriate punishment.

Key Terms
حد (hadd): A fixed punishment prescribed by divine law (Quran or Sunnah) that cannot be increased, decreased, or pardoned by human authority.
إحصان (ihsan): Legal state of being married with consummation, requiring the person to be free, sane, adult, and the spouse also free.
حرابة (hiraba): Highway robbery or armed banditry that threatens public security, punishable by one of four hadd punishments.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Cross-limb amputation
C) Crucifixion with execution

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi distributive rule for hiraba is an essential tool for the mufti, ensuring that the punishment matches the crime's severity, upholding justice and deterrence.

The Legal Institution of Qisas: Definition, Wisdom, Types, and Conditions
Exposition on the concept and wisdom of qisas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition, identify the two types, and list the three conditions of qisas, and explain its social wisdom as presented in the Quran.

Core Idea
Qisas is a divinely mandated equivalent punishment that deters crime and preserves social order.

This lesson unpacks the Islamic penal institution of qisas as outlined by Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa. You will explore its linguistic and legal meanings, the Quranic basis, and the profound social wisdom that makes qisas a life-preserving measure. Understanding these fundamentals is essential for any serious study of Hanafi criminal law.

Guided Path
1
Define the term
Start with the linguistic origin of 'qisas' (from 'iqtisas al-athar' – following a trace) and its synonym 'qawad'. Then give the legal definition: punishing the offender with an equivalent for intentional murder or injury.
The shift from linguistic to legal meaning shows how the term became specialized.
2
Identify the Quranic proof
Find the Quranic verses cited (2:178-179). Note that verse 178 prescribes qisas for killing, and verse 179 states the wisdom: 'And for you in qisas is life.'
The imperative 'kutiba' (prescribed) indicates obligation in Hanafi usul.
3
Extract the wisdom
Analyze the author's explanation: qisas deters the aggressor by making him fear equivalent punishment, thus preventing private vengeance and social chaos. This is supported by the rebuttal of modern theories that reject retaliation.
The key phrase is 'wa lakum fi al-qisasi hayatun' – qisas is life, not death.
4
Distinguish the two types
From the definition, you can derive two types: qisas for life (in murder) and qisas for organs (in amputation and wounding).
The same principles apply but with different practical rules.
5
List the three conditions
The passage states three general conditions for qisas: transgression ('udwan), intent ('amd), and possibility of equivalence (imkan al-mumathala). Memorize these.
These conditions ensure qisas is not applied unjustly.

والقصاص في لسان الشرع هو :
معاقبة الجاني على جريمة القتل أو القطع أو الجرح عمداً بمثلها . وقد جاء القرآن العظيم بشريعة القصاص في النفوس(٢) بقوله تعالى : يا أيها الذين امنوا كتب عليكم القصاص في القتلى [سورة البقرة ٢/ ۱۷۸] . ثم بين حكمته وثمرته الاجتماعية بقوله : ﴿ وَلَكُمْ فِي الْقِصَاصِ حَيَاةٌ يَا أُولِي الْأَلْبَابِ لَعَلَّكُمْ تَتَّقُونَ ﴾ [سورة البقرة ١٧٩/٢].

فمن فُقِئَت عينه أو قطعت يده عمداً وعدواناً من معتد جان أثيم كيف يطيق أن يرى الجاني يحملق بعينين، أو يتمتع بيدين !! .

(1) ومن هنا يسمى القصاص امتثالاً، فيقال في اللغة امثل الحاكم من فلان لفلان إذا : اقتص له منه .
Chapter (49) Disciplinary Sanctions: Prescribed Penalties. Second Topic: Qisas. 8/49 - Qisas (with kasr of qaf) linguistically derived from 'iqtisas al-athar' meaning following and tracing it. Then it came to be predominantly used for killing the killer because it involves tracing the blood trail through punishment. It is also called 'qawad' (with two fathas) because it was customary to lead the killer with a rope or similar to the place of qisas. Qisas in legal terminology is: punishing the offender for the crime of murder, amputation, or wounding intentionally with an equivalent. The Great Quran came with the law of qisas for lives: 'O you who believe, prescribed for you is qisas regarding the slain' [Surah al-Baqarah 2:178]. Then it stated its wisdom and social benefit: 'And for you in qisas is life, O people of understanding, that you may become righteous' [Surah al-Baqarah 2:179]. So it indicated that the growth of good social life through preservation of blood and protection of lives and bodies from aggression depends on qisas, which achieves complete justice and protects the human structure from being taken lightly in aggression, so that the aggressor against another's life or body knows that he ultimately attacks his own self in return through qisas. Then he becomes wary of committing this destructive crime out of concern for himself. And ongoing reality shows that all penalties other than this severe crime, such as imprisonment or fine or others, only facilitate its commission because the criminal is confident that he will ultimately remain safe and secure, and they also leave bitterness in souls, prompting the victim's family to harbor personal vengeance, leading to far-reaching consequences. For the one whose eye is gouged or hand cut off intentionally and aggressively by a sinful aggressor, how can he bear to see the offender stare with two eyes or enjoy two hands? In pre-Islamic times, killing a person could lead to annihilation of entire tribes in revenge, so the system of qisas came as a truly preventive remedy. If it is said - as seen in some modern foreign theories - that aggression against a human being is destruction of the human structure, and we must repair what has been destroyed as much as possible, not add another destruction by our will in retaliation, the answer is that the subsequent destruction is avoided by abandoning qisas, but it increases at the hands of the people themselves in an unjust manner. So it is better for us to avoid the great unjust destruction caused by chaos through the small destruction inflicted by the hands of justice by way of equivalent punishment, which no one can prevent except through the certainty that his arrow will return to his chest. 9/49 - Types of Qisas and Its Conditions: It is clear from the definition of qisas that it is of two types: qisas for life, which is in murder; and qisas for organs, which is in amputation and wounding. Generally, for an offense to be a crime warranting qisas, three conditions are required: transgression, intent, and possibility of equivalence between the crime and its qisas. (1) Hence qisas is also called 'imthal,' so in language it is said: the ruler exacted imthal from so-and-so for so-and-so if he executed qisas for him.
Source pages p. 641

Commentary
This passage is a legal exposition on qisas (retribution) in Islamic criminal law. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, begins by explaining the linguistic origin of the term 'qisas,' which derives from 'iqtisas al-athar' meaning following a trace, and its predominant use for capital punishment. He notes the synonym 'qawad,' referencing the practice of leading the offender to execution. The legal definition follows: qisas is punishing the offender with an equivalent act for intentional murder, amputation, or wounding. The author then cites the Quranic basis (2:178-179) and elaborates on the wisdom: qisas deters crime by making the offender realize he risks his own life or limb, preserves social stability by preventing private vengeance, and is uniquely effective compared to other penalties. A rebuttal to modern theories that reject retributive justice is provided: abandoning qisas leads to greater injustice through chaos. Finally, the two types (qisas for life and for organs) and three conditions (transgression, intent, and possibility of equivalence) are outlined. The entire discussion underscores qisas as a divinely ordained institution for justice and social order.

Key Terms
القصاص (al-qisas): Punishment of the offender with an equivalent act for intentional murder, amputation, or wounding.
القود (al-qawad): Synonym for qisas, derived from leading the offender to execution.
العدوان (al-udwan): Transgression – the act must be unjust and aggressive without legal justification.
العمد (al-amd): Intent – the offender must have acted deliberately and purposefully.
إمكان المماثلة (imkan al-mumathala): Possibility of equivalence – the ability to inflict exactly the same type and degree of injury as the crime.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Qisas is a divinely mandated, condition-bound penal institution whose ultimate purpose is the preservation of life and social order, not revenge.

Guarantee of the Price in Invalid Delivery
States a ruling with legal reasoning (talil)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a buyer who consumes before valid delivery guarantees the contract price, and apply the same principle to similar contracts like gift.

Core Idea
In a valid sale, the buyer guarantees the contract price upon consumption, not the market value, even if delivery was invalid.

This lesson examines a key Hanafi ruling on liability (daman) when possession is taken without legal authorization. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, explains that in a valid sale, ownership transfers upon contract, fixing the price. Therefore, if the buyer consumes before proper delivery, he guarantees the agreed price, not the fluctuating market value. This principle is extended to gifts, where invalid possession leads to liability for the fungible or non-fungible value.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
What is the specific act (consumption after invalid delivery) and what is the legal liability? The buyer guarantees the contract price, not market value.
The ruling hinges on the validity of the sale, not the delivery.
2
Understand the reasoning (talil)
Why does the buyer guarantee the contract price? Because ownership transferred upon the valid contract; the price was fixed by mutual consent and replaces the value.
The invalidity of delivery does not affect the ownership transfer.
3
Distinguish between price and value
The price is the agreed sum in the contract; the value is the market price at the time of consumption. The text emphasizes the price remains operative.
This distinction is crucial for correct fatwa.
4
Apply the analogy to gift (hibah)
In a gift, ownership does not transfer without authorized possession (qabd ma'dhun). If the donee takes possession without consent and the property perishes, he guarantees it as mithli or qimi.
The same principle of liability follows possession without right.
5
Extract the underlying principle
The general principle: liability for consumption of property follows the ownership or lack thereof. In a valid sale, ownership is with the buyer, so he bears the contract price. In a gift without consent, ownership is not transferred, so he bears the full value.
This principle applies across various contracts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
استهلكه، فإنما يضمن للبائع الثمن المتفق عليه في العقد، لا قيمة المبيع لأن المبيع قد انتقلت ملكيته إلى المشتري بمجرد تمام البيع الصحيح لقاء ثمن معين حل محل القيمة بالتراضي. ولم يبطل هذا التعيين للثمن، وإنما الذي بطَلَ هو القبض غير المستحق فيهلك المبيع في يد المشتري وهو مملوك له بالثمن المسمى لا بالقيمة التي قد تكون أكثر أو أقل منه وعلى هذا الأساس يتنزل أيضاً ما لو قبض الموهوب له المال الموهوب دون رضا الواهب صراحة أو دلالة فإنَّ هذا القبض باطل، لأن الهبة من العقود العينية التي لا تتم إلا بالقبض المأذون. فلو هلك المال المقبوض في يد الموهوب له في هذه الحال كان مضموناً عليه بالمثل لو مثلياً، وبالقيمة لو قيمياً.
He consumed it, then he guarantees for the seller the price agreed upon in the contract, not the value of the sold item, because the ownership of the sold item has transferred to the buyer upon the completion of the valid sale in exchange for a specific price that replaced the value by mutual consent. And this specification of the price was not invalidated; rather, what was invalidated is the unauthorized delivery, so the sold item perishes in the hand of the buyer while it is owned by him for the named price, not for the value which may be more or less. And on this basis also applies the case where the donee takes possession of the gifted property without the express or implicit consent of the donor, for this possession is invalid, because a gift is one of the real contracts that are not completed except with authorized possession. So if the possessed property perishes in the hand of the donee in this situation, it is guaranteed by him with the same type if it is a fungible, and with the value if it is a non-fungible.
Source pages p. 681

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling on the liability (daman) in cases of invalid delivery (qabd ghayr mustahiqq). The author explains that if a buyer consumes a purchased item before valid delivery, he guarantees the contract price, not the market value. The reasoning is that ownership of the item transferred to the buyer upon the valid contract, so the price is fixed. The defect is only in the delivery, which is invalid, so the buyer is liable for the price agreed. Similarly, in a gift (hibah), which is a real contract requiring authorized possession, if the donee takes possession without the donor's consent and the property perishes, he guarantees it with the same type (mithli) or value (qimi). This is a Hanafi position based on the theory of ownership transfer and the distinction between contractual obligation and possessory liability.

Key Terms
قبض غير مستحق (qabd ghayr mustahiqq): Possession taken without legal authorization, e.g., a buyer taking delivery before the seller's consent or before payment of price in a deferred sale.
ضمان (daman): Liability to compensate for loss or destruction of property in one's possession.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a buyer consumes a purchased item before valid delivery, what does he guarantee according to the Hanafi school?
A) The market value at the time of consumption
B) The agreed contract price
C) The value at the time of contract
D) Nothing, because ownership is with seller

Key Takeaway
In a valid sale, the buyer's liability for consumption before valid delivery is the contract price, not the market value, because ownership transfers upon contract.

History and Development of Fiqh

Ottoman Reform: Broadening the Contractual Object via Custom
Ottoman reform analysis and comparison of Hanafi/Hanbali doctrines

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the Ottoman reform replaced the Hanafi requirement of permissible benefit with custom as the basis for marketability, and evaluate the comparative Hanafi/Hanbali approaches to voidness and conditions.

Core Idea
The 1914 Ottoman reform shifted the basis of marketability from religious permissibility to customary trade, expanding contractual freedom.

The Ottoman state urgently needed to modernize contract law beyond the confines of the Hanafi-based Majalla. Instead of direct amendment, they inserted a new article into the Code of Civil Procedure establishing three principles. The first principle redefined what can be a valid object of contract: anything customarily traded is now marketable. This departure from the classical Hanafi standard opened new possibilities and sparked a valuable comparison between Hanafi and Hanbali doctrines on voidness and conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the historical impetus
Understand why the Ottoman state needed reform: the Majalla (based solely on Hanafi ijtihad) did not provide sufficient contractual freedom. The government sought to expand the object of contracts and permissible conditions.
The reform was enacted through the Code of Civil Procedure, not by amending the Majalla directly.
2
Analyze the three principles
Read the three principles laid out in the new Article 64. Focus especially on Principle 1: 'Everything customarily traded... is a valid object for contracting.' Note the shift from religious permissibility (ḥill al-intifāʿ) to custom (ʿurf) as the criterion for marketability.
Principles 2 and 3 govern conditions and completion of the contract; they are secondary for this lesson.
3
Compare Hanafi and Hanbali theories of voidness
The passage contrasts the two schools: Hanbali: any violation of imperative texts voids the contract (buṭlān), but the scope of prohibited conditions is narrow. Hanafi: narrow voidness via the theory of corruption (fasād), but a wide circle of prohibited conditions. Identify the practical consequence of each.
The author suggests combining Hanafi's distinction between bāṭil and fāsid with Hanbali's party autonomy and freedom of conditions.
4
Apply the first principle to specific cases
See how the new rule changes the status of wine, hashish, and abstract rights. Wine becomes marketable for Muslims (previously non-marketable); hashish remains non-marketable due to specific legal prohibition. Abstract rights (water rights, literary property) become valid objects if customarily traded.
For non-Muslims, wine was already marketable under classical Hanafi (with liability). The reform now extends this to Muslims.
5
Evaluate the mufta bihi and its limits
Recognize that the reform principles are positive law, not a change in classical Hanafi fiqh. A mufti operating under this regime uses custom as the basis, but for matters outside its scope, the classical view still applies. Identify the six exceptions that limit contractual freedom.
The reform does not abolish the classical distinction between bāṭil and fāsid but adopts a more flexible standard for marketability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب السابع : نظرية المؤيدات الشرعية في الفقه
لذلك شعرت الدولة العثمانية بالحاجة الملحة إلى وضع نص قانوني جديد يوسع حرية التعاقد والشروط العقدية وقابلية المحل المعقود عليه، مادامت المجلة - وهي بمثابة القانون المدني الشرعي العام - لم تحل هذه المشكلة، لأنها لم تأخذ من غير الاجتهاد الحنفي.
٣/٥٧ - لكن الحكومة العثمانية لم تشأ أن تجابه مجلة الأحكام الشرعية بالتعديل المباشر فالتجأت إلى قانون أصول المحاكمات الحقوقية الذي لا صلة له بهذا الموضوع، فاستبدلت بإحدى مواده الباحثة عن بعض إجراءات المحاكمة (وهي المادة / ٦٤ مادة أخرى جديدة أزلقتها محلها : بقانون صدر في ١٥ جمادى الآخرة سنة 1332هـ وقررت فيها المبادىء القانونية الثلاثة التالية :
۱ - إن كل ما تعورف تداوله من أعيان ومنافع وحقوق يعتبر محلاً صالحاً للتعاقد عليه، ككل مال متقوّم وكذلك الأشياء التي ستوجد.
۲ - إن كل اتفاق أو اشتراط يُعتبر ملزماً للمتعاقدين مالم يكن مخلاً بالآداب العامة، أو النظام العام، أو مخالفاً لنص قانوني خاص، أو لأحكام القوانين العقارية أو لأحكام الأوقاف أو لأحكام الأحوال الشخصية كالإرث والانتقال وأهلية العاقدين، وحقوق الأسرة. فهذه ست نواح مستثناة تعتبر أحكامها إلزامية لا يجوز الاتفاق على خلافها.
٣ - إذا اتفق المتعاقدان على النقاط الأصلية - أي النواحي الأساسية - يعتبر العقد تاماً، ولو لم تذكر النقاط الفرعية ثم عند الاختلاف في تلك النواحي الفرعية تعينها المحكمة بحسب ماهية القضية.

ومما يلحظ أن الاجتهاد الحنبلي قد شدد جداً في نظرية البطلان، فاعتبر كل مخالفة للنصوص الآمرة في نظام التعاقد الشرعي، وكل شرط ممنوع، سبباً لبطلان العقد. لكنه ضيق دائرة الشروط الممنوعة ففتح لحرية الاشتراط مجالاً واسعاً. أما الاجتهاد الحنفي فقد ضيق نطاق نظرية البطلان بتأسيس نظرية الفساد. لكنه جعل دائرة الشروط الممنوعة واسعة، فأورث ضيقاً محرجاً في حرية التعاقد. وكل من الاجتهادين أوجه من ناحية فلو يؤخذ من الاجتهاد الحنفي نظريته في التمييز بين البطلان والفساد ومن الحنبلي نظريته في سلطان الإرادة والشروط العقدية، لكان منهما أحكم وأرحب نظام مدني في العقود.

٤/٥٧ - ١ - تحليل المبدأ الأول:
فالمبدأ الأول من هذه المبادىء قد جاء تعديلاً لبعض أسباب البطلان فيما يتعلق بمحل العقد فإنه جاء بتوسيع قابلية المحل للتقويم، وهذه القابلية هي من شرائط الانعقاد العامة، فجعل في ذلك عرف الناس هو الأساس في التقوم، بينما كان أساسه في نظر فقهائنا هو حل الانتفاع شرعاً؛ فالخمر كانت تعتبر من الأموال غير المتقومة في حق المسلمين، فلا ينعقد بيعها وشراؤها، ولا يضمن متلفها، فأصبحت في حكم هذه المادة مالاً متقوّماً ومحلاً صالحاً للتبايع وسائر العقود (1).
(1) أما في حق غير المسلمين فتعتبر الخمر مالاً متقوّماً شرعاً، فيجوز تعاقدهم عليها. فيضمن المسلم ما أتلفه من الخمر لغير المسلم، لأن الإسلام أوجب معاملتهم بما يدينون. وهذا من مزيد سماحة الشريعة الإسلامية (راجع رد المحتار ١٠٤/٤).
(2) بخلاف الحشيش والأفيون ونحوهما من المواد المخدّرة التي يمنعها القانون بنص خاص، فإنها بقيت غير متقومة بالنظرين الشرعي والقانوني وفقاً للمبدأ الثاني. فلا تنعقد بيوعها، بل تجب مصادرتها ويعتبر اقتناؤها جريمة يعاقب القانون عليها. فنظرية عدم التقوم في المال موجودة أيضاً في النظر القانوني، لكن مبناها فيه هو المنع القانوني من تداول المال لا التحريم الشرعي. وسنرى تفصيل ذلك في فصل الأموال من الجزء الثالث الذي أسميناه المدخل إلى نظرية الالتزام العامة». (انظر أيضاً كتاب الملكية ونظرية العقد للأستاذ الشيخ محمد أبي زهرة ف/٤).

وكذلك الحقوق المجرَّدة كحق الشرب للأراضي، وحق التعلي والركوب في طوابق البناء، وكالديون في الذمم وكالامتيازات في بعض الحقوق القانوية الحديثة مثل حق الملكية الصناعية وحق الملكية الأدبية للمؤلفين في الطبع والنشر وامتيازات إصدار الصحف الدورية من جرائد ومجلات وأسماء هذه الصحف وأسماء المحلات التجارية المشتهرة التي يرغب الناس اليوم في شراء أسمائها من أصحابها للاستفادة من شهرتها.
Chapter Seven: The Theory of Legal Facilitations in Fiqh. Therefore the Ottoman state felt an urgent need to formulate a new legal text that expands freedom of contracting, contractual conditions, and the suitability of the contracted object, since the Majalla - which is like the general Islamic civil code - did not solve this problem, because it took from no other than Hanafi ijtihad. 3/57 - But the Ottoman government did not wish to directly amend the Majalla, so it resorted to the Code of Civil Procedure which has no connection to this subject, and replaced one of its articles concerning certain trial procedures (Article 64) with a new article inserted in its place: by a law issued on 15 Jumada al-Thani 1332 AH, which established the following three legal principles: 1- Everything customarily traded of objects, benefits, and rights is considered a valid object for contracting, like every marketable property, as well as things that will come into existence. 2- Every agreement or condition is binding on the contracting parties, unless it violates public morals, public order, or contradicts a specific legal text, or the provisions of land laws, or the provisions of endowments, or the provisions of personal status like inheritance, transfer, capacity of the contracting parties, and family rights. These are six exceptional aspects whose rulings are mandatory and cannot be contravened by agreement. 3- If the contracting parties agree on the essential points - i.e., the basic aspects - the contract is complete even if subsidiary points are not mentioned; then upon disagreement over those subsidiary aspects, the court determines them according to the nature of the case. It is observed that Hanbali ijtihad was very strict in the theory of voidness, considering every violation of imperative texts in the Islamic contracting system, and every prohibited condition, as a cause for voidness of the contract. But it narrowed the scope of prohibited conditions, thus opening a wide field for freedom of stipulation. As for Hanafi ijtihad, it narrowed the scope of the theory of voidness by establishing the theory of corruption. But it made the circle of prohibited conditions wide, thus causing an awkward narrowness in contractual freedom. Each of the two ijtihads has aspects of strength; if one were to take from Hanafi ijtihad its theory of distinguishing between voidness and corruption, and from Hanbali its theory of the supremacy of will and contractual conditions, a wiser and broader civil system in contracts would result from them. 4/57 - 1- Analysis of the First Principle: The first of these principles came as an amendment to some causes of voidness regarding the object of the contract; it expanded the suitability of the object for marketability, and this suitability is among the general conditions of formation. Thus it made custom of the people the basis for marketability, whereas in the view of our jurists its basis was permissibility of benefit legally. Thus wine was considered among non-marketable properties with respect to Muslims, so its sale and purchase were not valid, and one who destroys it is not liable. It became, according to this article, marketable property and a valid object for sale and other contracts. (1) As for non-Muslims, wine is considered legally marketable property, so their contracting upon it is permissible. A Muslim who destroys wine belonging to a non-Muslim is liable, because Islam requires treating them according to what they believe. This is from the abundant tolerance of Islamic law. (Ref: Radd al-Muhtar 4/104). (2) Unlike hashish, opium, and similar intoxicating substances which the law prohibits by specific text, for they remained non-marketable under both legal and statutory views according to the second principle. So their sales do not take effect, rather they must be confiscated, and possession of them is considered a crime punishable by law. Thus the theory of non-marketability of property also exists in legal theory, but its basis there is the legal prohibition of trading the property, not the religious prohibition. We will see details of that in the chapter on property in the third part which we have named 'Introduction to the General Theory of Obligation'. (See also the book 'Property and Theory of Contract' by Professor Sheikh Muhammad Abu Zahra, p. 4). Also abstract rights such as the right to water for lands, the right to elevate and ride in building floors, debts in liabilities, and concessions in some modern legal rights such as the right to industrial property, the right to literary property of authors in printing and publishing, concessions to issue periodical publications of newspapers and magazines, the names of these newspapers and the names of famous commercial establishments which people today desire to buy from their owners to benefit from their fame.
Source pages p. 718

Commentary
This passage presents a historical legal reform analysis. The author begins by noting the Ottoman state's need to expand contractual freedom beyond the Hanafi-based Majalla. The government avoided direct amendment and instead altered the Code of Civil Procedure, inserting a new Article 64 with three principles. Principle 1 broadens the object of contract: anything customarily traded (including future objects) is suitable, with custom replacing the earlier Hanafi requirement of permissible benefit. Principle 2 declares agreements binding unless they violate public morals, public order, specific laws, land laws, waqf laws, or personal status laws (six exceptions). Principle 3 states that agreement on essential terms perfects the contract, leaving subsidiary details to court determination. The author then compares Hanbali and Hanafi approaches: Hanbali is strict on voidness (any violation voids the contract) but narrow in prohibited conditions; Hanafi narrows voidness through the fasad theory but widens prohibited conditions, causing inconvenience. A combination of both is suggested. Paragraph 4/57 analyzes Principle 1 in depth. The shift from religious permissibility to custom as the basis for marketability is significant. The example of wine illustrates: previously non-marketable for Muslims, it becomes marketable under the new law (while remaining marketable for non-Muslims by shariah). Hashish and opium remain non-marketable due to specific legal prohibition, not religious. Finally, abstract rights (water rights, air rights, debts, industrial property, literary property, etc.) are recognized as marketable objects.

Key Terms
التقوم (al-taqawwum): The quality of being marketable property (māl mutaqawwim); the ability to be legally traded and compensated.
البطلان (al-buṭlān): Voidness of a contract due to a fundamental defect; in Hanafi, distinct from fasād (corruption) where some effects may be salvaged; in Hanbali, any defect causes buṭlān.
الفساد (al-fasād): Corruption; a defect in a contract that does not affect its existence but may be cured (e.g., removing a prohibited condition).
تعاقد (taʿāqud): Contracting; mutual agreement creating obligations.
عرف (ʿurf): Custom; established practice of the people. Here, it becomes the criterion for marketability instead of religious permissibility.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the first principle of the Ottoman reform, what became the basis for determining whether an object is marketable (mutaqawwim)?
A) Religious permissibility of benefit
B) Customary trading of the object
C) Explicit text in the Majalla
D) Approval of the judge

Key Takeaway
The Ottoman reform of 1914 broadened contractual freedom by making custom the criterion for marketability, while retaining exceptions for public order and specific laws, and the author suggests combining Hanafi voidness theory with Hanbali party autonomy for a more balanced system.

Zakat on a Child's Wealth: Hanafi vs. Jumhur
Presenting the difference of opinion on zakat for minors

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on zakat for a minor and contrast it with the jumhur view, identifying the root cause in the conceptualization of zakat (ibadah vs. social tax).

Core Idea
The Hanafi school holds that zakat, as a financial act of worship, requires puberty for obligation, unlike the jumhur who treat it as a property tax.

This passage presents a classic inter-madhhab dispute rooted in the nature of zakat. The Hanafi school classifies zakat as an act of worship ('ibādah māliyyah), which like prayer and fasting requires legal capacity (taklīf). The jumhur (Malik, Shafi‘i, Ahmad) view it as a social tax on property, thus applicable even to minors. Understanding this conceptual difference is key to grasping the ruling and its implications for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested issue
The issue is whether zakat is obligatory on the wealth of a minor (ṣaghīr) who has not reached puberty.
The dispute hinges on the nature of zakat, not on the minor's ability to pay.
2
Understand the Hanafi conceptualization
The Hanafi school considers zakat a financial act of worship ('ibādah māliyyah), which requires intention (niyyah) and the capacity of the person performing it. Since a minor lacks full legal capacity (taklīf), the obligation is not triggered.
This is analogous to prayer and fasting, which are not obligatory on minors.
3
Understand the jumhur conceptualization
The jumhur view zakat as a social tax (ḍarībah ijtimā‘iyyah) attached to the property itself, not to the person. Therefore, the guardian pays it on behalf of the minor from his wealth.
They compare it to other property rights like debts that are due regardless of the owner's capacity.
4
Trace the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
In the Hanafi school, zakat is not paid on a minor's wealth; in the jumhur school, the guardian must pay it. This affects estate planning and financial management for orphans.
The difference is binding in fatwa: Hanafi muftis do not require zakat on a minor's wealth.
5
Apply the Hanafi position in fatwa
When issuing a fatwa, a Hanafi mufti states that zakat is not obligatory on the wealth of a minor, citing the devotional nature of zakat and the condition of puberty.
This is the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٨/٦١ - أما زكاة الأموال فقد اختلفت الاجتهادات في إيجابها في مال الصغير لاختلافهم في طبيعتها : فذهب الاجتهاد الحنفي إلى أنها عبادة مالية يتوقف إيجابها على البلوغ كسائر التكاليف الدينية. ولذا لم يوجبها الحنفية في مال الصغير. - وذهب الأئمة الثلاثة مالك والشافعي وأحمد، إلى اعتبار الزكاة شرعاً من الضرائب الاجتماعية المتعلقة بالمال لا من العبادات، فلذا أو جبوها في مال الصغير ولو كان عديم أهلية الأداء، فيخرجها عنه نائبه الشرعي من ولي أو وصي ( ر ) بداية المجتهد أول كتاب الزكاة (٢٢٥/١).
8/61 - As for zakat on wealth, opinions differed concerning its obligation on the wealth of a minor because of their disagreement on its nature. The Hanafi opinion holds that it is a financial act of worship whose obligation depends on puberty, like all other religious obligations. Hence the Hanafis did not make it obligatory on the wealth of a minor. The three Imams – Malik, al-Shafi‘i, and Ahmad – considered zakat legally to be one of the social taxes related to wealth, not worship, so they made it obligatory on the wealth of a minor even if he lacks capacity of execution; his legal representative (guardian or executor) pays it on his behalf (see: Bidāyat al-Mujtahid, first chapter of Zakat, 1/225).
Source pages p. 752

Commentary
This passage presents a clear inter-madhhab dispute regarding zakat on a child’s wealth. The Hanafi view: zakat is an act of worship (ibādah māliyyah), and like prayer and fasting, it requires puberty (taklīf). Therefore, zakat is not obligatory on a minor’s wealth. The jumhūr (Malik, Shafi‘i, Ahmad) view: zakat is a social tax (ḍarībah ijtimā‘iyyah) on property, analogous to other property rights, and thus applies to minors. Their guardian pays it from the child’s wealth. The Hanafi school follows the former view, emphasizing the devotional nature of zakat.

Key Terms
زكاة (zakāh): The obligatory alms on wealth; disputed whether it is a worship act or a social tax.
عبادة مالية (ibādah māliyyah): A financial act of worship, requiring intention and active capacity.
ضرائب اجتماعية (ḍarā’ib ijtimā‘iyyah): Social taxes; property-based obligations unrelated to personal capacity.
البلوغ (al-bulūgh): Puberty, the threshold for full legal capacity (taklīf).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, is zakat obligatory on the wealth of a non-pubescent child?
A) Yes, because zakat is a property tax.
B) No, because zakat is an act of worship and requires puberty.
C) Yes, but only if the child is discerning (mumayyiz).
D) No, because a child has no legal capacity at all.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school does not obligate zakat on a minor's wealth because it classifies zakat as a devotional act that requires the payer's legal capacity (puberty).

Financial Duties on a Child's Property and the Zakah Dispute
Explaining types of financial duties on a minor's wealth and the zakah dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the types of financial obligations imposed on a minor's property and explain the Hanafi position on zakah, applying the concept of legal capacity (ahliyyah) and dhimma.

Core Idea
A child's property bears taxes and maintenance, but not zakah, because Hanafis consider zakah a devotional act requiring puberty.

This passage from a modern Hanafi legal introduction explores the financial duties that attach to a minor's wealth. The author distinguishes between taxes and maintenance, which apply irrespective of age, and zakah, which the Hanafi school treats as a devotional act conditional on legal capacity (taklif). The passage also critiques a modern civil code's treatment of fetal legal personality, reinforcing the Hanafi theory of dhimma. Understanding these distinctions is essential for a mufti when advising on financial obligations of guardians for minors.

Guided Path
1
Identify the types of financial duties
The passage mentions two types (after a first that is omitted): taxes on wealth (e.g., tithe, land tax, income tax) and family maintenance obligations. Note that the author lists these as applicable to the child's property.
The first type (not shown) likely relates to compensatory financial duties.
2
Analyze the legal cause (illa) for each duty
For taxes, the illa is simply ownership of wealth. For maintenance, the illa is being a wealthy relative. Both causes apply equally to minors and adults, hence the duties apply.
The text explicitly says 'the child and adult are equal in the cause (illa) of this ruling.'
3
Contrast the nature of zakah with taxes and maintenance
The Hanafi school classifies zakah as a devotional act (عبادة مالية), not a pure financial tax. Therefore, it requires the person liable to have the capacity for religious obligations (taklif), which is attained at puberty.
This is the crux of the ikhtilaf with the other schools.
4
Map the ikhtilaf on zakah for minors
Hanafis: zakah not obligatory on minor's wealth. Malik, Shafi'i, Ahmad: zakah is obligatory and guardian must pay. The dispute hinges on the nature of zakah: devotional vs. social tax.
Use the madhhab_dispute_map to study the proofs and tarjih.
5
Extract the legal principle regarding dhimma
The author criticizes the modern civil code for granting full legal personality from birth, arguing that Islamic law sees dhimma as contingent on independent personality. The fetus has rights (e.g., inheritance) but dhimma is dependent on the mother.
This point connects to the earlier discussion of ahliyyah and the stage of childhood.

الأنظار القانونية، وبه صرحت المادة / ۳۱/ من قانوننا المدني الجديد - تبعاً لأصله المصري - إذ نصت على أنه : تبدأ شخصية الإنسان بتمام ولادته حياً، وتنتهي بموته». وهذا خطأ ومتناقض مع إقرار حقوق للجنين مسلّم بها في جميع الشرائع والأنظار القانونية، ومنها الإرث ؛ إذ لا يعقل إثبات الحقوق إلا لشخص طبيعي أو اعتباري. والنظر الصحيح هو أن الذمة بمعناها الاصطلاحي في الفقه الإسلامي ليست من لوازم كل شخصية بل من لوازم الشخصية المستقلة. وشخصية الجنين غير مستقلة في نظر فقهائنا، بل هو تبع لأمه، كما سلفت الإشارة إليه ف (٣/٦٠) وكما سنراه موضحاً في نظرية الذمة المبسوطة في الجزء الثالث من هذه السلسلة الفقهية.
Chapter (61): The Second Stage of Legal Capacity: The Stage of Childhood. The second type: Taxes imposed on wealth, such as the tithe on crops and land tax. And of this category today are building tax, income tax, customs duties, and the like. All of these apply to the child, because these public rights that are imposed on wealth and feed the treasury are equal in their causes and reasons for obligation between the child and others. The third type: Social maintenance obligations tied to wealth, such as the maintenance of relatives like parents, siblings, etc. Indeed, this maintenance is a connection that carries the meaning of support required by the social system. It obligates family solidarity in preserving life. Therefore, the Law obligated maintenance for the poor of the family upon its rich members within certain degrees, limits, and conditions, and considered this maintenance like one's maintenance of oneself, to ensure stability; and because the minor and the adult among the wealthy are equal in the underlying cause of this ruling. 8/1 - As for zakah on wealth, the ijtihads differed regarding its obligation on the wealth of the minor due to their difference concerning its nature: The Hanafi ijtihad held that it is a financial act of worship whose obligation depends on puberty, like other religious obligations. Hence the Hanafis did not make it obligatory on the minor's wealth. - The three Imams—Malik, al-Shafi'i, and Ahmad—held that zakah is, legally, one of the social taxes related to wealth, not an act of worship; therefore they made it obligatory on the minor's wealth even if he lacks the capacity to perform, and his legal guardian (wali or wasi) pays it on his behalf. = Legal views: This is stated in Article 31 of our new Civil Code – following its Egyptian source – which stipulates: "A person's legal personality begins at the moment of his live birth and ends at his death." This is wrong and contradicts the recognition of the fetus's rights that are accepted in all laws and legal views, including inheritance; for it is inconceivable to establish rights except for a natural or juridical person. The correct view is that the dhimma in its technical sense in Islamic jurisprudence is not a necessary concomitant of every personality, but rather of independent personality. The fetus's personality is not independent according to our jurists; rather, it is subordinate to its mother, as previously indicated in (60/3) and as we will see explained in the theory of dhimma set forth in the third part of this jurisprudential series.
Source pages p. 752

Commentary
This passage is from a modern Hanafi legal introduction discussing the second stage of legal capacity: childhood. The author divides financial obligations imposed on a child's property into three types. The first type (not shown here) likely precedes. The second type includes public taxes on wealth, which are justified by the cause (illa) of having wealth, regardless of age. The third type is family maintenance obligations, also tied to wealth, based on the principle of family solidarity. The child, if wealthy, must pay both. Then the author presents the ikhtilaf on zakah: Hanafis consider it a devotional act (عبادة مالية), hence conditional on puberty; the other three schools consider it a social tax, thus obligatory on the minor's property, fulfilled by the guardian. This disagreement stems from the underlying nature of zakah. Finally, the author critiques a modern civil code's definition of legal personality, arguing that Islamic law recognizes a fetus's rights (e.g., inheritance) and that the dhimma (legal capacity) is not inherent but contingent on independent personality. The fetus lacks independent personality, being dependent on the mother.

Key Terms
الأهلية (al-ahliyyah): Legal capacity: the competence to acquire rights and assume obligations.
الزكاة (al-zakāh): An obligatory financial contribution on wealth, considered a devotional act by Hanafis.
الذمة (al-dhimmah): Legal personality or capacity to bear obligations; a technical term for the 'container' of rights and duties.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Because zakah is a devotional act that requires puberty.
C) Because zakah is a tax that applies only to adults.
D) Because the guardian is not responsible for the minor's wealth.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between wealth-based duties (taxes, maintenance) that apply regardless of age and devotional duties (zakah) that require puberty; the concept of dhimma further nuances legal personality.

The Third Stage: Tamyiz (Discernment) and Its Legal Significance
Introducing the stage of tamyiz: definition and characteristics

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define tamyiz in Hanafi legal theory and identify its characteristics and legal implications.

Core Idea
Tamyiz: the dawn of rational insight that allows a child to distinguish good from bad, but not yet fully mature.

This lesson introduces the third stage of legal capacity: the age of discernment (tamyiz). You will learn what tamyiz means, its characteristics, and why it is a foundational stage in Hanafi jurisprudence. Understanding tamyiz is crucial because it marks the point at which a minor acquires partial legal capacity for certain acts.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition
Read the passage and identify the precise definition of tamyiz provided by al-Zarqa.
The definition includes key phrases such as 'mental insight' and 'ability to distinguish between good and bad'.
2
Break down the definition
Identify the key elements: mental insight, ability to distinguish good/bad/benefit/harm, and its imperfection.
Note that the definition emphasizes that this insight is 'not deep' and 'not comprehensive'.
3
Relate to legal capacity
Understand how tamyiz fits into the stages of legal capacity (al-ahliyyah) as a transitional stage between non-discerning infancy and full maturity.
In Hanafi fiqh, tamyiz is the threshold for partial capacity.
4
Note the legal effect
Recognize that the discerning minor (saghir mumayyiz) can perform certain transactions with guardian's permission.
Purely beneficial acts are valid; harmful acts are void; neutral acts require permission.
5
Distinguish from full maturity
Clearly understand that tamyiz is not complete rationality; the child's intellect is still 'tender' and not fully illuminated.
Puberty (bulugh) is necessary for full legal capacity and religious obligations.

Chapter Sixty-Two: The Third Stage of Legal Capacity: The Stage of Discernment. Meaning of Discernment and Its Characteristics. The Beginning of Discernment. C - Limits of the Capacity of the Discerning Minor. - Religious Capacity and Civil Capacity. E - Classification of the Actions of the Discerning Minor. F - Rulings on Permitting the Minor to Act. G - Observations. A - Meaning of Discernment and Its Characteristics. 1/62 - This stage extends in a person's life from the age of his discernment until he reaches physical and mental maturity. What is meant by discernment is that he acquires mental insight by which he can distinguish between good and bad matters, between good and evil, benefit and harm, even if this insight is not deep, and this discernment is not complete nor comprehensive of consequences. Thus, this stage is in reality the stage of mental illumination: the human intellect enters the zone of light, and truths and perceptions begin to appear before him, until at the end of this stage they become clear and evident before the lamp of sound reason. Therefore, this stage is one of the foundational stages in a person's legal capacity, for in it a correct awareness is formed, but it emanates from a tender intellect that has not yet matured nor attained full illumination.
Source pages p. 753

Commentary
This passage opens the discussion of the third stage of legal capacity: the age of discernment (tamyiz). The author first outlines the topics to be covered: meaning, beginning, limits of capacity, classification of the minor's actions, permission to act, and observations. He then defines tamyiz as the acquisition of a basic rational insight enabling the child to differentiate between good and bad, beneficial and harmful, albeit not deeply. This stage is described as intellectual illumination: the child's mind begins to perceive truths, but still immature. It is a foundational stage because the child develops correct awareness, but the intellect is still 'tender' (غض) and not fully developed. In Hanafi fiqh, tamyiz is crucial because it determines the minor's capacity for certain acts (e.g., making contracts with permission) and distinguishes between purely beneficial, harmful, and neutral actions.

Key Terms
التمييز (al-tamyīz): Rational discernment: the ability to differentiate between good and bad, benefit and harm, albeit imperfectly.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Tamyiz marks the beginning of rational discernment, granting the minor partial legal capacity for certain acts under guardian supervision.

Classification of Actions of the Discerning Minor
Classification of minor's actions and a dispute over bequest validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three categories of a discerning minor's actions and apply the rule to determine which require guardian approval, including handling the dispute over bequests.

Core Idea
A discerning minor's actions are categorized by benefit/harm: pure benefit actions are valid independently, pure harm are void, and those with potential benefit/harm require guardian approval.

When a mufti encounters a transaction by a minor who has reached the age of discernment but not puberty, the ruling depends on whether the action is pure benefit, pure harm, or potentially beneficial or harmful. This lesson classifies these actions and explains the underlying maxims, including the disputed bequest case.

Guided Path
1
Identify the action type
Determine if the action is pure benefit (e.g., accepting a gift), pure harm (e.g., making a bequest), or potentially beneficial/harmful (e.g., sale).
Use the criteria: does it add to the minor's wealth without liability? Does it reduce his estate? Does it involve risk?
2
Apply the corresponding rule
Pure benefit actions are valid without guardian approval; pure harm actions are void; potential actions require guardian approval (prior permission or subsequent ratification).
For void actions, the maxim 'الباطل لا يقبل الإجازة' (the void does not accept validation) applies.
3
Check the disputed case (bequest)
Although the madhhab texts consider a minor's bequest void, some scholars (including al-Zarqa) argue it should be suspended pending heirs' approval. The mufta bihi is void.
The author's view is not the fatwa; rely on the established madhhab.
4
Determine the fatwa
State the ruling clearly: for pure harm actions like bequests, the action is void and cannot be validated; for pure benefit, it is valid; for potential actions, it is valid only with guardian approval.
Always cite the source: Majallah art. 967 for the classification.
5
Consider the maxim al-qawida
The maxim 'الباطل لا يقبل الإجازة' governs void actions. Note that this maxim is a minor (sughra) sub-maxim applicable to all contracts and dispositions.
The maxim prevents validating inherently defective acts.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب الثامن : نظرية الأهلية والولاية
واجباته الشرعية. أما الصغير فغير محتاج إليها لأنه غير مكلف شرعاً. وأن منفعة ورثته مصلحة له، ففي إيصائه عدول عما هو أفضل له من التوفير لورثته. وإن ترك الأفضل هو ضرر محض في حقه لا محالة، فيكون غير مشروع في حق الصغير فتبطل وصيّته (۱) (ر: أصول البزدوي وشرحه كشف الأسرار ٢٥٩/٤ : ٢٦٠).
٨/٦٢ - النوع الثاني: التصرفات التي هي نفع محض في الصغير. وذلك كقبول الهبة أو الصدقة وكإحراز المباحات . وهذا النوع تكفي فيه أهلية الأداء القاصرة لدى الصغير فتصح منه ممارسته ويكون نافذاً منه دون توقف على إجازة وليه أو وصيه، بل يكون شأنه كما لو صدر التصرف من كبير راشد لأن ممارسته هي منفعة للصغير على كل حال (ر: المجلة / ٩٦٧). وقد اعتبروا من هذا النوع قبول الصغير المميز للوكالة عن غيره في كل ما يجوز فيه التوكيل من بيع وشراء وزواج وطلاق وخصومة وقبض وغيرها. وتصرفاته بالوكالة في حدود ما وكل به تلزم الموكل كما لو كان الوكيل كبيراً. وذلك لأن ممارسة الصبي الصغير هذه التصرفات بالوكالة - ولو دون إذن وليه أو وصيه - فيها تمرين له وتصحيح العباراته، وتعريف له بوجوه التصرفات وأساليبها ونتائجها بقدر الإمكان كل ذلك على مسؤولية الموكل وعهدته وهذا نفع محض للصغير. (ر: جامع أحكام الصغار أول فصل مسائل الوكالة ورد المحتار» (٤٠٠/٤ .
(۱) أقول : إن التعليل بحقوق الورثة كان يقتضي أن تعتبر الوصية من الصغير المميز موقوفة على إجازة ورثته لا باطلة وذلك شأن جميع التصرفات القولية المضرة بحقوق الغير. وقد نقل العلامة محمد بن محمود الأستروشني في كتابه «جامع أحكام الصغار» عن شرح الطحاوي: أن الصبي لو أوصى في حال صغره بوصية لا تجوز إلا إذا أجاز ذلك بعد بلوغه، فيعتبر حينئذ كالابتداء» أي كابتداء الوصية بعد البلوغ (ر: جامع أحكام الصغار أول فصل الوصايا). وهذا يؤيد رأينا أن هذه الوصية ينبغي أن تعتبر موقوفة لا باطلة، لأن من القواعد المقررة أن الباطل لا يقبل الإجازة، كما تقدم في بحث البطلان (ف١٠/٥٣) ونص عليه الأستروشني أيضاً أول فصل مسائل البيوع من كتابه (جامع أحكام الصغار». لكن جميع نصوص المذهب التي اطلعت عليها متضافرة على البطلان، فتأمل. ولو قيل بالتوقف على إجازة الورثة كما بينا لكان أوجه وأجرى مع القواعد.
