٦٢ / ١٠ - وموافقة الممثل الشرعي تتحقق بأحد شكلين: إما بإذن منه سابق لتصرف الصغير، وإما بإجازة لاحقة بعد التصرف (۲) :
(1) المراد بالممثل الشرعي هنا من تكون له على الصغير ولاية المال. وسيأتي بيان ولي المال وولي النفس وصلاحياتهما في الفصل السادس والستين الباحث عن الولاية الشرعية الناشئة عن قصور الأهلية.
(۲) الإذن للصغير المميز بالتصرف المالي والتجارة هو تمرين مفيد له يكسبه مراساً وتجربة ومعرفة بأحوال الناس ونتائج المعاملات فهو تهيئة لرشده واختبار لمواهبه الفكرية. وأصله قول الله تعالى في سورة النساء: ﴿وَابْلُوا اليَتَى حَتَّى إذا بلغوا النِّكَاحَ فَإن انستُم منهم رُشْدًا فَادْفَعُوا إِلَيْهِمْ أَمْوَاهُمْ ﴾ [سورة النساء ٦/٤].
Chapter Eight: The Theory of Legal Capacity (Ahliyyah) and Guardianship (Wilayah). ... His religious obligations. As for the minor, he is not in need of them because he is not legally obliged (mukallaf). And that the benefit of his heirs is a benefit for him, so in his bequest (wasiyyah) there is a deviation from what is best for him, which is to preserve for his heirs. And abandoning what is best is pure harm (darar mahd) with respect to him without doubt, so it becomes unlegislated for the minor, and his bequest is invalid. (Reference: Usul al-Bazdawi and its commentary Kashf al-Asrar 4:259-260). 8/62 - The second type: actions that are pure benefit for the minor. That is like accepting a gift or charity, and taking possession of permitted things (mubahat). In this type, the limited capacity to perform (ahliyyat al-ada' al-qasirah) of the minor suffices, so his practice of it is valid and it is effective from him without being contingent upon the permission of his guardian (wali) or executor (wasi). Rather, it is as if the action came from a mature, rational adult because his practice is a benefit for the minor in any case. (Reference: Majallah / 967). They have considered from this type the acceptance by the discerning minor of agency (wakalah) on behalf of others in everything in which agency is permissible, such as sale, purchase, marriage, divorce, litigation, taking possession, and others. And his actions as an agent within the scope of what he is authorized bind the principal as if the agent were an adult. That is because the minor child's practice of these actions as an agent - even without the permission of his guardian or executor - involves training for him, correction of his expressions, and making him acquainted with the forms of transactions, their methods, and their results to the extent possible, all of which is on the responsibility and guarantee of the principal, and this is pure benefit for the minor. (Reference: Jami' Ahkam al-Sighar, first chapter on questions of agency, and Radd al-Muhtar 4:400). (1) I say: The reasoning based on the rights of the heirs would require that the bequest of a discerning minor be considered suspended pending the permission of his heirs, not void, as is the case with all verbal actions that harm the rights of others. And al-Allamah Muhammad ibn Mahmud al-Ustrushani has transmitted in his book "Jami' Ahkam al-Sighar" from Sharh al-Tahawi: that if a minor makes a bequest during his minority, it is not permissible unless he gives permission after reaching majority, then it is considered like a new beginning, i.e., like initiating the bequest after majority. (Reference: Jami' Ahkam al-Sighar, first chapter on bequests). This supports our view that this bequest should be considered suspended, not void, because among the established rules (qawa'id) is that the void (batil) does not accept validation (ijazah), as mentioned earlier in the discussion of voidness (section 5/10) and stated by al-Ustrushani as well in the beginning of the chapter on sales in his book (Jami' Ahkam al-Sighar). But all the texts of the madhhab that I have come across are unanimous on voidness, so ponder. If it were said to be suspended pending the permission of the heirs as we explained, it would be more appropriate and more in accordance with the rules. 62/9 - The third type: actions that are potentially beneficial or harmful. That is financial exchange transactions (mu'awadat maliyyah) in all their forms, such as sale, purchase, lease, rental, pledge, taking in pledge, crop-sharing (muzara'ah), sharecropping (musaqah), and everything that may involve profit and loss. This type of action the discerning minor is capable of practicing, and it is valid, but he does not have independent discretion in it; rather, for its effectiveness, the approval of his legal representative (mumaththil shar'i) is required. (Reference: Majallah / 967, as previously indicated in the discussion of effectiveness (nafadh) under general effects of contracts, section 5/38). The reason it is valid for the discerning minor to directly perform these actions by himself is that he possesses capacity to perform (ahliyyat ada), but the condition for its effectiveness is the approval of the legal representative (guardian or executor) due to the deficiency of that capacity in the minor and the potentiality of these actions for benefit and harm with respect to him. So if they are accompanied by the approval of his legal representative (guardian or executor), they are considered in the minor's interest and are effective on him as if his representative himself had performed them. 62/10 - The approval of the legal representative is realized in one of two forms: either by prior permission (idhn) before the minor's action, or by subsequent ratification (ijazah) after the action (2). (1) By "legal representative" here is meant the one who has guardianship over the minor's property (wilayat al-mal). The explanation of the guardian of property and guardian of the person and their powers will come in chapter 66, concerning legal guardianship arising from deficiency of capacity. (2) Permission for the discerning minor in financial transactions and trade is beneficial training for him, gaining him experience and knowledge of people's conditions and the results of transactions; it is a preparation for his maturity (rushd) and a test of his intellectual abilities. Its basis is the statement of Allah in Surah al-Nisa: "And test the orphans until they reach marriageable age; then if you perceive in them sound judgment, hand over their property to them" [Quran 4:6].
Source pages p. 758

Commentary
This passage from al-Zarqa's al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm explains the three categories of actions of a minor with discernment (صغير مميز). The classification is based on the extent of benefit or harm to the minor. First, actions that are pure harm (ضرر محض) are invalid; an example is a bequest (وصية) because it reduces the estate for the heirs, which is a pure loss for the minor. However, the author notes a scholarly disagreement: while the madhhab holds such a bequest void, he himself argues it should be suspended (موقوفة) pending the heirs' approval, since it is a verbal action harming others. Second, actions of pure benefit (نفع محض) are valid without guardian approval; examples include accepting gifts and acting as an agent. Third, actions with potential benefit or harm, like sales and leases, are valid only with the approval of the legal representative (guardian or executor). The approval can be prior permission or subsequent ratification. The passage emphasizes that this classification balances the minor's limited capacity with the need to protect his interests.

Key Terms
صغير مميز (saghir mumayyiz): A minor who has reached the age of discernment (tamyiz) but not puberty. He has limited capacity to perform (ahliyyat ada' qasirah).
أهلية الأداء القاصرة (ahliyyat al-ada' al-qasirah): The limited capacity to perform legal acts, possessed by the discerning minor. It enables him to perform actions but not independently for all types.
ممثل شرعي (mumaththil shar'i): The legal representative of the minor, either his guardian (wali) or executor (wasi), who has authority over the minor's property (wilayat al-mal).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following actions by a discerning minor is considered a pure benefit (نفع محض) and thus valid without guardian approval?
A) Making a bequest (وصية)
B) Accepting a gift (هبة)
C) Selling his property
D) Renting his asset

Key Takeaway
The discerning minor's actions are classified as pure benefit (valid independently), pure harm (void), or potential benefit/harm (requires guardian approval), with a dispute over bequests that is resolved by the madhhab as void.

Right to Ratify Past Transactions After Adulthood
Rule on ratifying past transactions after adulthood

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that a non-authorized minor's right to ratify his prior suspended transactions transfers to him upon adulthood only if the guardian had not already rejected them.

Core Idea
The guardian's prior rejection makes the minor's transaction void; adulthood cannot revive it.

When a non-authorized discerning minor reaches adulthood, a crucial question arises: can he now ratify the transactions he made while a minor? The answer depends on whether his guardian had already decided on those transactions. This lesson clarifies the rule, distinguishing between guardian rejection and inaction, and introduces the analogy of a dismissed agent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the actor and his capacity
The rule applies to a non-authorized discerning minor (saghīr mumayyiz ghayr ma'dhūn) who has reached maturity but is not yet an adult, or who has reached adulthood. Determine the type of transactions: suspended (mawqūfa) because the minor lacks full capacity.
Only discerning minors (mumayyiz) can perform suspended transactions; non-discerning minors' transactions are void ab initio.
2
Classify the guardian's prior action
Check whether the legal representative (walī) of the minor had already taken a decision on the transaction: either ratification (ijāza), rejection (rafḍ), or no decision. The rule's outcome critically depends on this.
If the guardian ratified, the transaction is effective from the start; if rejected, it is void.
3
Apply the rule for 'no decision'
If the guardian did not ratify or reject before the minor's adulthood, the right to ratify transfers to the minor upon his reaching rushd (maturity). He can now independently approve the transaction, making it binding.
Adulthood grants full capacity (ahliyyat al-adā' kāmila) to manage one's rights.
4
Apply the rule for 'guardian rejection'
If the guardian had rejected the transaction before the minor's adulthood, the transaction is void (bāṭil) and the minor cannot revive it even after reaching adulthood. The guardian's rejection is effective immediately, like the dismissal of an agent.
The analogy: dismissal of a wakīl only takes effect upon the agent's knowledge, but rejection is an act of disposal of the transaction itself, not the agent's capacity.
5
Compare with agency dismissal analogy
The author analogizes: when a principal dismisses his agent, the dismissal is effective only after the agent learns of it. Before that, the agent's transactions are valid. Similarly, the guardian's rejection is comparable to a principal's dismissal, but here the rejection of the transaction itself takes immediate effect.
This analogy reinforces that the guardian's rejection terminates the transaction, not the minor's capacity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
١٨/٦٢ - هذا ويجب أن يلحظ في هذا المقام ثلاثة أمور هامة : الأول : أنَّ الصغير المميز غير المأذون إذا بلغ الرشد ينتقل إليه حق إجازة تصرفاته الموقوفة التي كان باشرها حال الصغر ولم يبت فيها ممثله الشرعي بإجازة أو رفض، لأنه متى بلغ الرشد استقل في التصرف بحقوقه. أما إذا كان التصرف الواقع من الصغير قد رفضه ممثله قبل بلوغ رشده فلا يملك الصغير حق إجازته بعد بلوغه، لأن التصرف قد بطل بالرفض (1) كالوكيل إذا عزله الموكل، فإنه لا ينفذ عزله إلا بعد علمه به وقبل ذلك يبقى على وكالته، وتصرفاته نافذة على الموكل.
18/62 - Important notes to be observed in this regard: The first: The non-authorized discerning minor, if he reaches maturity (rushd), the right to ratify his suspended transactions that he performed during his minority and which his legal representative had not decided upon by ratification or rejection transfers to him. Because when he reaches maturity, he becomes independent in disposing of his rights. But if the transaction performed by the minor had been rejected by his representative before his reaching maturity, then the minor does not have the right to ratify it after his maturity, because the transaction had become void by rejection. For example, if an agent is dismissed by his principal, his dismissal is not effective until he knows of it; before that he remains an agent and his transactions are binding on the principal.
Source pages p. 764

Commentary
This passage discusses an important rule regarding the fate of transactions made by a non-authorized discerning minor (saghir mumayyiz ghayr ma'dhun) when he reaches maturity (rushd). The author presents the first of three important notes: Upon reaching adulthood, the minor gains the right to ratify his suspended transactions (tasarrrufat mawqufa) that he conducted while a minor, provided that the guardian had not already explicitly rejected them. This is because adulthood grants him full independent capacity to manage his rights. However, if the guardian had already rejected the transaction before the minor's adulthood, that transaction becomes void (batil) and cannot be revived even after adulthood. The author analogizes this to the case of a dismissed agent (wakil): the dismissal (azl) of the agent only takes effect once he learns of it; until then, his transactions are binding on the principal. Similarly, the guardian's rejection of the minor's transaction takes effect immediately, not needing the minor's knowledge because it is a rejection of a transaction, not of a person.

Key Terms
إجازة (ijaza): Ratification; the act of approving a suspended transaction, making it effective.
الصغير المميز غير المأذون (al-saghīr al-mumayyiz ghayr al-ma'dhūn): Non-authorized discerning minor: a minor who can discern benefits and harms but has not been given permission to engage in transactions.
الرشد (al-rushd): Maturity; the state of being an adult with full legal capacity, usually attained at puberty or by reaching the age of majority.
تبطل بالرفض (tabṭulu bi al-rafḍ): Becomes void by rejection; the transaction is nullified and cannot be revived.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Only if the guardian had already ratified them.
C) If the guardian had not already rejected them.

Key Takeaway
A non-authorized discerning minor may ratify his suspended transactions after reaching adulthood only if his guardian had not already rejected them; rejection voids the transaction permanently.

The Age of Majority (Sin al-Rushd) and Public Interest
Compares age of majority across legal systems, highlighting shift from puberty to higher ages due to maslaha.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of maslaha to justify adjusting the age of majority, distinguishing between fixed-age systems and the Hanafi reliance on prudent discernment.

Core Idea
Raising the age of majority is not a fixed rule but a maslaha-based adjustment.

In this lesson, we examine how the age of majority has shifted across legal systems from puberty-based ages to higher thresholds due to societal complexity. This comparative view illustrates the Hanafi principle that the age of majority is not fixed in revelation but left to the ruler's ijtihad based on public interest (maslaha).

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
Recognize that the age of majority (sin al-rushd) determines when a person gains full legal capacity. The passage shows historical variations.
Focus on the shift from simple to complex societies.
2
Observe the historical shift
Roman law initially set ages near puberty (14/12) but later raised it to 25 due to harms of early capacity. Modern laws vary (19, 21, 18).
The trend is upward.
3
Identify the underlying rationale
The passage cites public interest (maslaha) as the driver: complexity of transactions and weakened family ties necessitate raising the age.
Maslaha is a key concept in Hanafi ifta.
4
Connect to Hanafi fiqh
In Hanafi fiqh, sin al-rushd is not fixed by nass; it is determined by actual prudent behavior, but the ruler may set an age limit based on maslaha.
This contrasts with fixed-age systems.
5
Apply to fatwa context
A mufti in a modern state would recognize the legal age set by the ruler as binding, provided it does not contradict shariah. The Hanafi flexibility allows adaptation.
The mufta bihi follows the ruler's determination.

Source Text

٦/٦٤ - تحديد سن الرشد في القوانين :
كان في التشريع الروماني سن محددة للرشد الذي يخرج به الإنسان من نطاق الحجر الطبيعي القانوني، ويتمتع فيه بأهلية التصرف كاملة. وكانت لديهم هذه السن في بداية الأمر أربع عشرة سنة في الذكور، واثنتي عشرة في الإناث أي سن البلوغ الفعلي تقريباً)، لأن الأخلاق كانت ساذجة وروابط الأسرة ومراقبتها قوية الإحاطة بالناشئين. فلا حاجة للإبطاء منحهم الأهلية الكاملة.

6/64 - Determination of the Age of Majority in Laws: In Roman legislation, there was a specific age for majority, by which a person exits the scope of natural legal interdiction and enjoys full capacity of disposal. Initially, this age was fourteen years for males and twelve for females—approximately the actual age of puberty—because morals were simple and family ties and supervision were strongly encompassing the young; so there was no need to delay granting them full capacity. When society became crowded, business diversified, greed prevailed, and family ties weakened, the harms of hastening the grant of capacity appeared, forcing them to postpone it until the age of twenty-five. Some modern legal systems set the age of majority at nineteen years; some at twenty-one; and the former Egyptian law chose eighteen years, then raised it to twenty-one (see: Explanation of the Old Egyptian Civil Code by Professor Fathi Zaghloul Pasha, p. 28). 7/64 - It is clear that raising the age of majority to a certain point after puberty is today necessitated by public interest. Transactions and their procedures have become complex.
Source pages p. 775

Commentary
This passage provides a comparative legal perspective on the age of majority (sin al-rushd). The author begins by noting that Roman law had fixed ages—14 for males, 12 for females—which corresponded roughly to puberty. In early Roman society, these ages sufficed due to simple morals and strong family oversight. However, as society became more complex, with increased commercial activity and weakened family ties, the harms of granting full capacity too early became apparent, prompting a postponement to age 25. The author then surveys modern legal systems: some set the age at 19, others at 21. The former Egyptian law set it at 18 but later raised it to 21, citing Fathi Zaghloul's commentary. The final paragraph concludes that raising the age of majority is a requirement of public interest (maslaha) in our time, given the complexity of transactions and procedures. This comparative discussion is relevant for understanding how Islamic law's flexible approach (leaving age to rulers based on maslaha) aligns with contemporary legal developments.

Key Terms
سن الرشد (sin al-rushd): The legal age at which a person attains full capacity to dispose of property and rights, exiting the state of legal interdiction.
المصلحة (al-maslaha): Public interest or benefit; a principle in Islamic law that allows the ruler to enact regulations that serve the common good, provided they do not contradict explicit shariah texts.
الحجر (al-hajr): Legal interdiction; restriction of a person's capacity to act due to minority, insanity, or other reasons.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) To align with religious requirements
B) To simplify legal procedures
C) Because transactions and procedures have become complex, weakening family oversight
D) Because all countries agreed on a uniform age

Key Takeaway
The age of majority is not fixed in Hanafi fiqh; it is determined by the ruler based on public interest, allowing adaptation to changing societal conditions.

Hanafi Principles of Majority and Ottoman-Syrian Regulative Interplay
Historical analysis of the age of majority in Hanafi-based Syrian law

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi fiqh position on the age of majority (requiring both puberty and rushd) and evaluate how Ottoman-Syrian regulations modified this through the principle of maslaha.

Core Idea
Hanafi fiqh requires case-by-case rushd; Ottoman law set a fixed age of twenty for public interest.

This lesson traces the historical development of the legal age of majority in Ottoman Syria, revealing a dynamic interaction between classical Hanafi fiqh—which defines full capacity by the attainment of puberty and proven prudence (rushd)—and state regulations that raised the age to twenty for protective purposes. Understanding this interplay is crucial for appreciating how Islamic law accommodates changing circumstances without abandoning its core principles.

Guided Path
1
Recall the Hanafi theory of ahliyyah
Hanafi fiqh distinguishes between capacity for rights (ahliyyat al-wujub) and capacity for action (ahliyyat al-ada'). Full capacity for action requires both bulugh (puberty) and rushd (prudence). Without proof of rushd, a person remains under interdiction (hajr).
Rushd is not presumed; it must be established.
2
Analyze the Ottoman sultanic decree of 1288 AH
The decree prohibited judges from accepting claims of majority for anyone under twenty, effectively setting a minimum age. It was justified by 'temporal interest' (maslaha zamanah) due to widespread fraud.
This was a regulatory measure, not a change in fiqh.
3
Examine the Majalla's position (1293 AH)
The Majalla returned to the original Hanafi rule: a minor who reaches puberty may prove his rushd and receive property. But it did not make puberty a legal presumption of rushd; proof was still required.
The Majalla's committee was instructed to codify Hanafi fiqh, leaving public interest regulations to the Sultan.
4
Assess the 1324 AH Regulation for Orphans' Property
This regulation mandated that orphans receive their property only after completing twenty years. The author argues this effectively reimposed the fixed age, as it prevented both delivery of assets and, by implication, any disposition before that age.
The regulation was still in force in Syria at the time of writing.
5
Evaluate the role of maslaha in reconciling these positions
The regulations were not seen as contradicting Sharia but as implementations of the ruler's authority to protect public interest (tasarruf al-imam ala al-ra'iyya manutun bi al-maslaha). This shows the flexibility of Islam when applying a key legal maxim.
A modern mufti must weigh the original ruling against contemporary needs.

Source Text

٩/٦ - وبذلك نسخت المجلة حكم الإرادة السلطانية السابقة في تحديد سن الرشد بعشرين عاماً ورجعت به إلى حد البلوغ الطبيعي، ولكنها لم تجعل البلوغ قرينة شرعية (قانونية) كافية لاعتبار الشخص رشيداً إلى أن يثبت سفههه ويقضي عليه بالحجر، بل جعلت الشخص بالبلوغ قابلاً لأن يكون رشيداً. فعليه إثبات رشده ليصبح كامل الأهلية.
(۱) ترى هذه الإرادة السنية في شرح المادة // ۹۸۳/ من المجلة من شرح العلامة علي حيدر، وشرح الأستاذ سليم رستم الباز (الطبعة الثانية).
ثم صدر في ٤ من ربيع الأول سنة ١٣٢٤ هـ نظام إدارة أموال الأيتام الذي لا يزال بعض أحكامه نافذاً في سورية إلى اليوم) فنصت المادة / ٥٩ منه على أنه :

وهذا كما ترى عودة قانونية من السلطان العثماني إلى تحديد سن الرشد بالحد الذي كانت قررته الإرادة السنية السلطانية سنة ١٢٨٨ هـ، وهو تمام العشرين عاماً. ذلك لأن التعبير القانوني بتسليم المال إلى الأيتام بعد إكمالهم عشرين عاماً، أو بعدم تسليمهم أموالهم قبل هذه السن يستلزم نفي إمكان رشدهم ،قبلها ويساوي التعبير بعدم» كمال أهليتهم قبل سن العشرين .
فقد أسلفنا أن منع تسليم المال إلى صاحبه الناشيء قبل إيناس رشده يستلزم منع نفاذ تصرفاته القولية (ر : ف (٢/٦٤ . ولا يعقل في المنطق التشريعي خلاف ذلك؛ وإلا استطاع الشخص أن يتصرف بجميع أمواله ويبددها بكلمتين عن طريق التعاقد أو الإقرار، فلا يكون عدم تسليمها إليه من إتلافه لها، إذ يتسلمها غرماؤه الدائنون له بدلاً منه (1) .
(۱) أثناء الطبعة الثالثة من هذا الكتاب اطلعت على ما كتبه الأستاذ المحامي الدكتور صبحي المحمصاني من بيروت عن الأهلية في الجزء الثاني من كتابه القيم الذي ظهر حديثاً باسم النظرية العامة للموجبات والعقود في الشريعة الإسلامية» ونقلته عنه المجلة القضائية البيروتية في العدد الثالث من السنة / ۲۸/ فإذا به يقرر انفكاك التلازم بين كمال أهلية التصرف وتسليم المال : فإنه بعد أن حكى اختلاف الاجتهادات في ارتفاع الحجر عن الصغير بمجرد البلوغ أو بظهور الرشد بعد البلوغ، نقل ما جاء في المادة / ۹۸۲/ من المجلة ونصها :
Chapter Eight: Theory of Legal Capacity and Guardianship. It branched out greatly, public morals deteriorated, and the tricks of swindlers in seizing people's wealth multiplied, so it became necessary to increase caution in protecting minors and safeguarding their rights and property by setting the age of majority at a high limit. This does not contradict the wise Islamic Sharia, but rather fully agrees with its principles and jurisprudence in caring for interests. (Ref: Risala al-Ahliyya by Prof. Shaykh Ahmad Ibrahim, p. 21). The legal age of majority in Syria. 8/64 - (1) - Before the new civil code: In 1288 AH of the Ottoman era, a sultanic decree was issued prohibiting judges from hearing a claim of majority and proving it for anyone who had not completed twenty years of age, considering the temporal interest that necessitated raising the age of majority to a level where experience in financial matters is available (1). Then the Majallat al-Ahkam al-Adliyya was issued by the sultanic decree dated 23 Sha'ban 1293 AH, and its articles 981-983 allowed the minor, if he has reached puberty, to prove his majority from the time of his puberty, and then the guardian hands over his property to him. In that, the Majalla followed the original legal ruling, because the committee entrusted with drafting it proceeded to codify the original Sharia rulings based on Hanafi ijtihad, leaving matters of interests to the sultanic orders. 9/6 - Thus, the Majalla abrogated the previous sultanic decree regarding setting the age of majority at twenty years and reverted to the natural age of puberty, but it did not make puberty a sufficient legal presumption to consider a person mature until his folly is proven and he is interdicted. Rather, it made the person upon puberty capable of being mature. So he must prove his majority to become fully competent. (1) See this sultanic decree in the commentary of article 983 of the Majalla by Allama Ali Haydar and the commentary of Prof. Salim Rustam al-Baz (second edition). Then on 4 Rabi' al-Awwal 1324 AH, the Regulation for the Management of Orphans' Property was issued, some provisions of which are still in force in Syria until today. Its article 59 states: "After the orphans complete the age of twenty, the Sharia court prepares a document to that effect, and it is submitted to the orphan directors, and after the necessary procedures, their funds in the box are handed over to them." This, as you see, is a legal return by the Ottoman Sultan to setting the age of majority at the limit that the previous sultanic decree of 1288 AH had established, namely the completion of twenty years. That is because the legal expression of handing over property to orphans after they complete twenty years, or of not handing over their property before this age, necessarily implies the negation of the possibility of their majority before that and equals the expression of their lack of full legal capacity before the age of twenty. We have already mentioned that preventing the handing over of property to its young owner before perceiving his majority necessitates preventing the validity of his verbal dispositions (ref: Section 2/64). And it is not reasonable in legislative logic otherwise; otherwise, a person could dispose of all his property and squander it with a few words through contract or acknowledgment, so not handing it over to him would not prevent him from destroying it, since his creditors would receive it instead of him. (1) During the third edition of this book, I came across what Dr. Subhi al-Mahmassani, attorney from Beirut, wrote about legal capacity in the second part of his valuable book recently published under the title "The General Theory of Obligations and Contracts in Islamic Law," and it was quoted by the Beirut Judicial Journal in issue 3 of year 28. He affirmed the separation between complete capacity for disposition and the handing over of property: after he narrated the difference of ijtihads regarding the removal of interdiction from a minor upon mere puberty or upon the appearance of majority after puberty, he quoted what is in article 982 of the Majalla...
Source pages p. 776

Commentary
This passage is a historical and legal analysis of the age of majority in the context of Hanafi fiqh as applied in Ottoman and Syrian law. It begins by noting the need for a higher age of majority due to moral decay and fraud, asserting that this aligns with Islamic principles of protecting interests. It then traces the legal milestones: in 1288 AH, an Ottoman sultanic decree set the age of majority at 20, prohibiting judges from hearing claims of majority for anyone younger. Later, the Majalla (1293 AH) reversed this by allowing those who reached puberty to prove their majority, adhering to the original Hanafi ruling that puberty is the threshold, but requiring proof of prudence. However, a 1324 AH regulation for managing orphans' property reverted to the age of 20 by mandating that orphans receive their property only after completing that age. The author argues that this effectively denies the possibility of majority before 20, as it prevents both receipt of property and validity of verbal dispositions. The passage concludes with a reference to a scholarly opinion that separates full capacity from property delivery, but the author implies that the law's practical effect is otherwise. The key lesson is that while Hanafi fiqh emphasizes discernment (rushd) rather than a fixed age, administrative regulations can raise the age for public interest (maslaha), reflecting the flexibility of Islamic law.

Key Terms
أهلية (ahliyyah): Legal capacity to acquire rights and bear obligations; full capacity requires both puberty (bulugh) and prudence (rushd).
رشد (rushd): Prudence or sound judgment in financial matters, separate from physical puberty; required for full legal capacity.
حجر (hajr): Interdiction, a legal restriction preventing a person from disposing of property due to incapacity (e.g., minority, insanity, or proven foolishness).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What was the effect of the Ottoman sultanic decree of 1288 AH on the age of majority?
A) It abolished the requirement of puberty for majority.
B) It prohibited judges from accepting claims of majority for anyone under twenty.
C) It lowered the age of majority to fifteen.
D) It required a court-appointed expert to assess rushd.

Key Takeaway
The age of majority in Hanafi-based law is not a fixed fiqh ruling but a regulatory measure that can be adjusted by the ruler for public interest, while the core fiqh principle of rushd remains intact.

History and Development of Fiqh

The Ruling on Debtor's Transactions: From Hanbali Istihsan to Hanafi Fatwa
Explaining the Hanafi adoption of the ruling that a debtor's waqf is void except for surplus assets

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi position that a debtor's waqf (and similar harmful transactions) is void against creditors, and identify the illah that governs the extension to other transactions.

Core Idea
A debtor's harmful disposition is void against creditors – the illah is protecting rights, not the transaction form.

This passage traces the development of a pivotal ruling in Hanafi fatwa: the non-enforcement of a debtor's waqf. Originating from Hanbali jurists as istihsan, it was later adopted by the great Ottoman mufti Abu al-Su'ud and enforced by sultanic decree. More than a historical fact, the passage teaches how an illah can extend a ruling to new cases – a critical skill for the mufti.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original ruling and its dalil
Recognize that the default rule (from qiyas) was that debt does not restrict capacity, so a debtor's transactions are valid. The original proof was the general principle of unrestricted capacity.
This original ruling is found in the earlier Hanafi and Hanbali schools.
2
Note the departure via istihsan
Later Hanbali jurists gave fatwa that a debtor's waqf is void except for surplus assets. This is contrary to qiyas but justified by istihsan to protect creditors.
The passage cites Ibn Rajab's Qawa'id as a source.
3
Trace the Hanafi adoption
Hanafi jurists, led by Abu al-Su'ud, followed this fatwa. He obtained a sultanic order making it law. This shows how fatwa can become enforced legislation.
Abu al-Su'ud's Ma'rudat recorded these opinions.
4
Extend the illah to other transactions
The author argues that the same illah (protecting creditors) applies to gifts and sales with undervalue. Therefore, those transactions are also not enforceable against creditors by priority (awlawiyyah).
This qiyas-based extension is the author's own reasoning.
5
Recognize the methodological lesson
This case illustrates how a mufti must consider the effective cause (illah) rather than stick to literal texts, and how rulings can evolve with changing times and circumstances.
This is a key principle in Rasm al-Mufti: the mufti may adopt a ruling based on istihsan and public interest.

ومعنى هذا أن المديونية أصبحت تنتقص من أهلية التصرف في المدين، وتجعله محجوراً حجراً عفوياً كالصغير المميز، وبذلك أصبحت المديونية عارضاً من عوارض الأهلية.
بهذا أفتى المتأخرون من فقهاء المذهب الحنبلي استحساناً على خلاف القواعد القياسية التي كان عليها الحكم في أصل المذهب (ر : القواعد لابن رجب تحت القاعدة ١١ ص ١٤).

الأصل ج ۲ ٨٤٠

ونرى أن فتواهم هذه بعدم نفاذ وقف المدين إلا في الجزء الزائد من أمواله عن وفاء الدين يدل على أنَّ غير الوقف من التصرفات المضرة بحقوق الدائنين كالهبة) والبيع مع المحاباة في الثمن) حكمه كحكم الوقف في عدم النفاذ بطريق الأولوية. وإنما نصَّ فقهاء الحنفية على الوقف لأنه هو الطريقة التي شاعت إذ ذاك في تهريب المديونين لأموالهم. فإذا شاع لجوء المديونين إلى تصرفات تهريبية أخرى كالبيع والهبة، كما في زماننا اليوم، شملها أيضاً حكم عدم النفاذ لأنّ العلة واحدة في الوقف وغيره. وهذا التعميم هو ظاهر نصوص المذهب الحنبلي والمالكي أيضاً(۱) .
٦/٦٥ - تنبيه مهم : اعتاد المؤلفون في العرض الفقهي التقليدي لعوارض الأهلية أن يضيفوا حالات أخرى كثيرة غير التي ذكرنا في هذا الفصل. ولنا على ذلك مآخذ نبيِّنها في الملحق الأول لهذا الباب الثامن وهو يلي الفصل ٦٦ مباشرة .
(۱) الفقه الأجنبي الحديث قد أقر أيضاً هذا المبدأ الذي قرره الفقه الإسلامي. فإن الفقه الأجنبي يعتبر تصرفات المدين المعسر في أمواله للإضرار بحقوق الدائنين غير نافذة عليهم. فلكل من الدائنين حق إقامة الدعوى بطلب عدم سريانها بالنسبة إليه. وبذلك جاءت المادة / ١١٦٧ من القانون المدني الفرنسي. ويسمون هذه الدعوى بالدعوى البولصية : Action paulienne نسبة إلى الذي قال بها. ويسميها القانونيون العرب : دعوى عدم نفاذ التصرفات .(٢ / ٤٣٧ ويقال إن أصلها لديهم يرجع إلى التشريع الروماني، ثم تطورت بعد ذلك إلى أحكامها الجديدة في الفقه الأجنبي وقوانينه (ر) کولان وكابيتان، ج ولكن وجود هذه الدعوى في التشريع الروماني أصبح اليوم محل شك كبير لدى الباحثين المحققين (ر): نظرية العقد للأستاذ الكبير عبد الرزاق السنهوري ف/ ٧٢٩/ الحاشية) .
٨٤١
Chapter Eight: Theory of Legal Capacity and Guardianship. Safeguarding their rights: if they ratify it, it takes effect; if they reject it, it becomes void. This means that debt diminishes the capacity to dispose of the debtor and makes him interdicted involuntarily like a discerning minor. Thus, debt became an impediment to legal capacity. The later jurists of the Hanbali school gave fatwa on this as istihsan, contrary to the analogical rules that were the original ruling in the school (see: Qawa'id of Ibn Rajab under rule 11, p. 14). Then the Hanafi jurists followed them in this fatwa. Thus, al-Mawla Abu al-Su'ud, Mufti of the Romans, gave fatwa that the waqf of a debtor is not valid except for the portion exceeding what suffices to pay his debts. He obtained a sultanic order for this and recorded it in his 'Ma'rudat', and the jurists of his time and after approved it (see: F 6/15 and 5/38, type 4). (1) Abu al-Su'ud is Muhammad ibn Muhammad al-'Imadi from Isklib, a village in the lands of the Romans. Hence he is called Mufti of the Romans. The leadership of the Hanafi school in the time of Sultans Selim and Sulayman ended with him in his lifetime, and he was the general mufti of the Ottoman state and the judge of Constantinople and its dependencies. He lived respected and honored throughout his life. He would engage in ijtihad in some issues and had opinions that were unique to him, and the jurists of his time and after followed him in them. He also had good poetry and a famous Qur'an commentary. He died in 982 AH (see: Shadharat al-Dhahab by Ibn al-'Imad under events of 982, vol. 8, p. 398). His mentioned Ma'rudat include what he gave fatwa on from these new opinions, which involve restricting an absolute text or modifying an ijtihadi legal ruling based on changing times and requirements of public interest. He presented these to the Sultan, who issued an order to the judges to act upon them, giving them the force of law, like the case of the non-enforcement of the debtor's waqf mentioned (see: F 4/29 footnote, al-Durr al-Mukhtar, and Radd al-Muhtar, Book of Waqf, 3/395). These Ma'rudat are written in Turkish. The German scholar Paul Horster wrote a treatise on Islamic jurisprudence in the 16th century CE and on the Ma'rudat of Shaykh al-Islam Abu al-Su'ud, published this treatise in German in 1935, presented it to the University of Bonn, and printed in Stuttgart (see: History of Arabic Literature by the German scholar Brockelmann, vol. 2, p. 580, and supplement vol. 2, p. 651).

We see that their fatwa on the non-enforcement of the debtor's waqf except in the portion of his property exceeding the debt indicates that non-waqf harmful transactions toward creditors' rights, such as gifts and sales with favoritism in price, have the same ruling as waqf in non-enforcement by priority. The Hanafi jurists only specified waqf because it was the method commonly used at that time by debtors to smuggle their property. If debtors take to other smuggling transactions like sale and gift, as in our time today, the ruling of non-enforcement also includes them because the cause (illah) is the same in waqf and others. This generalization is apparent from the texts of the Hanbali and Maliki schools as well(1).
6/65 - Important note: Authors in the traditional fiqh presentation of impediments to legal capacity are accustomed to add many other situations besides those we mentioned in this chapter. We have objections to that which we will explain in the first appendix to this eighth chapter, which immediately follows section 66.
(1) Modern foreign jurisprudence has also approved this principle that Islamic jurisprudence established. Foreign jurisprudence considers the transactions of an insolvent debtor in his property to harm creditors' rights as non-enforceable against them. Each creditor has the right to bring a lawsuit requesting non-invalidation concerning him. This is what Article 1167 of the French Civil Code stipulates. They call this lawsuit the Paulian action (Action paulienne) after its proponent. Arab jurists call it 'the lawsuit of non-enforcement of transactions' (2/437). It is said that its origin traces back to Roman legislation, then it developed into its new rulings in foreign jurisprudence and its laws (see: Colin and Capitaine, vol. 2/437). However, the existence of this lawsuit in Roman legislation has become highly questionable today among verified researchers (see: Theory of Contract by the great professor Abd al-Razzaq al-Sanhuri, p. 729, footnote 1).
Source pages p. 789

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling on the effect of debt on legal capacity (ahliyyah) and its consequences for the validity of a debtor's transactions. The author explains that debt restricts the debtor's capacity to dispose of property, making him akin to an interdicted discerning minor. This ruling was initially adopted by later Hanbali jurists as istihsan (juristic preference), deviating from the standard analogical rules that would not restrict a debtor's capacity. Hanafi jurists, led by Shaykh al-Islam Abu al-Su'ud (the Mufti of the Romans in the Ottoman Empire), subsequently adopted this ruling. Abu al-Su'ud issued a fatwa that a debtor's waqf (endowment) is only valid in the portion of his assets exceeding the amount needed to pay his debts. He obtained an imperial sultanic decree to enforce this, elevating the fatwa to the status of law. The passage then extends the principle: any transaction by a debtor that harms creditors (such as gifts or sales at undervalue) is also void against creditors' rights, since the underlying cause (illah) – protecting creditors – is the same as in waqf. The Hanafi jurists originally focused on waqf because that was the common method of asset concealment in their time. The author notes that modern French law has a similar concept (the Paulian action). A final remark warns that some classical authors include many other impediments to capacity, but those will be addressed in an appendix.

Key Terms
المديونية (al-madyuniyyah): The state of being indebted, which is considered a temporary impediment to full legal capacity.
أهلية التصرف (ahliyyat al-tasarruf): The legal capacity to perform acts of disposition, such as selling, gifting, or endowing property.
حجر (hajr): A legal interdiction preventing a person from disposing of his property due to a cause like debt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on a debtor's gift if it harms the creditors' rights?
A) It is valid and enforceable.
B) It is void only if the debtor becomes bankrupt later.
C) It is not enforceable against the creditors, like the debtor's waqf.
D) It is always void regardless of harm.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school adopted a ruling based on istihsan that a debtor's harmful dispositions are not enforceable, and the same illah extends to all transactions that harm creditors.

Hanafi Fetal Rights and the Application of the Maxim 'Harm Must Be Removed'
Qawida definition: fetal rights limited; harm prevention justifies guardianship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four limited rights of the fetus in Hanafi fiqh, apply the maxim 'al-darar yuzal' to justify appointing a property guardian, and evaluate the Zaydi position.

Core Idea
The fetus has no property ownership but necessity grants four specific rights and allows a guardian for its assets.

This passage from Zarqa's al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi defines the fetus's legal capacity (ahliyya) in Hanafi fiqh: it is not a full legal person, so gifts are invalid. However, necessity (darurah) establishes four specific rights (inheritance, bequest, etc.) and even permits appointing a guardian to manage its property. The text contrasts the moderate Hanafi approach with the Zaydi Shia extreme of allowing marriage of the fetus, and shows how modern positive law (Egyptian and Syrian) adopted the Hanafi-inspired guardianship rule using the maxim that harm must be removed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi ruling on gifts to a fetus
Read the first sentence: 'if one gives it a gift, the gift is not valid and the fetus does not own the gifted property.' This is the core Hanafi position: no property ownership for the unborn.
Contrast with later statement about the four rights – the exception proves the rule.
2
Analyse the necessity justification for the four rights
The text says 'only the four rights... are established for the fetus, due to necessity (li al-darurah).' Necessity overrides the default lack of capacity.
The four rights are not detailed here, but typically include inheritance, wasiyyah, kinship, etc.
3
Extract the underlying qawida from the guardianship argument
The Ministry of Justice justification: 'not appointing a guardian... harms its interest.' This directly invokes the maxim 'al-darar yuzal' (harm must be removed). The guardian is appointed to prevent harm to the fetus's property.
This is a clear iftaa application of the qawida to a modern problem.
4
Evaluate the Zaydi position as a contrast
The author calls it 'extremeness from which legal thought recoils' – marryin￾g a fetus is beyond the ambit of necessity. This highlights the Hanafi discipline of qiyas and sadd al-dhara'i.
The critique is methodological, not disrespectful.
5
Apply the qawida to the legislative adoption
The Syrian Personal Status Law (Article 177) allows a judge to appoint a guardian for fetus property. This shows the qawida's operativity in state codification.
Note the footnote correcting a linguistic error – details like this matter in iftaa practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب الثامن : نظرية الأهلية والولاية
الحمل ولو ولد حياً. ومثل ذلك ما لو وهبه شيئاً فإنَّ الهبة لا تصح ولا يملك الجنين المال الموهوب ؛ وإنما تثبت للجنين الحقوق الأربعة التي تقدم بيانها فقط للضرورة (ر: ف ٣/٦٠ - ٤) .
وفي فقه الشيعة الزيدية ما يفيد ثبوت الولاية على الجنين في النفس وفي المال. فقد نصوا في كتبهم، كشرح الأزهار» وغيره، على جواز تزويج الجنين !! وفي هذا من الغلو ما ينبو عنه الفكر التشريعي.
على أن إثبات ولاية مالية فقط على مال الجنين له وجه شرعي، لأن المال الذي يكون محجوزاً للجنين يحتاج إلى حفظ وإدارة. ومن ثم شرعت الحكومة المصرية في قانون المجالس الحسبية (وهي تقابل مجالس الأيتام المؤلفة برئاسة قضاة الشرع لدينا) جواز تعيين وصيّ للحمل المستكن. وقد عللت وزارة العدل المصرية ذلك في منشورها الصادر في ٢١ من كانون الأول سنة ۱۹۱۱ م بأنّ عدم تعيين وصيّ للحمل فيه ضرر بمصلحته، لما يترتب عليه من ترك أمواله بلا حصر وتأجير (۱) وقبض وغير ذلك من الأمور الوقتية التي تقتضي السرعة .

Chapter Eight: The Theory of Capacity and Guardianship. The fetus, even if born alive. And similar to that is if one gives it a gift: the gift is not valid and the fetus does not own the gifted property; rather, only the four rights previously explained are established for the fetus, due to necessity. In the fiqh of the Zaydi Shia, there is what indicates the establishment of guardianship over the fetus in both person and property. They have stated in their books, such as Sharh al-Azhar and others, the permissibility of marrying the fetus!! In this there is an extremeness from which legal thought recoils. However, establishing only financial guardianship over the property of the fetus has a legitimate basis, because the property that is reserved for the fetus needs preservation and management. Hence, the Egyptian government legislated in the Law of the Hasba Councils (which correspond to the orphan councils composed under the leadership of our Sharia judges) the permissibility of appointing a guardian for the hidden fetus. The Egyptian Ministry of Justice justified this in its circular issued on December 21, 1911 AD, that not appointing a guardian for the fetus harms its interest, due to the consequent leaving of its property without inventory, leasing, collection, and other time-sensitive matters that require urgency. In this, as Professor Sheikh Ahmad Ibrahim Bik says, it is a good precaution that fiqh principles accept.
Source pages p. 796

Commentary
This passage defines the legal status of the fetus in Hanafi fiqh, beginning with a ruling: if someone gifts something to a fetus, the gift is invalid because the fetus does not own property (it is not yet a legal person). The fetus only has four specific rights (which were detailed earlier, likely inheritance, bequest, etc.) established by necessity. The author contrasts this with the Zaydi Shia position, which extends guardianship to allow marrying off a fetus—an extreme view. The Hanafi perspective restricts financial guardianship, but the author argues that appointing a guardian for the fetus's property is justified to prevent harm (the principle of darar). He cites the Egyptian law of 1911 that allowed such guardianship, and the Syrian personal status law of 1953 (where he was a committee member) also adopted this. The footnote corrects a common linguistic error: 'تأجير' should be 'إيجار' from 'آجر'. The passage thus clarifies the limits of fetal rights and the basis for appointing a property guardian.

Key Terms
الجنين (al-janin): The unborn child in the womb; has limited legal rights in Hanafi fiqh.
الولاية على الجنين (al-wilayah 'ala al-janin): Guardianship over the fetus; in Hanafi law, only financial guardianship is allowed, and only by appointing a guardian for its property.
الضرر يزال (al-darar yuzal): A major legal maxim: harm must be removed. Used here to justify appointing a guardian for the fetus's property to prevent financial loss.
الضرورة (al-darurah): Necessity; the factor that permits limited exceptions to the general rule of no fetal capacity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view in this passage, if someone gives a gift to a fetus, what is the ruling?
A) The gift is valid and the fetus owns it.
B) The gift is invalid and the fetus does not own the property.
C) The gift is valid only if the fetus is born alive.
D) The gift is valid but the guardian manages it.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school grants the fetus only four specific rights by necessity; gifts are invalid, but a property guardian may be appointed to prevent harm under the maxim 'al-darar yuzal'.

Critique of Traditional Impediments to Legal Capacity
Critique of traditional listing of impediments to legal capacity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate whether a given condition qualifies as a true impediment to legal capacity (āriḍ al-ahliyyah) in the Hanafi usul framework, distinguishing it from conditions that merely affect specific obligations.

Core Idea
Not every condition that affects a ruling is an impediment to legal capacity.

Traditional Hanafi usul works list numerous states as impediments to legal capacity. In this passage, a contemporary author challenges that list, arguing that many are merely exceptional rulings that affect obligations, not the underlying capacity. This lesson will clarify the proper scope of 'awāriḍ al-ahliyyah and the implications for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's claim
Read the passage and note the author's central thesis: the nine additional states (infancy, forgetfulness, death, menstruation, postpartum bleeding, travel, ignorance, error, jest) are not true impediments to legal capacity.
He acknowledges they may affect obligations but insists they do not alter capacity itself.
2
Distinguish capacity from obligation
Understand the difference between a condition that affects the capacity to acquire rights and obligations (ahliyyah al-wujūb/al-adā') and one that merely excuses from a specific obligation (e.g., travel exemptions). True impediments directly impact capacity; the others only adjust rulings.
For example, travel does not make one incapable of fasting; it allows a concession.
3
Examine the rejected categories
List the five heavenly (samāwiyya) and four acquired (muktasaba) conditions the author excludes. Note that they appear in standard usul works but the author argues they do not belong under 'awāriḍ al-ahliyyah.
Heavenly: ṣighar (infancy), nisyān (forgetfulness), mawt (death), ḥayḍ (menstruation), nifās (postpartum). Acquired: safar (travel), jahl (ignorance), khaṭa' (error), hazl (jest).
4
Apply the definition of 'āriḍ al-ahliyyah
Using the author's implied definition (directly affecting capacity itself), assess each of the nine conditions. Why would each fail? For example, death ends capacity, but traditionally it's listed as an impediment; the author may consider it a termination, not an impediment. Infancy: partial capacity exists, so it is a state of insufficient capacity, not an impediment per se.
The author does not give full reasoning here but promises to elaborate.
5
Evaluate for fatwa application
Consider how a mufti should apply this refined understanding. When encountering a condition that might excuse a ruling, determine if it truly affects capacity or merely provides a concession. The latter should not be cited as an 'āriḍ al-ahliyyah.
This affects how fatwas are structured: avoid conflating 'āriḍ al-ahliyyah with other legal excuses.

Source Text

الملحق الأول بالباب الثامن
مآخذ على العرض الفقهي التقليدي لعوارض الأهلية ١١/٦٦ - اعتاد المؤلفون في أصول الفقه أن يذكروا في جملة عوارض الأهلية حالات أخرى كثيرة غير التي ذكرناها هنا. فهم يضيفون على العوارض السماوية غير ما تقدم خمسة أخرى هي: الصغر، والنسيان والموت، وفي النساء خاصة الحيض والنفاس . ويضيفون على العوارض المكتسبة أربعة أخرى هي: السفر والجهل، والخطأ، والهزل.
والباحثون المعاصرون الذين كتبوا في الأهلية كالأستاذ العلامة الشيخ أحمد إبراهيم رحمه الله قد تابعوا هذا العرض التقليدي المأثور لنظرية الأهليات وعوارضها ونقلوا أحكام هذه الحالات الأخرى على أنها جميعاً من جملة عوارض الأهلية .
ولكننا نرى أن هذه الحالات التسع الأخرى لا يصح أن تعد من عوارض الأهلية في شيء، وإن كان لها تأثير منعي في بعض التكاليف الشرعية، وأحكام استثنائية.
فلذا طويناها واقتصرنا على ما ذكرنا من الحالات، لأنها هي وحدها التي يصح أن تسمى عوارض على أهلية الأشخاص.
ويحسن أن نوضح هنا ملاحظاتنا على كل من هذه الحالات التي طويناها، ليظهر للقارىء أنها ليس فيها معنى عوارض الأهلية، فلا يصح أن تحشر معها :
First Appendix to Chapter Eight
Objections to the Traditional Fiqh Presentation of Impediments to Legal Capacity 11/66 - The authors of usul al-fiqh are accustomed to mentioning, among the impediments to legal capacity, many other states besides those we have listed here. Thus they add to the heavenly impediments, besides what has preceded, five others: infancy, forgetfulness, death, and specifically for women menstruation and postpartum bleeding. And they add to the acquired impediments four others: travel, ignorance, error, and jest.
Contemporary researchers who have written on legal capacity, like the erudite scholar Sheikh Ahmad Ibrahim (may Allah have mercy on him), have followed this traditional inherited presentation of the theory of legal capacities and their impediments and have transmitted the rulings of these other states as all being part of the impediments to legal capacity.
But we hold that these other nine states are not valid to be considered impediments to legal capacity at all, even though they have a preventive effect on some legal obligations and are exceptional rulings.
Therefore, we have omitted them and restricted ourselves to the states we have mentioned, because only these are valid to be called impediments to the legal capacity of persons.
It is appropriate that we clarify here our observations on each of these states we have omitted, so that it becomes clear to the reader that they do not contain the meaning of impediments to legal capacity, so they should not be lumped with them:
Source pages p. 801

Commentary
This appendix is a critical refinement of the Hanafi theory of ‘awāriḍ al-ahliyyah (impediments to legal capacity). The author challenges the traditional classification inherited from usul al-fiqh works. Traditionally, authors list numerous conditions as temporary impediments to capacity: from heaven (like infancy, forgetfulness, death, menstruation, postpartum bleeding) and acquired (like travel, ignorance, error, jest). The author argues that these nine conditions are not true impediments to capacity itself. Rather, they may affect the obligation of certain acts (e.g., travel may suspend some obligations, ignorance may excuse) but they do not change the person's underlying capacity for rights and obligations. True impediments are those directly affecting the capacity to acquire rights or bear obligations, such as insanity, minority (in a certain sense), or death (though death ends it, not impedes). The author states he will later detail observations on each, but this passage just sets up the objection. This is a rasm rule about what properly qualifies as an impediment to capacity.

Key Terms
عوارض الأهلية (‘awāriḍ al-ahliyyah): Temporary or permanent conditions that affect a person's legal capacity (ahliyyah), such as insanity, unconsciousness, or being legally interdicted (ḥajr). The author restricts this to conditions directly impacting capacity, not merely affecting specific obligations.
عوارض سماوية (‘awāriḍ samāwiyya): Impediments not caused by human action, such as infancy, forgetfulness, death, menstruation, postpartum bleeding. In traditional works, these affect capacity; the author excludes most of them except a narrower set.
عوارض مكتسبة (‘awāriḍ muktasaba): Impediments that arise from human choice, such as travel, ignorance, error, jest. The author denies they are true impediments to capacity, though they may have special rulings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Travel (safar)
B) Forgetfulness (nisyān)
C) Infancy (ṣighar) as traditionally listed
D) None of the nine listed states qualify; the author only hints that his own list (not fully reproduced here) is correct.

Key Takeaway
True impediments to legal capacity are only those conditions that directly affect the capacity itself, not those that merely provide exceptions to obligations.

History and Development of Fiqh

8 min
The Gradual Formation of General Legal Maxims
Historical sketch of maxim formation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the historical process by which the general legal maxims (qawa'id kulliyya) were formulated in the Hanafi school and identify the key sources from which they were derived.

Core Idea
The general legal maxims were not enacted by individuals but emerged gradually through the collective reasoning of senior jurists over centuries.

This lesson explores how the foundational legal maxims of Islamic jurisprudence came to be. Understanding their organic, scholarly development is crucial for the mufti, who must apply these maxims not as rigid codes but as flexible principles rooted in the tradition. The passage from al-Zarqa highlights the gradual formation through takhrij and tarjih.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the non-legislative origin
Contrast the gradual formation of maxims with modern statutory legislation. Maxims were not enacted at a single time by known individuals.
This counters the assumption that maxims are like codified laws.
2
Identify the formulators
Senior jurists (kibar fuqaha) from the major schools, known for takhrij and tarjih, were responsible. They deduced maxims from legislative texts, usul al-fiqh, legal causes (ilal), and rational principles.
Their expertise in derivation and preference was key.
3
Distinguish types of maxims
Some maxims are direct prophetic hadith (e.g., 'La darar wa la dirar') or statements from imams (e.g., Abu Yusuf's principle). Most were refined through circulation (tadaawul) and analogical reasoning.
Only a few maxims can be attributed to specific individuals.
4
Understand the role of ta'lil and istidlal
The justifications (ta'lil) for ijtihadi rulings and methods of qiyas provided the material from which maxims were abstracted and polished.
This occurred after the stabilization of the major schools.

لمحة تاريخية عن صياغة القواعد الكلية

هذا، ولا يعرف لكل قاعدة صائغ معين من الفقهاء إلا ما كان منها نص حديث نبوي كقاعدة لا ضرر ولا ضرار أو ما أثر عن بعض أئمة المذاهب وكبار أتباعهم من عبارات جرت بعد ذلك مجرى القواعد، كقول أبي يوسف صاحب أبي حنيفة في كتاب الخراج» الذي وضعه للرشيد: ليس للإمام أن يخرج شيئاً من يد أحد إلا بحق ثابت معروف».

Chapter Eighty: A Historical Glimpse at the Formulation of General Maxims. 1/80 - Indeed, the inherited general maxims in Islamic jurisprudence were not all set down at once as legal texts are set at a specific time by known individuals. Rather, their concepts were formed and their texts were formulated gradually during the eras of the flourishing and rise of fiqh, by the senior jurists of the schools who were experts in derivation and preference, deduced from the indications of general legislative texts, principles of usul al-fiqh, legal causes, and rational determinations. Moreover, it is not known for each maxim a specific formulator among the jurists, except what is a text of a prophetic hadith like the maxim 'No harm and no reciprocation of harm' or what is transmitted from some imams of the schools and their senior followers as expressions that later became current as maxims, like the statement of Abu Yusuf, the companion of Abu Hanifa, in his book al-Kharaj which he wrote for al-Rashid: 'The Imam has no right to remove anything from anyone's hand except by a proven known right.' Reference: al-Kharaj, section on revival of dead lands, Radd al-Muhtar 2/257. As for most of those maxims, they acquired their final inherited formulation through circulation, polishing, and modification at the hands of senior jurists of the schools in the field of legal reasoning and evidence. For the justifications of ijtihadi fiqh rulings and the methods of analogical reasoning upon them were the greatest source for establishing these maxims and refining their formulations, after the stabilization of the major legal schools and the turning of their senior followers to...
Source pages p. 904

Commentary
This passage is a historical sketch of how the general legal maxims (qawa'id kulliyya) in Islamic fiqh developed. The author emphasizes that these maxims were not enacted at once like modern legislation; rather, they evolved gradually through the work of leading jurists who derived them from the Qur'an, Sunnah, usul al-fiqh, legal causes (ilal), and rational principles. He notes that specific authors of maxims are generally unknown except in cases where the maxim itself is a hadith (e.g., 'La darar wa la dirar') or a statement from an imam that became a maxim, such as Abu Yusuf's principle about the imam's authority over property. The passage highlights that most maxims were refined through debate and analogical reasoning in the post-formative period of the schools. This underscores the organic, scholarly nature of the maxims' development.

Key Terms
تخريج (takhrij): The process of deriving rulings from established principles or texts.
ترجيح (tarjih): Preference among conflicting evidences or opinions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the origin of most general legal maxims?
A) They were all directly taken from the Qur'an and Sunnah.
B) They were formulated by a single scholar at a specific time.
C) They were developed gradually through the reasoning and analogical deduction of senior jurists.
D) They were introduced by later governments.

Key Takeaway
General legal maxims were not enacted but evolved organically through the cumulative ijtihad of senior jurists, drawing from texts, usul, and legal reasoning.

History and Development of Fiqh

8 min
Historical Development of Hanafi Legal Maxims
Historical narrative of the formulation of legal maxims

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the gradual process of formulating Hanafi legal maxims and identify the earliest compilers and their works.

Core Idea
Hanafi legal maxims evolved organically through centuries of juristic reasoning, not by a single act of codification.

This passage traces how the universal legal maxims (qawa'id kulliyya) that underlie Hanafi fiqh were not enacted like modern statutes. Instead, they emerged over generations through the ta'lil (causal reasoning) of senior jurists. Understanding this history helps you appreciate the authority and flexibility of these maxims in fatwa and ijtihad today.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the organic development
Realize that the maxims were not compiled at once but formed gradually from the indications of primary texts, usul al-fiqh, and rational reasoning.
This is unlike modern legal codes.
2
Identify the Hanafi precedence
Note the passage's claim that the Hanafi school, being the oldest, pioneered the formulation of these maxims, and other schools transmitted from them.
3
Note the earliest compilations
Learn that Abu Tahir al-Dabbas (3rd-4th c. AH) compiled 17 maxims, repeated nightly; also al-Karkhi's and al-Dabusi's works are mentioned.
4
Trace the evolution of a maxim
Examine the example of the acknowledgement maxim: from al-Karkhi's wording to the refined version in the Majalla, 'al-iqrar hujja qasira.'
This shows refinement over time.

·
وكانت هذه القواعد تسمى أصولاً كما قال القرافي آنفاً. فكثيراً ما نرى شُرَّاح المذهب في تعليلات الأحكام ونرى المؤلفين في القواعد، يقولون من أصول أبي حنيفة، أو الأصل عند أبي حنيفة كذا وكذا ويذكرون بعض هذه القواعد، كما يرى في كتاب «تأسيس النظر» للدبوسي، وفي قواعد الكرخي الآتي ذكرهما.
۳/۸۰ - ولعل أقدم خبر يروى عن جمع القواعد الكلية في المذهب الحنفي مصوغة بصيغها الفقهية المأثورة ما رواه العلامة ابن نجيم في مقدمة كتابه «الأشباه والنظائر» من أنَّ الإمام أبا طاهر الدباس - وهو ممن عاش في القرنين الثالث والرابع للهجرة كما سنرى - قد أهم قواعد مذهب أبي حنيفة في سبع عشرة قاعدة كلية. وكان أبو طاهر ضريراً يكرّر كل ليلة تلك القواعد بمسجده بعد خروج الناس منه. وذكر ابن نجيم أن أبا سعيد الهروي الشافعي قد رحل إلى أبي طاهر ونقل عنه
(۱)
جمع
ومن دلائل هذا التطور في الصياغة الفقهية الفنية للقواعد أنَّ القاعدة المشهورة الأساسية في كون الإقرار إنما يلزم صاحبه المقر ولا يسري حكمه على غيره، نرى نصها المتداول في كتب المتأخرين وفي المادة / ۷۸ من المجلة هو : «الإقرار حجة قاصرة بينما أن أصل هذه القاعدة في قواعد الإمام الكرخي التي سيأتي ذكرها
التالي :
بالنص
الأصل: أن المرء يعامل في حق نفسه كما أقر به ولا يصدق على إبطال حق الغير ولا بإلزام الغير حقاً».
وهكذا كثير من القواعد المأثورة إذا قورنت نصوصها الأخيرة بأصولها القديمة.
٩٧٠
Chapter Eighty: A Historical Glimpse at the Formulation of the Universal Maxims
1/80 - Indeed, the transmitted universal maxims in Islamic jurisprudence were not all laid down as a single whole, as legal texts are laid down at a specific time by known people. Rather, their concepts were formed and their texts were formulated gradually during the eras of the flourishing and revival of fiqh at the hands of the senior jurists of the schools, those qualified for extraction and preference, derived from the indications of general legislative texts, the principles of usul al-fiqh, the causes of rulings, and rational determinations.
Thus, no specific formulator is known for each maxim, except for those that are texts of prophetic hadith, such as the maxim 'No harm and no reciprocating harm,' or what has been transmitted from some imams of the schools and their senior followers of phrases that later became current as maxims, such as the statement of Abu Yusuf, the companion of Abu Hanifa, in his book 'al-Kharaj' which he wrote for al-Rashid: 'The imam may not remove anything from anyone's hand except with a known established right.'
Ref: 'al-Kharaj, section on reviving dead lands, Radd al-Muhtar 2/257).
As for most of those maxims, they acquired their final transmitted formulation through circulation, refinement, and modification at the hands of senior jurists of the schools in the field of causation and reasoning. For the causal explanations of ijtihadic legal rulings and the paths of analogical reasoning upon them were the greatest source for formulating these maxims and solidifying their structures, after the stabilization of the major juristic schools and the attention of their senior followers to
:
Chapter Ten: The Universal Maxim in Fiqh
refining them and arranging their principles and evidences (1) (see: F 2/13).
2/80 - And it appears that the Hanafi school, which is the oldest of the four major schools, had its higher strata of jurists precede others in formulating those universal juristic principles in the form of maxims and arguing by them, and from them scholars of other schools transmitted whatever they wished.
·
And these maxims used to be called 'principles' as al-Qarafi said earlier. For often we see commentators of the school in their causal explanations, and authors in maxims, saying 'from the principles of Abu Hanifa' or 'the principle according to Abu Hanifa is such and such,' and they mention some of these maxims, as can be seen in the book 'Ta'sis al-Nazar' by al-Dabusi, and in the maxims of al-Karkhi which will be mentioned.
3/80 - And perhaps the oldest report transmitted about the collection of universal maxims in the Hanafi school, formulated in their transmitted juristic expressions, is what was related by the scholar Ibn Nujaym in the introduction to his book 'al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir': that Imam Abu Tahir al-Dabbas - who lived in the third and fourth centuries AH as we will see - included the maxims of the school of Abu Hanifa in seventeen universal maxims. And Abu Tahir was blind, and he used to repeat those maxims every night in his mosque after people left. And Ibn Nujaym mentioned that Abu Sa'id al-Harawi al-Shafi'i traveled to Abu Tahir and transmitted from him.
(1)
Collection
And among the evidence of this development in the technical juristic formulation of maxims is that the famous basic maxim concerning that an acknowledgement only binds the acknowledger himself and does not extend to others, we see its common text in the books of the later scholars and in Article 78 of the Majalla is: 'Acknowledgement is a confined proof,' whereas the origin of this maxim in the maxims of Imam al-Karkhi which will be mentioned
following:
With the text: 'The principle: that a person is treated with regard to his own right as he acknowledged, and he is not believed with regard to nullifying the right of another nor to imposing a right on another.'
And so it is with many transmitted maxims when their later texts are compared to their ancient origins.
970
Source pages p. 904

Commentary
This passage is a historical overview of how the universal legal maxims (القواعد الكلية) in Islamic jurisprudence were formulated. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, explains that these maxims were not codified all at once like modern legal codes, but developed gradually over centuries through the work of senior jurists (especially from the Hanafi school) who derived them from the general indications of primary texts (Quran and Sunnah), the principles of usul al-fiqh, the causes behind rulings (علل الأحكام), and rational reasoning. The passage then notes that while some maxims originate directly from prophetic hadith (e.g., 'لا ضرر ولا ضرار') or from statements of early imams (like Abu Yusuf's statement in his book al-Kharaj), most maxims attained their final form through circulation and refinement by later jurists. The Hanafi school, being the oldest, is highlighted as a pioneer in formulating these maxims, which were often referred to as 'principles' (أصول). The passage cites early collections: the Ta'sis al-Nazar of al-Dabusi and the maxims of al-Karkhi, and mentions the earliest known compilation by Abu Tahir al-Dabbas, who blind, would repeat 17 maxims nightly. Finally, the author illustrates the evolution of specific maxims, such as the one about the scope of an acknowledgement (الإقرار حجة قاصرة), showing how its formulation became more refined over time. The aim is to demonstrate that legal maxims are a product of historical development within the madhhab, not a single piece of legislation.

Key Terms
قواعد كلية (qawaʿid kulliyya): Universal legal maxims that are general principles derived from the sources of Islamic law and applied across various fiqh chapters.
تعليلات الأحكام (taʿlilāt al-aḥkām): The causal reasoning behind legal rulings, which serves as a basis for formulating maxims.
أصول (uṣūl): In this context, it refers to the universal maxims themselves, called 'principles' of the madhhab.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Ibn Nujaym's al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir with 100 maxims

Key Takeaway
Hanafi legal maxims developed gradually through the ta'lil of senior jurists, with the earliest compilation by Abu Tahir al-Dabbas (3rd-4th c. AH) containing 17 maxims.

History and Development of Fiqh

Historical Development of Hanafi Legal Maxims
Historical overview of maxim compilation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the earliest compilers of Hanafi legal maxims, state their contributions, and evaluate the historical reliability of transmission reports regarding the five major maxims.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school pioneered universal legal maxims; al-Dabbaas collected 17, al-Karkhi expanded to 37, and five became foundational across madhahib.

This lesson traces the roots of Hanafi legal maxims (al-qawāʿid al-kulliyya) from their earliest formulations by the school's leading jurists. You will learn how these principles were first compiled, transmitted, and expanded, and why they remain central to Hanafi legal reasoning today.

Guided Path
1
Identify the earliest known compiler
Locate the name of the Hanafi jurist who first collected universal maxims and the number he compiled (17). Note his practice of nightly repetition.
Abu Tahir al-Dabbaas is key.
2
Trace the chain of transmission and expansion
Follow how al-Karkhi later added to al-Dabbaas's collection, resulting in 37 maxims. Observe the cross-madhhab interest (e.g., al-Harawi's journey).
Consider the historical reliability of Ibn Nujaym's account.
3
Recognize the five major maxims
List the five maxims mentioned as 'mothers of maxims' and note that they are accepted beyond the Hanafi school.
Memorize these: intentions, harm, custom, certainty, hardship.
4
Evaluate the historical evidence
Analyze the footnote on Ibn Nujaym's narration: why are details considered improbable but the core plausible? Consider the cross-references to al-Suyuti and Ibn al-Humam.
This builds critical historical skills.
