الباب العاشر : القواعد الكلية في الفقه تحريرها وترتيب أصولها وأدلتها (۱) (ر: ف ۲/۱۳). ۲/۸۰ - والظاهر أنَّ المذهب الحنفي، وهو أقدم المذاهب الأربعة الكبرى، قد كانت الطبقات العليا من فقهائه أسبق إلى صياغة تلك المبادىء الفقهية الكلية في صيغ قواعد والاحتجاج بها وعنهم نقل رجال المذاهب الأخرى ما شاؤوا منها. · وكانت هذه القواعد تسمى أصولاً كما قال القرافي آنفاً. فكثيراً ما نرى شُرَّاح المذهب في تعليلات الأحكام ونرى المؤلفين في القواعد، يقولون من أصول أبي حنيفة، أو الأصل عند أبي حنيفة كذا وكذا ويذكرون بعض هذه القواعد، كما يرى في كتاب «تأسيس النظر» للدبوسي، وفي قواعد الكرخي الآتي ذكرهما. ۳/۸۰ - ولعل أقدم خبر يروى عن جمع القواعد الكلية في المذهب الحنفي مصوغة بصيغها الفقهية المأثورة ما رواه العلامة ابن نجيم في مقدمة كتابه «الأشباه والنظائر» من أنَّ الإمام أبا طاهر الدباس - وهو ممن عاش في القرنين الثالث والرابع للهجرة كما سنرى - قد أهم قواعد مذهب أبي حنيفة في سبع عشرة قاعدة كلية. وكان أبو طاهر ضريراً يكرّر كل ليلة تلك القواعد بمسجده بعد خروج الناس منه. وذكر ابن نجيم أن أبا سعيد الهروي الشافعي قد رحل إلى أبي طاهر ونقل عنه بعض هذه القواعد (۱) ، ومن جملتها القواعد الخمس التي تعتبر أمهات القواعد ومباني الأحكام الشرعية من نصية واجتهادية، وهي: ١ - الأمور بمقاصدها ۲ - الضّررُ يُزال ۳ - العادةُ مُحَكَمةٌ ٤ - اليقين يزول بالشك ٥ - المشقة تجلب التيسير (۲) ٤/٨٠ ـ وأقدم مجموعة من مجموعة من هذه القواعد الكلية وصلت إلينا في شكل رسالة هي قواعد الإمام أبي الحسن الكرخي، وقد شرحها وأوضحها بالأمثلة الإمام نجم الدين أبو حفص عمر النَّسَفي الحنفي المتوفى سنة ٥٣٧ للهجرة (۳) والظاهر أن الكرخي قد أخذ القواعد التي جمعها أبو طاهر الدباس وأضاف إليها، فقد جاءت مجموعة الكرخي بسبع وثلاثين قاعدة، بينما رأينا آنفاً فيما رواه ابن نجيم أن القواعد التي جمعها الإمام الدباس كانت سبع عشرة . (۱) يروي ابن نجيم قصة هذه الرحلة والنقل بصورة مستبعدة لا تصح تفاصيلها. ولكن الظاهر أن لها أصلاً ثابتاً، إذ رواها الجلال السيوطي الشافعي في كتابه «الأشباه والنظائر (ص/ ٤ - ٥ ) ، والكمال ابن الهمام الحنفي في فتح القدير». ر: مقدمة الأشباه لابن نجيم وحاشية الحموي عليها). (۲) وقد نظمها بعض الشافعية مشيراً إلى أساسيتها في مذهبهم أيضاً بقوله : خمس مقررة قواعد مذهب للشافعي فكن بهن خبيرا ضرر يزال، وعادة قد حكمت وكذا المشقة تجلب التيسيرا والشك لا ترفع به متیقناً والقصد أخلص إن أردت أجورا (۳) الإمامان الكرخي والدباس كانا متعاصرين، وكلاهما من أكابر فقهاء الطبقات العليا في المذهب الحنفي، ولكن الكرخي أكثر شهرة وذكراً. فالكرخي: هو الإمام أبو الحسن عبيد الله بن الحسين الكرخي، نسبة إلى كرخ العراق ولد سنة / ٢٦٠ هـ، وتوفي سنة / ٣٤٠ هـ، وانتهت إليه رئاسة المذهب الحنفي. · والدباس : هو الإمام أبو طاهر محمد بن محمد الدباس كان إمام أهل الرأي في العراق حفاظاً خبيرا بالروايات ومن أقران الكرخي. وقد ولي القضاء بالشام. (ر: الفوائد البهية في تراجم الحنفية للشيخ عبد الحي اللكنوي ص / ۱۰۹ و ۱۸۷).
Chapter Ten: The Universal Legal Maxims in Fiqh: Their Formulation, Arrangement of Their Principles and Proofs (1) (See: F 2/13). 2/80 - It appears that the Hanafi school, which is the oldest of the four major schools, had its higher-ranking jurists precede others in formulating those universal juristic principles into the form of maxims and arguing by them, and from them the men of other schools transmitted whatever they wished. · These maxims were called 'principles' as al-Qarafi said earlier. So we often see commentators of the school in their legal justifications and authors on maxims saying: 'From the principles of Abu Hanifa,' or 'The principle according to Abu Hanifa is such and such,' and they mention some of these maxims, as seen in the book 'Ta'sis al-Nazar' by al-Dabusi and in the maxims of al-Karkhi to be mentioned later. 3/80 - Perhaps the earliest report about the collection of universal maxims in the Hanafi school, formulated in their transmitted juristic expressions, is what al-Allamah Ibn Nujaym narrated in the introduction of his book al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir: that Imam Abu Tahir al-Dabbaas - who lived in the third and fourth centuries AH as we will see - collected the maxims of the Hanafi school into seventeen universal maxims. Abu Tahir was blind and would repeat those maxims every night in his mosque after the people had left. Ibn Nujaym mentioned that Abu Sa'id al-Harawi al-Shafi'i traveled to Abu Tahir and transmitted from him some of these maxims (1), among them the five maxims that are considered the mothers of maxims and the foundations of legal rulings, both textual and jurisprudential, namely: 1. Matters are determined by their objectives, 2. Harm must be removed, 3. Custom is authoritative, 4. Certainty is not overruled by doubt, 5. Hardship brings ease (2). 4/80 - The oldest collection of these universal maxims that has reached us in the form of a treatise is the maxims of Imam Abu al-Hasan al-Karkhi, which were explained and clarified with examples by Imam Najm al-Din Abu Hafs Umar al-Nasafi al-Hanafi, who died in 537 AH (3). It appears that al-Karkhi took the maxims collected by Abu Tahir al-Dabbaas and added to them, for al-Karkhi's collection came to thirty-seven maxims, while we saw earlier, according to Ibn Nujaym, that the maxims collected by Imam al-Dabbaas were seventeen. (1) Ibn Nujaym narrates the story of this journey and transmission in an improbable manner, the details of which are not authentic. However, it seems to have a solid basis, as it was narrated by Jalal al-Suyuti al-Shafi'i in his book al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir (pp. 4-5) and by Kamal Ibn al-Humam al-Hanafi in Fath al-Qadir. See: Introduction of al-Ashbah by Ibn Nujaym and its gloss by al-Hamawi. (2) Some Shafi'is have versified them, indicating their fundamentality in their school as well, saying: 'Five established maxims for the school of al-Shafi'i, so be well-versed in them: harm is removed, custom is authoritative, hardship brings ease, doubt does not remove certainty, and purify your intention if you desire rewards.' (3) The two Imams al-Karkhi and al-Dabbaas were contemporaries, both among the great jurists of the higher classes of the Hanafi school, but al-Karkhi is more famous and mentioned. Al-Karkhi: Imam Abu al-Hasan Ubayd Allah ibn al-Husayn al-Karkhi, attributed to Karkh of Iraq, born in 260 AH, died in 340 AH, and leadership of the Hanafi school ended with him. · Al-Dabbaas: Imam Abu Tahir Muhammad ibn Muhammad al-Dabbaas, was the imam of the people of opinion in Iraq, a preserver and expert in narrations, and a peer of al-Karkhi. He served as judge in Syria. (See: al-Fawa'id al-Bahiyya fi Tarajim al-Hanafiyya by Shaykh Abd al-Hayy al-Laknawi, pp. 109 and 187).
Source pages p. 905

Commentary
This passage is a historical introduction to the chapter on universal legal maxims. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, explains that the Hanafi school was a pioneer in formulating universal juristic principles as formal legal maxims, and that other schools later adopted many of these. He notes that these maxims were originally called 'usul' (principles). The earliest known compilation is attributed to Abu Tahir al-Dabbaas, a blind Hanafi jurist of the 3rd-4th centuries AH, who collected 17 maxims and would recite them nightly. A Shafi'i scholar, Abu Sa'id al-Harawi, traveled to him to learn them. Among these were the five foundational maxims (al-qawa'id al-khams al-kubra) that underpin much of Islamic jurisprudence: intentions determine outcomes, harm must be removed, custom is authoritative, certainty is not removed by doubt, and hardship brings ease. Al-Zarqa then mentions that a later collection by Abu al-Hasan al-Karkhi expanded this to 37 maxims, showing the growth of the genre. He also cites a verse by Shafi'i scholars acknowledging these same five maxims, indicating cross-madhhab acceptance. The footnotes provide critical historical evaluation: Ibn Nujaym's story about al-Harawi is considered weak in details but has a core of truth, and biographical details of al-Karkhi and al-Dabbaas are given. This passage sets the stage for the detailed study of qawa'id by tracing their origins and highlighting the leading role of early Hanafi jurists.

Key Terms
القواعد الكلية (al-qawāʿid al-kulliyya): Universal legal maxims that encompass many specific rulings and are used as principles for deducing and justifying fiqh rules.
أمهات القواعد (ummahāt al-qawāʿid): The five major maxims from which many other maxims branch, considered the foundational mothers of legal rules.
الطبقات العليا (al-ṭabaqāt al-ʿulyā): The highest-ranking classes of Hanafi jurists, those who formulated the early maxiological framework.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's early formulation of universal maxims, from al-Dabbaas to al-Karkhi, laid the foundation for a systematic legal methodology that influences fatwa practice today.

History and Development of Fiqh

Early Hanafi Qawaid: Maxims or Methodological Directives?
Historical overview of early qawaid and their nature

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between al-Karkhi's methodological directives and substantive qawaid, and explain the contribution of al-Dabusi to the development of qawaid and comparative fiqh.

Core Idea
Some early Hanafi 'rules' are methods for reconciling texts, not substantive maxims; al-Dabusi systematized qawaid and comparative fiqh.

This passage traces the early development of qawaid in the Hanafi school. You will see that scholars like al-Karkhi articulated principles that served as hermeneutic tools for interpreting texts, while others like al-Dabusi organized substantive maxims for deriving rulings. Understanding this distinction helps you appreciate how the Hanafi school built its legal framework.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of statements in al-Karkhi's rules
Read the example given: al-Karkhi's rule about Quranic verses that contradict the Hanafi view. Is this a substantive legal maxim (e.g., 'no harm, no harassment') or a guideline for interpreting texts?
The passage explicitly says some of his rules are not 'rules' in the defined sense.
2
Distinguish between qawaid and usul al-fiqh
Note the author's statement: qawaid are distinct from usul al-fiqh. Usul al-fiqh is the science of textual interpretation; qawaid are substantive principles that govern multiple fiqh chapters.
Ensure you see the two as complementary but different.
3
Trace the historical timeline
The passage mentions that the codification of qawaid began in the late 3rd century AH. Earlier, these principles were used implicitly by mujtahids. This marks a transition from tacit to explicit formulation.
Think about why explicit codification became necessary.
4
Recognize al-Dabusi's role
Al-Dabusi authored 'Tasis al-Nazar', which included specific fiqh principles and general rules for branching. He is credited with founding 'ilm al-khilaf (comparative fiqh).
His work moved beyond isolated maxims to systematic comparative analysis.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب العاشر : القواعد الكلية في الفقه
ويرى الناظر في قواعد الإمام الكرخي هذه أن بعضاً منها ليس من قبيل القواعد بالمعنى الذي حددناه للقاعدة، وإنما هو من قبيل الأفكار التوجيهية لرجال المذهب في تعليل المسائل، كقول الكرخي مثلاً فيها : «الأصل: أنَّ كل آية تخالف قول أصحابنا فإنها تحمل على النسخ أو
على الترجيح أو على التأويل من جهة التوفيق».
ونستخلص من ذلك أن هذه القواعد وهي مفاهيم ومبادىء فقهية کبری ضابطة لموضوعاتها قد بدأت حركة تقعيدها وتدوينها أواخر القرن الثالث الهجري وأنَّ معانيها الفقهية كانت مقررة لدى الأئمة المجتهدين تعتبر أصولاً عملية لهم يقيسون بها ويبنون عليها ويعللون بها، وإن كانت لم تُفرد بالتدوين قبل ذلك، ولم تأخذ الصياغة التي انتهت إليها فيما بعد إلا بالصقل والتحوير وهي غير أصول الفقه التي هي علم يقرر الطريقة العلمية في تفسير النصوص وفهمها والاستنباط منها.
٥/٨٠ - ثم جاء الإمام أبو زيد عبيد الله بن عمر الدبوسي الحنفي، فوضع كتابه «تأسيس النظر وضَمَّنه طائفة هامة من الضوابط الفقهية الخاصة بموضوع معين، ومن القواعد الكلية التفريع عليها (۱). مع
(1) الدبوسي من أكابر فقهاء المذهب الحنفي كان يُضرب به المثل في النظر والاحتجاج الفقهي. وذكر ابن خلكان أنه أول من وضع علم خلاف الفقهاء. والمراد بعلم خلاف الفقهاء ما نسميه اليوم في اصطلاح علم الحقوق الحديث باسم : الفقه المقارن». فالدبوسي رحمه الله أول من عني بتنظيم البحث في الفقه المقارن بين المذاهب وجعل منه علماً مستقلاً.
وكتابه هذا، تأسيس النظر مطبوع مع قواعد الإمام الكرخي في المطبعة الأدبية
بمصر.
۹۷۲
Chapter Ten: The General Legal Maxims in Fiqh
And one who examines the rules of Imam al-Karkhi sees that some of them are not of the type of 'rules' in the sense we have defined for a rule, but rather they are of the type of directional ideas for the scholars of the madhhab in providing legal reasoning for issues, such as al-Karkhi's statement in them: 'The principle is that every verse that contradicts the statement of our companions is interpreted as abrogated, or given preference [to another interpretation], or interpreted allegorically for the sake of reconciliation.'
And we deduce from this that these rules, which are major fiqh concepts and principles governing their subjects, began their movement of codification and compilation at the end of the third century AH, and that their fiqh meanings were established among the mujtahid imams, considered as practical principles by which they analogize, upon which they build, and with which they provide reasoning, even though they were not individually compiled before that, and they did not take the formulation they later reached except through refinement and modification, and they are distinct from usul al-fiqh, which is a science that determines the scientific method for interpreting texts, understanding them, and deriving [rulings] from them.
80/5 - Then came Imam Abu Zayd 'Ubayd Allah ibn 'Umar al-Dabusi al-Hanafi, and he wrote his book 'Tasis al-Nazar' and included in it an important group of fiqh principles specific to a particular subject, and general rules for deriving branches (1). With
(1) Al-Dabusi is among the great fuqaha of the Hanafi school; he was exemplary in legal reasoning and argumentation. Ibn Khallikan mentioned that he was the first to establish the science of disagreement among fuqaha (ikhilaf al-fuqaha). By 'science of disagreement among fuqaha' is meant what we today call, in modern legal terminology, 'comparative fiqh'. Al-Dabusi, may Allah have mercy on him, was the first to care for organizing research in comparative fiqh between schools and made it an independent science.
His book, 'Tasis al-Nazar', is printed with the rules of Imam al-Karkhi at the Literary Press in Egypt.
972
Source pages p. 907

Commentary
This passage is a historical overview of the development of qawaid (legal maxims) in the Hanafi school. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, explains that some of Imam al-Karkhi's rules are not maxims in the strict sense but guiding principles for scholars to reconcile conflicting evidence. For example, al-Karkhi's rule that any Quranic verse contradicting Hanafi positions must be interpreted as abrogated, preferred, or allegorized is a methodological directive, not a substantive maxim. The author then notes that the actual movement of codifying qawaid began in the late 3rd century AH, and that these principles were already in use by earlier mujtahids as tacit tools. He distinguishes qawaid from usul al-fiqh, which is a formal methodology for interpreting texts. Finally, he introduces Imam al-Dabusi, who wrote 'Tasis al-Nazar', a work that included important fiqh principles and general rules for deriving subsidiary rulings. Al-Dabusi is credited with founding the science of comparative fiqh (ilm al-khilaf). The footnote clarifies al-Dabusi's stature and his pioneering role in systematizing inter-madhhab disputes.

Key Terms
القواعد الكلية (al-qawāʿid al-kulliyyah): General legal maxims that apply across multiple chapters of fiqh, used for deriving and organizing rulings.
أصول الفقه (uṣūl al-fiqh): The science of legal methodology for interpreting primary sources (Qur'an, Sunnah) and deriving rulings.
تأسيس النظر (taʿsīs al-naẓar): The title of al-Dabusi's book, meaning 'Establishing Legal Reasoning', which contains fiqh principles and comparative discussions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Usul al-fiqh is a science of methodology for interpreting texts, while qawaid are substantive principles.
B) Usul al-fiqh is the same as qawaid.
C) Qawaid are derived from usul al-fiqh.
D) Usul al-fiqh is only for the Shafi'i school, while qawaid are for Hanafis.

Key Takeaway
Early Hanafi qawaid include both substantive maxims and methodological directives; al-Dabusi later systematized them and founded comparative fiqh.

Ibn Nujaym and al-Khadimi: Codifying Hanafi Legal Maxims
Historical survey of qawaid codification with critical analysis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the status of the maxim 'No reward except with intention' within the Hanafi qawaid framework and compare the contributions of Ibn Nujaym and al-Khadimi to the codification of legal maxims.

Core Idea
The proposed sixth maxim 'No reward except with intention' is actually a branch of the primary maxim 'Matters are judged by their objectives'.

This lesson traces the historical development of Hanafi legal maxims (qawaid). You will learn how Ibn Nujaym systematized 25 rules and how al-Khadimi expanded them into 154 rules arranged alphabetically. The author critically examines a proposed sixth primary maxim, arguing it is merely a submaxim.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main collections
Note the authors (Ibn Nujaym, al-Khadimi), their works (al-Ashbah wa al-Nazair, Majami' al-Haqaiq), and the number of rules each contains.
Ibn Nujaym: 25 rules (6 basic + 19 subsidiary); al-Khadimi: 154 rules arranged alphabetically.
2
Understand the author's critique
Some scholars add a sixth maxim 'No reward except with intention'. The author argues it is not independent but a branch of 'Matters are judged by their objectives'.
Both are derived from the hadith 'Actions are by intentions'.
3
Analyse the scope of the two maxims
Compare the coverage: 'Matters are judged by their objectives' applies to both worldly legal effects and otherworldly reward, while the proposed maxim is limited to otherworldly reward only.
4
Recognize the features of al-Khadimi's collection
Al-Khadimi's 'Majami' al-Haqaiq' includes guiding principles (like those of al-Karkhi) and substantive maxims, drawing heavily from Ibn Nujaym and arranged alphabetically after al-Zarkashi's method.
The arrangement follows the first letter of the first word of each maxim.

Section (80): A historical glimpse of the formulation of general legal maxims
1- Basic rules, like pillars in the fiqh schools, are six: the five preceding maxims, with a sixth added: 'No reward except with intention (niyya)' (1). 2- Nineteen other rules on various topics of lesser scope and generality, from which some other subsidiary rules and many rulings branch. Ibn Nujaym elaborated on the subsidiary practical rulings that branch from these rules.
Then in the middle of the 12th century AH came the Turkish Hanafi jurist Muhammad Abu Sa'id al-Khadimi, the author of the marginalia on the book 'al-Durar Sharh al-Ghurar' (text), and he wrote in usul al-fiqh a book called 'Majami' al-Haqaiq', and he concluded it with an appendix in which he collected a large group of general fiqh maxims, presented them in their texts without commentary, and arranged them alphabetically according to the first letter of the first word of each, amounting to one hundred and fifty-four rules (2).
Some of them contain rules of the type of guiding principles for the scholars of the madhhab in providing reasoning for issues, like those found in the rules of al-Karkhi. Some are overlapping, and the rest are all fiqh maxims with general constitutional rulings. In them, al-Khadimi took most of what Ibn Nujaym had gathered in 'al-Ashbah' and added to it.
(1) In our view, this rule is not worthy of being added to those five mother maxims; rather, it is a branch under the generality of the maxim 'Matters are judged by their objectives', which suffices for it. Both are derived from the prophetic hadith: 'Actions are by intentions, and each person has what he intended.' However, the maxim 'Matters are judged by their objectives' agrees with the text of this hadith in the generality of the ruling, for each of them includes the otherworldly reward for deeds as well as many results of civil transactions and criminal acts whose worldly judicial rulings differ according to the doer's purpose and intention. As for the maxim 'No reward except with intention', it is specific only to otherworldly reward. (2) 'Majami' al-Haqaiq' by al-Khadimi is printed with a commentary called 'Manafi' al-Haqaiq' at the al-Amira Press in Constantinople, year 1308 AH. His rules also have an independent commentary. Al-Khadimi followed this alphabetical arrangement from a book on maxims by the 'Allamah al-Zarkashi al-Shafi'i (d. 794 AH) called 'al-Manthur fi Tartib al-Qawa'id al-Fiqhiyyah'.
Source pages p. 908

Commentary
This passage continues the historical survey of qawaid codification. The author notes that the five primary maxims (the classical five kulli maxims) are expanded to six by some by adding 'No reward except with intention.' However, the author criticizes this addition, arguing that it is actually a branch of the broader maxim 'Matters are judged by their objectives' (al-umur bi maqasidiha) and does not deserve independent status. He explains that both are derived from the hadith 'Actions are by intentions,' but the broader maxim covers both this-worldly legal effects and otherworldly reward, while the proposed sixth maxim is limited to otherworldly reward.
The passage then discusses Ibn Nujaym (d. 970 AH), who in his 'al-Ashbah wa al-Nazair' presented 25 rules: the six basic ones and nineteen others, with detailed branches. Later, al-Khadimi (d. 1176 AH) compiled 154 rules alphabetically in his 'Majami' al-Haqaiq'. Al-Khadimi's collection includes both guiding principles (like those of al-Karkhi) and substantive maxims, drawing heavily from Ibn Nujaym. The footnote clarifies that al-Khadimi followed the alphabetical arrangement of al-Zarkashi's work in the Shafi'i school.

Key Terms
الاشباه والنظائر (al-ashbāh wa al-naẓāʾir): Ibn Nujaym's work on legal maxims, containing 25 rules with practical branches.
مجامع الحقائق (majāmiʿ al-ḥaqāʾiq): Al-Khadimi's compilation of 154 general fiqh maxims arranged alphabetically.
لا ثواب إلا بالنية (lā thawāb illā bi al-niyyah): The proposed sixth maxim: 'No reward except with intention', limited to otherworldly reward.
الأمور بمقاصدها (al-umūr bi maqāṣidihā): The primary maxim 'Matters are judged by their objectives', covering both worldly and otherworldly intentions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) It is already covered by the maxim 'Matters are judged by their objectives'.
C) It applies only to transactions, not worship.
D) It contradicts the earlier five maxims.

Key Takeaway
The codification of Hanafi qawaid evolved from Ibn Nujaym's 25 rules to al-Khadimi's 154 alphabetized maxims, and the proposed sixth maxim is best understood as a subset of the broader principle 'Matters are judged by their objectives'.

History and Development of Fiqh
intermediate
10 min
The Historical Development of Hanafi Legal Maxims
Historical survey of Hanafi legal maxim compilations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key compilations of Hanafi legal maxims and evaluate their relative authority in fatwa.

Core Idea
The Hanafi codification of legal maxims evolved from Ibn Nujaym to the Majalla, forming the backbone of Ottoman judicial practice.

Legal maxims (al-qawa'id al-kulliyya) are foundational principles that govern many particular rulings. The Hanafi school led the way in compiling and organizing these maxims. This lesson traces the historical development from an initial set of six basic maxims through the major collections of Ibn Nujaym, al-Khadimi, and the Ottoman Majalla, and evaluates their significance for fatwa today.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Six Basic Maxims
Begin by recognizing that the Hanafi school initially identified five major maxims, with a sixth (no reward except with intention) later added. Note the author's critique that the sixth is actually a subset of the maxim 'matters are judged by their intentions'.
This initial set forms the core around which later compilations expanded.
2
Trace the Expansion by Ibn Nujaym
Ibn Nujaym's work 'al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir' collected and elaborated on numerous subsidiary maxims and practical rulings branching from the basic ones. This work became a major reference for later compilers.
Ibn Nujaym's compilation is the foundation for all later collections.
3
Examine Al-Khadimi's Alphabetical Compilation
Al-Khadimi's 'Majami' al-Haqa'iq' (mid-12th century AH) gathered 154 maxims arranged alphabetically, without commentary. It included directive principles and overlapping categories, but remains a key repository.
Alphabetical arrangement facilitated reference but mixed universal maxims with partial controls.
4
Analyze the Majalla's Selection
The Ottoman Majallat al-Ahkam al-Adliyya (19th century) selected 99 maxims from the works of Ibn Nujaym and al-Khadimi, placing them as introductory articles. This codification became the official authority for Hanafi-fatwa in the Ottoman judiciary.
The Majalla's maxims are the mufta bihi (operative) in fatwa practice.
5
Evaluate Al-Hamzawi's Contribution
Al-Hamzawi's 'al-Fara'id al-Bahiyya' attempted to collect maxims beyond the Majalla, but the author notes it contains mostly partial controls (dawabit) rather than universal maxims. Recognize its limited scope for fatwa.
Not all compilations are equal; the Majalla remains the authoritative source.

ولكن القواعد الكلية بمعناها الذي بيناه كالتي جاءت بها المجلة قليلة جداً فيه وجل ما جاء فيه تحت عناوين القواعد إنما هو ضوابط جزئية أو أحكام أساسية في موضوعات خاصة من أبواب الفقه.
وللمؤلف رسالة أخرى في قواعد أحكام الوقف خاصة.

Chapter (80): A historical glimpse into the formulation of the universal maxims
1 - Basic maxims like pillars in the jurisprudential schools: six maxims: the five already mentioned, plus a sixth maxim: 'No reward except with intention.' 2 - Nineteen other maxims on various topics of lesser scope and comprehensiveness, from which some subsidiary maxims and many rulings branch out. Ibn Nujaym elaborated on the branches of practical rulings that branch from these maxims.
Then in the middle of the twelfth century AH, the Hanafi Turkish jurist Muhammad Abu Sa'id al-Khadimi, author of the gloss on the book 'al-Durar Sharh al-Ghurar' as a matn, composed a work in usul al-fiqh named 'Majami' al-Haqa'iq' and concluded it with a conclusion in which he collected a large group of universal jurisprudential maxims, presenting them in their texts without explanation, and arranged them alphabetically according to the first letter of the first word of each one. They amounted to one hundred fifty-four maxims (2).
It included some maxims of the type of directive principles for the scholars of the school in rationalizing issues, like those in the maxims of al-Karkhi. Some overlap, and the rest are all jurisprudential maxims with general constitutional rulings. Al-Khadimi took most of what Ibn Nujaym collected in al-Ashbah and added to it.
(1) This maxim, in our view, was not worthy of being added to those five mother maxims. Rather, it is a branch of the general maxim 'Matters are judged by their intentions,' and this one suffices for it. Both are derived from the Prophetic hadith: 'Actions are but by intentions, and each person has but that which he intended.' However, the maxim 'Matters are judged by their intentions' is the one that agrees with the text of this hadith in the generality of the ruling, for each of them includes the recompense of otherworldly deeds as well as many results of civil transactions and criminal acts whose worldly judicial rulings differ according to the doer's purpose and intention. As for the maxim 'No reward except with intention,' it is specific only to otherworldly reward. (2) Majami' al-Haqa'iq by al-Khadimi, printed with a commentary called Manafi' al-Haqa'iq at the al-Amira Press in Constantinople in 1308 AH. His maxims also have an independent commentary. Al-Khadimi was followed in this alphabetical arrangement by a book on maxims by the Shafi'i scholar al-Zarkashi, died 794 AH, named al-Manthur fi Tartib al-Qawa'id al-Fiqhiyya.
Then came the Majallat al-Ahkam al-Adliyya, bearing in its preamble a large collection of these maxims, selected from the most important of what Ibn Nujaym and al-Khadimi had collected, with some other maxims added. They amounted to ninety-nine maxims in 99 articles (from Article 2 to Article 100), with which the rulings of the Majalla began, after Article 1 which included the definition of fiqh and the division of its topics.
After the Majalla, the eminent scholar Sayyid al-Shaykh Mahmud al-Hamzawi, Mufti of Damascus during the reign of Sultan Abd al-Hamid, undertook an exhaustive survey of maxims, controls, and principles in most chapters of fiqh beyond the maxims of the Majalla. He collected them in a book named al-Fara'id al-Bahiyya fi al-Qawa'id wa al-Fawa'id al-Fiqhiyya, explained them with some examples, and arranged them by chapters of fiqh. It was the latest and most comprehensive collection under the name of jurisprudential maxims and principles that we know, and it intersperses among the maxims fundamental rulings he calls 'Fawa'id' (benefits). It was printed in Damascus in 1298 AH.
But the universal maxims in the sense we have explained, like those in the Majalla, are very few in it, and most of what it contains under the heading of 'maxims' are only partial controls or fundamental rulings on specific topics from the chapters of fiqh.
The author also has another treatise specifically on the maxims of waqf rulings.
This is the summary of the movement to codify jurisprudential maxims, then their collection in the Hanafi school. It appears from tracing the movement of authorship on maxims that the Shafi'i, then Hanbali, then Maliki jurists followed the Hanafis in this, then it passed to Shia scholars, in this historical order!
The eighth century AH was the most abundant of centuries for such authorship, as can be known from tracing what is recorded in the catalogs of famous libraries of authored works as well. It is noted here that many works known by the name 'al-Ashbah

Commentary
This passage is a historical survey of the codification of legal maxims (qawa'id kulliyya) within the Hanafi school. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, traces the development from an initial set of six basic maxims (the five famous ones plus 'no reward except with intention') to subsequent expansions. He critically notes that the sixth maxim is actually a subset of 'matters are judged by their intentions' (al-umur bi maqasidiha). Ibn Nujaym's work 'al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir' is highlighted as a key early compilation. Al-Khadimi's 'Majami' al-Haqa'iq' (mid-12th century AH) organized 154 maxims alphabetically. The Majalla (19th century) selected 99 maxims as its introductory articles. Later, al-Hamzawi's 'al-Fara'id al-Bahiyya' attempted a broader collection but contained mostly partial controls rather than universal maxims. The passage notes that Hanafis pioneered this field, with Shafi'is, Hanbalis, Malikis, and even Shia scholars following suit, and that the 8th century AH was the peak period for such works.

Key Terms
القواعد الكلية (al-qawa'id al-kulliyya): Universal legal maxims that encompass many particular rulings and serve as principles for deriving subsidiary rules.
مجامع الحقائق (majami' al-haqa'iq): A work by al-Khadimi collecting 154 legal maxims arranged alphabetically, without detailed commentary.
مجلة الأحكام العدلية (majallat al-ahkam al-`adliyya): The Ottoman civil code that began with 99 legal maxims (Articles 2-100) selected from earlier Hanafi collections.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which work arranged 154 legal maxims alphabetically and served as a major source for the Majalla?
A) الأشباه والنظائر لابن نجيم
B) مجامع الحقائق للخادمي
C) مجلة الأحكام العدلية
D) الفرائد البهية للحمزاوي

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school pioneered the codification of legal maxims, culminating in the Majalla's 99 maxims which remain authoritative for fatwa.
